Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Swearing on Current State vs. Cause in Judicial Oaths
Explains the rule and intra-Hanafi dispute on oath modality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on oath modality and apply the mufta bihi ruling to cases with reversible causes.

Core Idea
When a cause can be reversed, swearing on the present state protects the defendant from perjury.

In judicial oaths, the defendant is asked to swear. But should he swear on the past cause (sabab) or the current state (hasil)? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This lesson unpacks the rule from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, presenting the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage states a principle: if the cause is not removable (e.g., a death that cannot be undone), there is consensus to swear on the cause. The dispute arises only when the cause is legally reversible.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They rule that the defendant swears on the current state (al-hasil), e.g., 'By Allah, there is no existing sale now.' Reason: to avoid perjury if the cause has been reversed.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf rules that the defendant swears on the cause (al-sabab), e.g., 'By Allah, I did not sell it,' unless the defendant requests otherwise by saying 'O judge, do not make me swear on the cause, for I may have sold then rescinded.'
4
Analyze the rational argument (talil)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad emphasize harm to the defendant: swearing on a cause that no longer exists is false and would force the defendant into either perjury or admission. Abu Yusuf emphasizes the plaintiff's right: the claim is about the cause, so the oath should match the claim; the defendant can raise a valid concern.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The text states that the chosen view in al-Ikhtiyar is that of Abu Yusuf. Thus, the default is to swear on the cause, but if the defendant requests, the oath shifts to the current state. The practical difference is the timing and truthfulness of the oath.

كتاب الدعوى تعريفها وأحكامها

ويحلف على الحاصل في البيع والنكاح: بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم، أو نكاح قائم في الحال، وفي الطلاق: ما هي بائن منك الآن، وفي الغصب: ما يجب عليك رده. [1] قوله: ويحلف... الخ؛ هذا نوع آخر من كيفية اليمين، وهو الحلف على الحاصل والسبب. والضابط في ذلك: إن السبب إما أن يرتفع برافع أو لا، فإن كان الثاني، فالتحليف على السبب بالإجماع، وإن كان الأول فإن تضرر المدعي بالتحليف على الحاصل فلذلك، وإن لم يتضرر يحلف على الحاصل عند الإمام ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف: يحلف على السبب. كما ستعرف. [2] قوله: على الحاصل؛ أي على صورة إنكار المنكر، وهذا معناه الاصطلاحي، فإن معناه اللغوي: هو ما بقي وثبت وذهب ما سواه. كما في «القاموس»، ويمكن اعتباره هاهنا، فإنه يحلف على الثابت والمستقر الآن. [3] قوله: في الحال؛ متعلق بكلا القولين، وتقدير الكلام: بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم في الحال، أو بالله ما بينكما نكاح قائم في الحال، والأول إذا ادعى أنه اشتراه، والثاني إذا ادعت النفقة، فلو ادعت النكاح كان المثال على مذهب الصاحبين لا على مذهب الإمام، فعنده حينئذ لا يحلف لما مر سابقاً. وقال شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي: وهذا في النكاح على قوله محمول على ما إذا كان مع النكاح دعوى المال، أما إذا تجرد عنها، فإن الإمام لا يرى الحلف فيه، والمذهب قولهما، وهو التحليف، قاله المقدسي. انتهى. [4] قوله: ما هي بائن منك؛ أي بالله ما هي بائن منك، وهذا في البائن الواحد، وأما إذا كان بالثلاث يحلف بالله ما طلقتها ثلاثاً في النكاح الذي بينكما، وفي الرجعي: يحلف بالله ما هي طالق في النكاح الذي بينكما، وهو معنى قوله الآن. [0] قوله: وفي الغصب... الخ؛ وفي الوديعة: بالله ما له هذا الذي ادعاه في يدك وديعة، ولا شيء من الذي في يدك، ولا له قبلك حق، ويحلفه في الدين: بالله ما له عليك من الدين والقرض قليل ولا كثير؛ لاحتمال أنه أدى البعض، أو أبرأه منه فلا يحنث في يمينه على الجميع. هذا ما اختاره في «الاختيار»، وغيره.

لا على السبب: بالله ما بعته ونحوه، مثل: بالله ما نكحتها، وبالله ما طلقتها، وبالله ما غصبت؛ لأن هذه الأسباب ترتفع بأن باع شيئاً ثم تقايلا، فإن حلف على السبب يتضرر المدعى عليه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد. وعند أبي يوسف: يحلف على السبب في جميع ذلك إلا تعريض المدعى عليه بأن يقول: أيها القاضي لا تحلفني على السبب، فإن الإنسان قد يبيع ثم يقيل، أو يطلق ثم يتزوج. [1] قوله: لا على السبب... الخ؛ يعني أنه لا يحلف على السبب، نحو أن يقول في البيع: بالله ما بعته؛ لاحتمال أنه باع ثم أقال، ولا يحلف في النكاح: بالله ما نكحتها؛ لاحتمال أنه نكحها ثم خالعها أو أبانها. ولا يحلف في الطلاق: بالله ما طلقتها؛ لاحتمال أنه طلقها ثم نكحها، ولا يحلف في الغصب: بالله ما غصبته؛ لاحتمال أنه غصب ثم سلم أو ملك بالهبة أو بالبيع، وكذا لا يحلف في الوديعة: بالله ما أودعتك هذا؛ لاحتمال أنه أودعه ثم رده أو هلك في يده بغير صنعه. [2] قوله: لأن هذه الأسباب... الخ؛ يعني أن هذه الأسباب قد تقع ثم ترتفع، فالبيع بالإقالة، والنكاح بالطلاق، والطلاق بالنكاح الجديد، والغصب بالهبة، فلو حلف على السبب لتضرر المدعى عليه؛ لأن حلفه عليه في هذه الوجوه كاذب، وإقرار به، ثم ادعاؤه طرد الرافع لا يسمع. [3] قوله: يحلف على السبب في جميع ذلك؛ لأن اليمين حق المدعي، فيحلف على وفق دعواه، وهي هاهنا السبب، فيحلف إلا إذا قال المدعى عليه عند عرض القاضي اليمين عليه: أيها القاضي لا تحلفني على السبب، فإن الإنسان قد يبيع شيئاً ثم يقيل فيه، فحينئذ يحلف على الحاصل، وصار العدول عن اليمين على مقتضى الدعوى حقاً للمنكر حين طالب به.
The chapter of lawsuit: its definition and rulings. And he swears concerning the current state in sale and marriage: 'By Allah, between you two is no existing sale or existing marriage at present,' and in divorce: 'She is not a final divorcee from you now,' and in usurpation: 'What is upon you to return.' [1] His saying: 'And he swears... etc.'; this is another type of the modality of oath, namely swearing concerning the current state and the cause. The principle in that: the cause either can be removed by a remover or not. If it is the second, then swearing concerning the cause is by consensus. If it is the first, then if the plaintiff is harmed by swearing concerning the current state, then [so]; and if he is not harmed, he swears concerning the current state according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] and Muhammad, and according to Abu Yusuf: he swears concerning the cause, as you will know. [2] His saying: 'concerning the current state'; i.e., concerning the form of the defendant's denial. This is its technical meaning; its linguistic meaning is what remains and is established while what other than it has gone, as in al-Qamus. And it can be considered here, for he swears concerning what is established and settled now. [3] His saying: 'at present'; it relates to both statements, and the estimation of the speech: 'By Allah, between you two is no existing sale at present' or 'By Allah, between you two is no existing marriage at present.' The first is when he claims that he bought it, and the second when she claims maintenance. If she claims marriage, then the example is according to the madhhab of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) not according to the madhhab of the Imam; according to him then he does not swear due to what has passed before. And our teacher, the all-knowing al-Tahtawi said: 'This concerning marriage according to his statement is interpreted as when with the marriage there is a claim for wealth; but if it is free from it, then the Imam does not consider the oath in it, and the madhhab is their statement (the two companions), which is to administer the oath.' So said al-Maqdisi. End. [4] His saying: 'She is not a final divorcee from you'; i.e., 'By Allah, she is not a final divorcee from you.' This is for a single final divorce. As for if it is by three, he swears 'By Allah, I did not divorce her three times in the marriage between you two.' And in a revocable divorce: he swears 'By Allah, she is not divorced in the marriage between you two,' and that is the meaning of his saying 'now'. [0] His saying: 'And concerning usurpation... etc.'; and concerning a deposit: 'By Allah, this which he claimed is not with you as a deposit, nor is there anything of what is in your hand, nor does he have any right against you.' And he swears him concerning a debt: 'By Allah, you owe him nothing from the debt or loan, little or much,' because of the possibility that he paid some or he discharged him from it, so he would not be guilty of perjury in his oath concerning the whole. This is what was chosen in al-Ikhtiyar and others. Not concerning the cause: 'By Allah, I did not sell it' and the like, such as 'By Allah, I did not marry her,' and 'By Allah, I did not divorce her,' and 'By Allah, I did not usurp it'; because these causes are removed by a person's selling something then they mutually rescind, so if he swears concerning the cause, the defendant is harmed. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And according to Abu Yusuf: he swears concerning the cause in all of that, except if the defendant suggests by saying: 'O judge, do not make me swear concerning the cause, for a person may sell then rescind, or divorce then remarry.' [1] His saying: 'Not concerning the cause... etc.'; meaning that he does not swear concerning the cause, such as saying in sale: 'By Allah, I did not sell it' due to the possibility that he sold then rescinded; and he does not swear in marriage: 'By Allah, I did not marry her' due to the possibility that he married her then divorced her (khul') or gave a final divorce; and he does not swear in divorce: 'By Allah, I did not divorce her' due to the possibility that he divorced her then remarried her; and he does not swear in usurpation: 'By Allah, I did not usurp it' due to the possibility that he usurped then surrendered or acquired ownership by gift or sale; and likewise he does not swear concerning a deposit: 'By Allah, I did not deposit this with you' due to the possibility that he deposited it then it was returned or perished in his hand without his action. [2] His saying: 'Because these causes... etc.'; meaning that these causes may occur then be removed: sale by rescission, marriage by divorce, divorce by new marriage, usurpation by gift. So if he swore concerning the cause, the defendant would be harmed because his oath in these cases would be false, and an admission of it, then his claim of repelling the remover would not be heard. [3] His saying: 'He swears concerning the cause in all that'; because the oath is the right of the plaintiff, so he swears in accordance with his claim, which here is the cause, so he swears except if the defendant says when the judge presents the oath to him: 'O judge, do not make me swear concerning the cause, for a person may sell something then rescind it,' then he swears concerning the current state, and the deviation from the oath in accordance with the claim becomes a right of the defendant when he demands it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with the modality of judicial oaths (yamin) in the Hanafi school. It opens by defining the two types: swearing on the current state (al-hasil) and swearing on the cause (al-sabab). The passage is an expansion of the base text, explaining the rule and presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani on one hand, and Abu Yusuf on the other. The principle is: if the cause (e.g., a sale or marriage) once it occurred cannot be reversed, then there is consensus that the oath is on the cause. But if the cause may be reversed by a legal act (e.g., rescission of sale, divorce of marriage, or gift of usurped property), then Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that the defendant swears on the current state (e.g., 'there is no sale now') to avoid him committing perjury. If he swore on the cause, he might inadvertently lie because the cause existed but was later undone. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that the oath should be on the cause in all cases, because the plaintiff's claim is about the cause, except if the defendant requests the judge to avoid the cause oath, citing the possibility of reversal. The passage then gives detailed examples for sale, marriage, divorce, usurpation, deposit, and debt. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Yusuf as chosen in al-Ikhtiyar. The text also clarifies the linguistic and technical meaning of 'al-hasil' and addresses a nuance regarding marriage: if the woman claims marriage without financial claim, Imam Abu Hanifa does not administer the oath at all, but the madhhab of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is to administer it. The commentary follows the author's order: first the general rule and dispute, then explanations of the key terms and examples, then the reasoning behind the positions.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-hāsil): The current state or existing reality; what remains and is established now, as opposed to the past cause.
السبب (al-sabab): The cause or occasion of the claim, such as the act of sale, marriage, divorce, or usurpation.
اليمين (al-yamīn): The oath administered by the judge to the defendant in a lawsuit.
تعريض (ta'rīḍ): A suggestion or indirect request by the defendant to the judge to avoid swearing on the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a case where someone sold an item then later rescinded the sale, and the buyer sues claiming the sale, how does Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule the defendant should swear?
A) Swear on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not sell it.'
B) Swear on the current state: 'By Allah, there is no existing sale between us now.'
C) No oath is required because the sale is rescinded.
D) Swear on the cause only if the plaintiff demands it.

Key Takeaway
When a cause is removable, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad swear on the current state to avoid perjury; Abu Yusuf swears on the cause unless the defendant requests otherwise; the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view.

Qada — Judiciary

Oath on the Cause vs. Oath on the Outcome: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on swearing on the cause versus the outcome and apply the preferred ruling to a given lawsuit scenario.

Core Idea
The oath is on the outcome, not the cause, to protect the defendant from harm if the cause was nullified.

When a defendant is accused of a past act like a sale or divorce, should he swear on that act itself (the cause) or on the current state of affairs (the outcome)? The Hanafi school differs internally, with three distinct positions. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the preferred practice according to later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the views of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. Note the key distinction: swearing on cause vs. outcome.
2
Understand the rationale for each
For each position, explain why swearing on the cause harms the defendant or why it is necessary as the plaintiff's right.
Consider the possibility of subsequent nullification (iqala, remarriage, etc.)
3
Apply the tarjih (preference)
Determine which view is mufta bihi and why. Al-Halwani's view is considered good and used by most judges.
Tarjih is by al-Ayni in al-Dhakhira
4
Map to practice
Given a concrete case (e.g., sale lawsuit), decide which oath to administer based on the defendant's denial.
If defendant denies the cause itself, swear on cause; if he denies the outcome, swear on outcome

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
لا على السبب بالله ما بعثه، ونحوه
لا على السبب باللهِ ما بعتُه ونحوه ، مثل : بالله ما نكحتها ، وبالله ما طلقتها، وبالله ما غصبت ؛ لأنَّ هذه الأسباب ترتفع بأن باع شيئاً، ثم تقايلا ، فإن حلف - على السبب يتضرّر المدعى عليه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة الله ومحمد .
عند
وعند أبي يوسف : يحلف على السبب في جميع ذلك إلا . تعريض المدعى عليه بأن يقول : أيها القاضي لا تُحلّفْنِي على السبب، فإن الإنسان قد يبيع ، ثم يقيل، أو يُطلِّقُ ثُمَّ يتزوج.
عليك من الدين والقرض قليل ولا كثير؛ لاحتمال أنه أدّى البعض، أو أبرأه منه فلا يحنث في يمينه على الجميع. هذا ما اختاره في «الاختيار)، وغيره.
[١] قوله : لا على السبب... الخ ؛ يعني أنه لا يحلف على السبب، نحو أن يقول في البيع : بالله ما بعته ؛ لاحتمال أنه باع ثمّ أَقَال ، ولا يحلف في النكاح : بالله ما نكحتها ؛ لاحتمال أنه نحكها ثم خالعَها أو أبانَها.
ولا يحلف في الطلاق : بالله ما طلقتُها ؛ لاحتمال أنه طلقها ثم نكحها، ولا يحلفُ في الغصب : بالله ما غصبتُه ؛ لاحتمال أنه غصب ثُمَّ سَلَّمَ أو ملك بالهبة أو بالبيع ، وكذا لا يحلف في الوديعة : بالله ما أودعتك هذا ؛ لاحتمال أنه أودعه ثم ردَّه أو هلك في يده بغير صنعه.
[٢] قوله : لأنّ هذه الأسباب.... الخ ؛ يعني أنّ هذه الأسباب قد تقع ثم ترتفع ، فالبيع بالإقالة والنكاح بالطلاق، والطلاق بالنكاح الجديد، والغصب بالهبة، فلو حلف على السبب لتضرر المدعى عليه ؛ لأنّ حلفَهُ عليه في هذه الوجوه كاذب، وإقرار به، ثمَّ ادعاؤه طرد الرافع لا يسمع. [٣] قوله : يحلف على السبب في جميع ذلك ؛ لأنَّ اليمينَ حقٌّ المدعي فيحلفُ على وفق دعواه، وهي هاهنا السبب، فيحلف إلا إذا قال المدعى عليه عند عرض القاضي اليمين عليها : أيها القاضي لا تُحَلِّفني على السبب، فإن الإنسان قد يبيع شيئاً ثم يقيل فيه، فحينئذ يحلف على الحاصل، وصار العدول عن اليمين على مقتضى الدعوى حقاً للمنكر حين طالب به.

وقيل : ينظر إلى إنكار المدعى عليه ، فإن أنكر السبب يَحْلِفُ عليه ، وإن أنكر الحكم يحلف على الحاصل كدعوى الشفعة، هذا ما قالوا.
ولقائل أن يقول : ينبغي أن يحلف على السبب دائماً ، وإِن عَرَضَ المدعى عليه، فلا اعتبار لذلك التعريض ؛ لأنَّ غاية ما في الباب أنَّه وَقَعَ البيع ، ثم وَقَعَ الإقالة، ففي دعوى الإقالة يصيرُ المدعى عليه مدعياً، فعليه البينة على الإقالة ، فإن عجز فعلى المدعي اليمين.
[1] قوله : وقيل ... الخ ؛ قائله شمس الأئمة الحَلْوَانِيُّ ، ويفهـم مـن تقـريـر : إنّه رواية عن أبي يوسف الله ، وذكر العيني نقلا عن «الذخيرة»: وهو حسن ، وعليه عمل أكثر القضاة. انتهى.
وقال فخر الإسلام له : يفوض إلى رأي القاضي. انتهى. ثم اعلم أنّ هذا الخلافُ فيما إذا لم يكن في التحليف على الحاصل ضرر بالمدعي، وفيما إذا كان السبب يرتفع برافع ؛ ولذا قال المصنف الله : فيما سيأتي إلا إذا لَزِمَ ترك النظر للمدَّعي... الخ. [٢]قوله : ولقائل أن يقول ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ المناسب أن يعم الحلـف علـى السبب ويجري في جميع الصور، ولا فائدة في استثناء صورة التعريض ؛ لأنَّ في صورة التعريض إن وقع البيع ثم الإقالة، فالمدعى عليه إذا ادعى الإقالة صار مدعياً، فعليه أن يقيم البينة على الإقالة، فإن أقامها فبها ، وإن عجز عنها فاليمين على المدعي ؛ لأنه صار حينئذ المدعى عليه، وهاهنا كلام نفيس. وهو أنه يحتمل أن تقع الإقالة بلا شهود، والخصم يكون ممن يقدم على اليمين الكاذبة ففـيـه تـوى حق المسلم، وفي صورة الطلاق إن حلف على السبب يتضرر بـه المدعى عليه ؛ لأنه قد يعجز عن إقامة البينة على النكاح، ولا حلف فيه عنده ، فتوى حقه، فتدبر.
Cream of the Ending for the Support of the Care - Not on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not sell it,' and similar. Not on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not sell it,' and similar, like: 'By Allah, I did not marry her,' and 'By Allah, I did not divorce her,' and 'By Allah, I did not usurp it'; because these causes are lifted by the fact that he sold something, then they mutually rescinded. For if he swears on the cause, the defendant would be harmed. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf: he swears on the cause in all that except a hint from the defendant by saying: 'O judge, do not make me swear on the cause, for a man may sell, then rescind, or divorce then remarry.' You owe of debt and loan little or much; because of the possibility that he paid part or released him from it, so he does not break his oath on the whole. This is what he chose in Al-Ikhtiyar and others. [1] His saying: 'not on the cause... etc.' means that he does not swear on the cause, such as saying in sale: 'By Allah, I did not sell it' because of the possibility that he sold then rescinded, and he does not swear in marriage: 'By Allah, I did not marry her' because of the possibility that he married her then divorced or separated. And he does not swear in divorce: 'By Allah, I did not divorce her' because of the possibility that he divorced her then married her, and he does not swear in usurpation: 'By Allah, I did not usurp it' because of the possibility that he usurped then delivered or owned by gift or sale, and similarly he does not swear in deposit: 'By Allah, I did not deposit this with you' because of the possibility that he deposited then returned it or it perished in his hand without his act. [2] His saying: 'because these causes... etc.' means that these causes may occur then be lifted, so sale by rescission, marriage by divorce, divorce by new marriage, usurpation by gift. If he swore on the cause, the defendant would be harmed because his oath in these cases is a lie and an acknowledgment, then his claim of removing the cause is not heard. [3] His saying: 'he swears on the cause in all that' because the oath is the right of the plaintiff, so he swears according to his claim, which is here the cause. So he swears unless the defendant says when the judge presents the oath to him: 'O judge, do not make me swear on the cause, for a man may sell something then rescind it.' Then he swears on the outcome, and the deviation from the oath according to the claim becomes a right for the denier when he demands it. Book of Lawsuit: Its Definition and Rulings (1)113 And it was said: Look at the defendant's denial. If he denies the cause, he swears on it; if he denies the ruling, he swears on the outcome, like the claim of preemption. This is what they said. And one may say: He should swear on the cause always, even if the defendant hints, so that hint is not considered because the most in this matter is that the sale occurred then rescission occurred. In the claim of rescission, the defendant becomes a claimant, so he must provide evidence of rescission; if he fails, then the oath is on the plaintiff. [1] His saying: 'and it was said... etc.' Its speaker is Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and it is understood from the report that it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, and al-Ayni mentioned quoting from al-Dhakhira: it is good, and upon it is the practice of most judges. End. And Fakhra al-Islam said: it is delegated to the judge's opinion. End. Then know that this disagreement is when there is no harm to the plaintiff in making him swear on the outcome, and when the cause can be lifted by a remover. Hence the author said later: 'except if abandoning consideration for the plaintiff becomes necessary... etc.' [2] His saying: 'and one may say... etc.' Its summary: It is appropriate to generalize swearing on the cause and apply it in all cases, and there is no benefit in excepting the case of hint because in the case of hint if a sale occurred then rescission, then the defendant if he claims rescission becomes a claimant, so he must provide evidence of rescission; if he provides it, then good; if he fails, then the oath is on the plaintiff because then he becomes the defendant. And here is a refined discussion: It is possible that rescission occurs without witnesses, and the opponent is one who would proceed with false oath, so there is loss of a Muslim's right. And in the case of divorce, if he swears on the cause, the defendant is harmed because he may be unable to provide evidence of marriage, and there is no oath for him according to him, so his right is lost. So ponder.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi dispute on whether a defendant in a lawsuit should be made to swear an oath on the cause (sabab) of the claim or on the current state (hasil). The cause is the original act that gives rise to the claim, such as a sale, marriage, divorce, or usurpation. The current state is the outcome after potential subsequent events like rescission (iqala) or remarriage. The passage begins by stating the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the defendant should not swear on the cause because it may have been undone. For example, if someone is sued for a sale, he should not swear 'By Allah, I did not sell it' because he might have sold and then rescinded. Similarly for marriage, divorce, and usurpation. The reason is that swearing on the cause would harm the defendant, as his oath would be technically false (since the sale did occur) and he would be confessing to the cause, but his subsequent claim of rescission would not be heard. Then Abu Yusuf's position is presented: he swears on the cause in all these cases, except if the defendant hints that the cause may have been nullified (e.g., by saying 'O judge, do not make me swear on the cause, for a man may sell then rescind'). In that case, the oath is on the outcome. This is because the oath is the plaintiff's right, so it should be on the cause as claimed, but the defendant's hint shifts it to the outcome. Then a later opinion (qil) by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani is reported: the judge should look at what the defendant denies. If he denies the cause itself (e.g., that a sale ever occurred), he swears on the cause; if he denies the ruling (e.g., that he now owes something), he swears on the outcome. This is applied to the claim of preemption. Then a counter-argument is presented by an unnamed objector (likely a later scholar): the defendant should always swear on the cause, and the hint is irrelevant. If the sale occurred and then rescission, the defendant claiming rescission becomes a plaintiff and must prove it. If he cannot, the oath returns to the original plaintiff. The passage ends with a note that this dispute applies when there is no harm to the plaintiff and when the cause is reversible. The authoritative weight of these positions: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf are from the Zahir al-Riwayah. Al-Halwani's opinion is considered good and followed by most judges, but Fakhra al-Islam delegates it to the judge's discretion. The counter-argument is a fine point that warns against the loss of a Muslim's right if false oaths are made. In practice, the mufta bihi likely follows Abu Yusuf's view with the gloss of Halwani, as it balances the rights of both parties.

Key Terms
السبب (as-sabab): The original act or event that is the subject of the lawsuit, e.g., sale, marriage, divorce, usurpation.
الحاصل (al-haasil): The current state of affairs after potential subsequent events like rescission, remarriage, or restoration.
الإقالة (al-iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale, which undoes the original contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this passage, how does a judge determine the subject of the defendant's oath?
A) He always makes the defendant swear on the cause.
B) He looks at what the defendant denies: if he denies the cause, swear on cause; if he denies the outcome, swear on outcome.
C) He asks the plaintiff which oath he prefers.
D) He makes the defendant swear on the outcome because the cause might have been nullified.

Key Takeaway
The oath in a lawsuit should be tailored to what the defendant actually denies – the cause or the outcome – to prevent injustice and perjury.

Oath in Lawsuits: Cause vs. Result
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath content in lawsuits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rule for the defendant's oath (on the cause or result) and identify the exception when swearing on the result would harm the plaintiff.

Core Idea
The oath is on the result, unless it harms the plaintiff—then it shifts to the cause.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, the content of the defendant's oath can determine the outcome of a lawsuit. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah examines a dispute: should the defendant swear on the underlying cause (sabab) or on the legal result (hāsil)? The default rule is to swear on the result, but a crucial exception protects the plaintiff's right when the opponent's oath could be truthful according to a different madhhab. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the intra-Hanafi difference, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions
The passage presents two views: some say the oath is on the result (default), others say always on the cause. Identify who holds each.
The default view is attributed to Abu Hanifa; the other to Abu Yusuf and al-Halwani.
2
Understand the general rule
The text explains that when the cause can be overridden (e.g., sale rescinded), the oath is on the result.
This is based on the idea that the result is the actual legal effect the plaintiff claims.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues for universal oath on the cause, making indirect opposition (ta'rīḍ) a shift in burden of proof.
The objector says if defendant claims rescission, he becomes plaintiff and must prove it.
4
Find the exception
The exception is when swearing on the result harms the plaintiff. Identify the two examples: pre-emption by neighborhood and irrevocably divorced wife's maintenance.
In both cases, the opponent's madhhab does not recognize the right, so a truthful oath on the result would defeat the claim.
5
Apply the exception
For these cases, the defendant must swear on the cause to prevent a false oath based on another madhhab.
The cause (e.g., buying the house, divorcing) cannot be denied by madhhab difference.

Source Text

ولقائل أن يقول : ينبغي أن يحلف على السبب دائماً ، وإِن عَرَضَ المدعى عليه، فلا اعتبار لذلك التعريض ؛ لأنَّ غاية ما في الباب أنَّه وَقَعَ البيع ، ثم وَقَعَ الإقالة، ففي دعوى الإقالة يصيرُ المدعى عليه مدعياً، فعليه البينة على الإقالة ، فإن عجز فعلى المدعي اليمين.
[1] قوله : وقيل ... الخ ؛ قائله شمس الأئمة الحَلْوَانِيُّ ، ويفهم من تقـريـر : إنّه رواية عن أبي يوسف الله ، وذكر العيني نقلا عن «الذخيرة»: وهو حسن، وعليه عمل أكثر القضاة. انتهى.
وقال فخر الإسلام له : يفوض إلى رأي القاضي. انتهى. ثم اعلم أنّ هذا الخلافُ فيما إذا لم يكن في التحليف على الحاصل ضرر بالمدعي، وفيما إذا كان السبب يرتفع برافع ؛ ولذا قال المصنف الله : فيما سيأتي إلا إذا لَزِمَ ترك النظر للمدَّعي... الخ.
[٢]قوله : ولقائل أن يقول ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ المناسب أن يعم الحلـف علـى السبب ويجري في جميع الصور، ولا فائدة في استثناء صورة التعريض ؛ لأنَّ في صورة التعريض إن وقع البيع ثم الإقالة، فالمدعى عليه إذا ادعى الإقالة صار مدعياً، فعليه أن يقيم البينة على الإقالة، فإن أقامها فبها ، وإن عجز عنها فاليمين على المدعي ؛ لأنه صار حينئذ المدعى عليه، وهاهنا كلام نفيس. وهو أنه يحتمل أن تقع الإقالة بلا شهود، والخصم يكون ممن يقدم على اليمين الكاذبة ففـيـه تـوى حق المسلم، وفي صورة الطلاق إن حلف على السبب يتضرر بـه المدعى عليه ؛ لأنه قد يعجز عن إقامة البينة على النكاح، ولا حلف فيه عنده ، فتوى حقه، فتدبر.
إلا إذا لزم ترك النظر للمدعي ، فيحلفُ على السبب كدعوى شفعة بالجوار، ونفقة المبتوتة، والخصم لا يراهما) أي يحلف على الحاصل" إلا أن يلزم من الحلف على الحاصل تركَ النَّظرِ للمدعي ، فحينئذ يحلف على السبب كدعوى الشفعة بالجوار، فإنَّه يمكن أن يحلف على الحاصل أن لا يجب الشفعة بناءً على مذهب الشافعي له ، فإنَّ الشَّفعة لا تثبت بالجوار عنده ، فيحلف المشتري : بالله ما اشتريت هذه الدار، وكذا إذا ادعت المرأة النفقة بالطلاق البائن كالخلع مثلاً ، فإنَّه لا يَجِبُ النَّفقةُ عند الشَّافِعِي . ويجب عندنا، فإن حلف بالله ما يجب عليك النفقة، فربما يحلفُ على مذهب الشَّافِعِي ، فيحلف على السبب بالله ما طلقتها طلاقاً بائناً. [١] قوله : إلا إذا لزم... الخ ؛ استثناء من قوله : ويحلف على الحاصل ... الخ ؛ فالحاصل أنه لا يحلف على الحاصل إذا لزم ترك النظر للمدعي، بل يحلف حينئذ على السبب.
[٢] قوله : أي يحلف على الحاصل ... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الحاصل هو الأصل عند الطرفين إذا كان سبباً يرتفع برافع كالبيع ، يقال فيه : والغصب يفسخ بالهبة، والنكاحُ يفسخ بالخلع والطلاق، يتجدد فيه بعد الإبانة، فيحلفُ على الحاصل عندهما، إلا إذا لزم من الحلف على الحاصل تركُ النَّظرِ للمدعي. فحينئذ يحلف على السبب بالإجماع رعاية لجانبه، مثل أن يدعي شفعة بالجوار، أو تدعي مبتوتة نفقة العدة، والخصم لا يراهما بناءً على مذهب الشافعي له، فإنَّ الشفعة بالجوار لا تثبت عنده. وكذا لا تجب نفقه المبتوتة عنده، فالخصم يحلفُ على السبب : بالله لا اشتريت هذه الدار، وما هي معتدة منك ، إذ لو حلف على الحاصل : بالله لا تجب الشفعة عليك، وبالله لا تجب عليك النفقة، يصدَّقُ في يمينه في اعتقاده، فيفوتُ النظر في حق المدعي.
It is said: Consideration is given to the denial of the defendant: if he denies the cause, he swears on it, and if he denies the ruling, he swears on the result, like a pre-emption claim. This is what they said.
And someone may say: He should always swear on the cause. And if the defendant makes an indirect opposition, that indirect opposition is not considered, because the utmost in the matter is that a sale occurred, then a rescission occurred. In the claim of rescission, the defendant becomes the claimant, so he must provide evidence on the rescission. If he fails, then the plaintiff must take the oath.
[1] His statement: "It is said...etc." Its proponent is Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and it is understood from the wording that it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. Al-'Ayni mentioned, quoting from al-Dhakhira: It is good, and most judges act upon it. End.
And Fakhr al-Islam said: It is delegated to the judge's opinion. End. Then know that this difference is in cases where there is no harm to the plaintiff in swearing on the result, and where the cause can be overridden by a countervailing factor. Hence the author said later: "Except if it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff...etc."
[2] His statement: "And someone may say...etc." Its summary: The appropriate approach is to apply swearing on the cause universally, and there is no benefit in excepting the case of indirect opposition, because in the case of indirect opposition, if a sale occurred then a rescission, the defendant, when he claims the rescission, becomes the claimant, so he must produce evidence of the rescission. If he produces it, fine; if he fails, then the oath is on the plaintiff, because he then becomes the defendant. And here there is a subtle point: It is possible that the rescission occurred without witnesses, and the opponent may be one who would take a false oath, resulting in loss of a Muslim's right. And in the case of divorce, if he swears on the cause, it harms the defendant, because he may be unable to produce evidence of marriage, and there is no oath for him in his view, so his right is lost. So reflect.
Except if it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff, then he swears on the cause, like a claim of pre-emption by neighborhood, and maintenance for an irrevocably divorced woman, with the opponent not recognizing them. That is, he swears on the result, unless swearing on the result necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff; then at that time he swears on the cause, like a claim of pre-emption by neighborhood. For it is possible that the opponent could swear on the result that pre-emption is not obligatory based on al-Shafi'i's school, because pre-emption by neighborhood is not established according to him. So the buyer swears: By Allah, I did not buy this house. Similarly, if a woman claims maintenance due to irrevocable divorce like khul', etc., then maintenance is not obligatory according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, but is obligatory according to us. So if he swears, "By Allah, maintenance is not obligatory on you," he might swear according to al-Shafi'i's school. Therefore he swears on the cause: By Allah, I did not divorce her irrevocably.
[1] His statement: "Except if it necessitates...etc." This is an exception to his statement: "He swears on the result...etc." So the conclusion is that he does not swear on the result if it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff; rather, he then swears on the cause.
[2] His statement: "That is, he swears on the result...etc." It means that the result is the original rule between the two parties when there is a cause that can be overridden by a countervailing factor, like sale (it is said: usurpation is annulled by gift), and marriage is annulled by khul' and divorce; it renews after separation. So he swears on the result according to both, except if swearing on the result necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff. Then he swears on the cause by consensus, out of regard for his side, like if someone claims pre-emption by neighborhood, or an irrevocably divorced woman claims maintenance during the waiting period, and the opponent does not recognize them based on al-Shafi'i's school. For pre-emption by neighborhood is not established according to him, and similarly, maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman is not obligatory according to him. So the opponent swears on the cause: By Allah, I did not buy this house, and she is not undergoing waiting period from you. Because if he swore on the result: By Allah, pre-emption is not obligatory on you, and By Allah, maintenance is not obligatory on you, he would be truthful in his oath according to his belief, thus consideration for the plaintiff is lost.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses a key procedural issue in Hanafi law: upon what should the defendant take an oath in a lawsuit? The text presents a dispute. The initial view (attributed to some Hanafi scholars) states that the oath depends on what the defendant denies: if he denies the underlying cause (e.g., the sale itself), he swears on the cause; if he denies the legal result (e.g., the right of pre-emption), he swears on the result. This is the view often attributed to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and is reported as a narration from Abu Yusuf. Most judges practice it, though Fakhr al-Islam says it is left to the judge's discretion. However, the author objects to this distinction, arguing that the oath should always be on the cause. He reasons that if the defendant makes an indirect opposition (e.g., claims the sale was rescinded), he effectively becomes the claimant and must provide evidence. If he fails, the plaintiff takes the oath. Swearing on the cause universally avoids complexities. But then the text introduces a crucial exception: if swearing on the result would harm the plaintiff by causing his right to be lost, then the defendant must swear on the cause. This exception is illustrated with two examples: pre-emption by neighborhood (shuf'ah bi al-jiwar) and maintenance for an irrevocably divorced woman (nafaqat al-mabtutah). In both cases, the opposing party (buyer or ex-husband) might honestly swear on the result according to Imam al-Shafi'i's school, which does not recognize these rights, thereby defeating the plaintiff's claim. To protect the plaintiff, the defendant is required to swear on the cause (e.g., 'I did not buy this house' or 'I did not divorce her'), which cannot be avoided by differing madhhab positions. The commentary explains that the default rule is swearing on the result when the cause can be overridden (e.g., sale rescinded), but the exception is applied by consensus to safeguard the plaintiff's right. The passage thus refines the general rule with a practical exception based on istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): The underlying transaction or event that gives rise to the legal claim, e.g., sale in pre-emption, marriage in maintenance.
حاصل (hāsil): The legal result or effect derived from the cause, e.g., the right of pre-emption or obligation of maintenance.
شفعة بالجوار (shuf'ah bi al-jiwār): Pre-emption right based on neighborly proximity, recognized by Hanafis but not by Shafi'is.
نفقة المبتوتة (nafaqat al-mabtūtah): Maintenance for a woman who has been irrevocably divorced (bā'in) during her waiting period.
تعريض (ta'rīḍ): Indirect opposition by the defendant, e.g., claiming the cause has been nullified (like rescission of sale).

Key Terms from Source Passage

In which scenario does the defendant swear on the cause instead of the result?
A) When the defendant denies the cause
B) When swearing on the result would harm the plaintiff's right
C) When the cause cannot be overridden
D) In all cases by consensus

Key Takeaway
The default oath is on the legal result, but the defendant must swear on the cause when swearing on the result would cause the plaintiff's right to be lost due to a differing madhhab.

Swearing on the Cause: Protecting the Plaintiff's Right in Hanafi Court
Exception to oath on result when it harms plaintiff.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that triggers a shift from oath on the result to oath on the cause, and apply this rule to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Swearing on the result harms the plaintiff when the defendant's madhhab differs – the oath shifts to the cause to ensure justice.

This lesson examines an important procedural rule in Hanafi qada. Normally, a defendant swears on the legal consequence (hasil). But when the defendant could truthfully swear according to his own madhhab and thus defeat the plaintiff's claim, the law intervenes. The oath is then administered on the underlying fact (sabab). We will explore the rationale, examples, and internal Hanafi discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the default: in Hanafi courts, the defendant swears on the hasil (result), e.g., 'By Allah, pre-emption is not wajib' or 'By Allah, maintenance is not due.' This is the standard procedure from the zahir al-riwayah.
The hasil is the legal ruling that would follow from the cause.
2
Recognize the exception
The exception applies when swearing on the hasil would necessitiate abandoning consideration for the plaintiff (luzum tark al-nazar lil-mudda'i). This occurs when the defendant belongs to a madhhab that does not recognize the substantive right claimed by the plaintiff, allowing the defendant to swear truthfully according to his own belief and thereby defeat the claim.
The key is the defendant's ability to be truthful in his oath while harming the plaintiff.
3
Understand the cause (sabab)
When the exception applies, the oath is shifted to the sabab (cause), i.e., the underlying fact that would give rise to the claimed right. For example, instead of swearing on the result 'pre-emption is not wajib,' the defendant swears 'I did not buy this house.' Similarly, for maintenance, the defendant swears 'I did not divorce her irrevocably.'
The sabab is an objective fact that can be verified, unlike the hasil which depends on madhhab interpretation.
4
Examine the examples provided
The passage gives two key examples: (1) shuf'a bil-jiwar (pre-emption by neighborship) – not recognized by Shafi'i, so if defendant is Shafi'i, he swears on cause: 'By Allah, I did not buy the house.' (2) nafaqah al-mubtatta (maintenance for irrevocably divorced woman) – not obligatory in Shafi'i, so defendant swears on cause: 'By Allah, I did not divorce her irrevocably.'
In both cases, swearing on the hasil would allow a truthful oath that harms the plaintiff.
5
Consider the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage references a related ikhtilaf: whether the judge should ask the plaintiff if he believes in the obligation (e.g., shuf'a bil-jiwar) before administering the oath on the cause. One view says no (the judge's madhhab governs); another says yes, which is preferred (from Sharh al-Sadr al-Shahid). This affects how the exception is applied in practice.
The preferred view protects the plaintiff by ensuring he genuinely holds the belief that triggers the exception.

Source Text

إلا إذا لزم ترك النظر للمدعي، فيحلف على السبب كدعوى شفعة بالجوار، ونفقة المبتوتة، والخصم لا يراهما (1) إلا إذا لزم ترك النظر للمدعي، فيحلف على السبب كدعوى شفعة بالجوار، ونفقة المبتوتة والخصم لا يراهما) أي يحلف على الحاصل إلا أن يلزم من الحلف على الحاصل ترك النظر للمدعي، فحينئذ يحلف على السبب كدعوى الشفعة بالجوار، فإنه يمكن أن يحلف على الحاصل أن لا يجب الشفعة بناءً على مذهب الشافعي له، فإن الشفعة لا تثبت بالجوار عنده، فيحلف المشتري: بالله ما اشتريت هذه الدار، وكذا إذا ادعت المرأة النفقة بالطلاق البائن كالخلع مثلاً، فإنه لا تجب النفقة عند الشافعي . ويجب عندنا، فإن حلف بالله ما يجب عليك النفقة، فربما يحلف على مذهب الشافعي، فيحلف على السبب بالله ما طلقتها طلاقاً بائناً. [1] قوله: إلا إذا لزم... الخ؛ استثناء من قوله: ويحلف على الحاصل... الخ؛ فالحاصل أنه لا يحلف على الحاصل إذا لزم ترك النظر للمدعي، بل يحلف حينئذ على السبب. [2] قوله: أي يحلف على الحاصل... الخ؛ يعني إن الحاصل هو الأصل عند الطرفين إذا كان سبباً يرتفع برافع كالبيع، يقال فيه: والغصب يفسخ بالهبة، والنكاح يفسخ بالخلع والطلاق، يتجدد فيه بعد الإبانة، فيحلف على الحاصل عندهما، إلا إذا لزم من الحلف على الحاصل ترك النظر للمدعي. فحينئذ يحلف على السبب بالإجماع رعاية لجانبه، مثل أن يدعي شفعة بالجوار، أو تدعي مبتوتة نفقة العدة، والخصم لا يراهما بناءً على مذهب الشافعي له، فإن الشفعة بالجوار لا تثبت عنده. وكذا لا تجب نفقه المبتوتة عنده، فالخصم يحلف على السبب: بالله لا اشتريت هذه الدار، وما هي معتدة منك، إذ لو حلف على الحاصل: بالله لا تجب الشفعة عليك، وبالله لا تجب عليك النفقة، يصدق في يمينه في اعتقاده، فيفوت النظر في حق المدعي. (1) ينظر: «التنبيه»(ص80)، وغيره. (2) ينظر: «التنبيه»(ص129)، وغيره.
وكذا في سبب لا يرتفع كعبد مسلم يدعي عتقه، فإن المولى يحلف بالله ما أعتقته، فإنه لا ضرورة إلى الحلف على الحاصل؛ لأن السبب لا يمكن ارتفاعه، فإن العبد المسلم إذا أعتق لا يسترق
Except when it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff, then he swears on the cause, such as a claim of pre-emption by neighborship, and maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman, and the opponent does not see them. (1) Except when it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff, then he swears on the cause, such as a claim of pre-emption by neighborship, and maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman, and the opponent does not see them.) Meaning: He swears on the result unless swearing on the result necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff; then at that time he swears on the cause, such as a claim of pre-emption by neighborship, for it is possible that he could swear on the result that pre-emption is not obligatory based on al-Shafi'i's madhhab for him, for pre-emption is not established by neighborship according to him, so the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy this house.' Similarly, if a woman claims maintenance due to an irrevocable divorce like khul' for example, for maintenance is not obligatory according to al-Shafi'i, and it is obligatory according to us. If he swears: 'By Allah, maintenance is not obligatory on you,' perhaps he swears according to al-Shafi'i's madhhab, so he swears on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not divorce her with an irrevocable divorce.' [1] His statement: 'Except when it necessitates...' etc.; an exception from his statement: 'He swears on the result...' etc. The outcome is that he does not swear on the result when it necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff, but rather swears at that time on the cause. [2] His statement: 'Meaning: He swears on the result...' etc.; meaning that the result is the original principle for both parties when the cause can be removed by a remover, like sale, it is said therein: and usurpation is annulled by gift, and marriage is annulled by khul' and divorce, it is renewed after separation, so he swears on the result according to them, except when swearing on the result necessitates abandoning consideration for the plaintiff. Then at that time he swears on the cause by consensus, out of regard for his side, such as if someone claims pre-emption by neighborship, or an irrevocably divorced woman claims maintenance during the waiting period, and the opponent does not see them based on al-Shafi'i's madhhab for him, for pre-emption by neighborship is not established according to him. Similarly, maintenance of the irrevocably divorced woman is not obligatory according to him, so the opponent swears on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not buy this house, and she is not in waiting period from you,' because if he swore on the result: 'By Allah, pre-emption is not obligatory on you,' and 'By Allah, maintenance is not obligatory on you,' he would be truthful in his oath according to his belief, and consideration for the plaintiff would be lost. (1) See: 'al-Tanbih' (p. 80) and others. (2) See: 'al-Tanbih' (p. 129) and others.
And similarly in a cause that cannot be removed, like a Muslim slave claiming his manumission, for the master swears: 'By Allah, I did not manumit him,' for there is no need to swear on the result, because the cause cannot be removed, for if the Muslim slave is manumitted, he cannot be enslaved.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains an important exception in judicial oath-taking procedure. Normally, a defendant swears on the result (hasil) of an action — for example, 'By Allah, the pre-emption is not obligatory.' However, if swearing on the result would allow the defendant to be truthful according to his own legal school (madhhab) while harming the plaintiff's case, then the judge must administer the oath on the cause (sabab) instead. For instance, in a Hanafi court, if a plaintiff claims pre-emption by neighborship (shuf'a bil-jiwar) but the defendant follows the Shafi'i school which does not recognize this right, swearing on the result ('pre-emption is not wajib') would be true for the defendant, unjustly harming the plaintiff. Therefore, the defendant swears on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not buy this house.' The same applies to an irrevocably divorced woman (mubtatta) claiming maintenance during her waiting period; the defendant swears on the divorce itself. The passage also considers a case where the cause cannot be reversed, like a Muslim slave claiming he was freed: the master swears on the cause (the act of freeing) because the result cannot be changed — a freed slave cannot be re-enslaved. The commentary emphasizes that this exception is a matter of consensus (ijma) among Hanafi scholars, designed to protect the plaintiff's right. The examples illustrate how procedural fairness requires considering the defendant's potential to swear truthfully according to his belief.

Key Terms
حلف على السبب (half 'ala al-sabab): Taking an oath on the cause of the claim, e.g., 'I did not buy this house' or 'I did not divorce her'.
حلف على الحاصل (half 'ala al-hasil): Taking an oath on the resulting legal consequence, e.g., 'Pre-emption is not obligatory' or 'Maintenance is not due'.
شفعة بالجوار (shuf'a bil-jiwar): The right of pre-emption based on being a neighbor to the property sold.
مبتوتة (mubtatta): A woman divorced with an irrevocable (bā'in) divorce, who is entitled to maintenance during her waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi court, if a defendant follows the Shafi'i school and the plaintiff claims shuf'a by jiwār, what should the defendant swear on?
A) On the result: 'By Allah, pre-emption is not wajib'
B) On the cause: 'By Allah, I did not buy this house'
C) The judge decides based on his own madhhab
D) No oath is required

Key Takeaway
When swearing on the result would harm the plaintiff due to the defendant's differing madhhab, the oath is shifted to the cause by consensus.

Oath When Cause Cannot Be Reversed: Inter-Madhhab Dispute on Considering the Claimant's School
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute about oath form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires an oath on the cause when the cause is irreversible, and state the three opinions on whether the claimant's school is considered in formulating the oath.

Core Idea
When a cause is irreversible, the oath must be on the cause; the judge should tailor the oath to the claimant's belief to avoid injustice.

In judicial oaths, the key distinction is between swearing on the cause (the act) and swearing on the state (the current condition). This passage clarifies that when the cause is irreversible—like the emancipation of a Muslim slave—the oath must be on the cause. It then enters a classic inter-madhhab dispute: should the judge consider the school of the claimant when formulating the oath? The preferred Hanafi view, supported by a practical story from Qadi Abu 'Asim, is that the judge should ask the claimant if he believes the right is obligatory and then tailor the oath accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of an irreversible cause
The passage gives the example of a Muslim slave claiming emancipation. Once emancipated, a Muslim cannot be re-enslaved (apostasy leads to execution, flight is rare). Thus, the cause (emancipation) cannot be undone.
2
Understand the ruling: oath on the cause, not on the state
The master must swear 'I did not emancipate him' (on the cause) rather than 'He is not free' (on the state). The reasoning: since the cause cannot be reversed, there is no need to swear on the state; the critical point is the act itself.
This is the Hanafi position.
3
Trace the dispute about considering the claimant's school
The text presents three opinions: (1) only the judge's school matters; (2) not even that; (3) the judge asks the claimant if he believes the right is obligatory. The preferred view (as in Sharh al-Sadr al-Shahid) is the third.
The defendant's school is never considered.
4
Analyze the story of Qadi Abu 'Asim
A woman claimed 'idda maintenance; the judge inquired about the husband's school to formulate the oath appropriately (Shafi'i vs Hanafi). This demonstrates the practical application of considering the claimant's belief.

Source Text

وكذا في سبب لا يرتفعُ كعبد مسلم يدعي عتقه، فإنَّ المولى يحلف بالله ما أعتقتُه ، فإنه لا ضرورة إلى الحلف على الحاصل ؛ لأنَّ السَّبب لا يمكن ارتفاعه ، فإن العبد المسلم إذا أعتق لا يُسترق

فإن قلت : إنَّ المدعى عليه يتضرَّرُ ببطلان الشفعة بتأخير الطلب.
قلت : لا بد للقاضي من الإضرار بأحدهما ، والأولى بالضرر المدعى عليه ؛ لأنه متمسك بـ بعارض السقوط، والمدعي بالأصل حيث أثبت حقه بالسبب الموجب من الشراء، فيجب التمسك بالأصل حتى يقوم الدليل على العارض، وقد استفيد من هاهنا أنه لا اعتبار بمذهب المدعى عليه. وأما مذهب المدعي ففيه اختلاف :
فقيل : إنه لا اعتبار به أيضاً، وإنّما الاعتبار لمذهب القاضي، فلو ادعى شافعي شفعة الجوار عند حنفي سمعها.

وقيل : يسأله القاضي : هل يعتقد وجوبها أو لا، وفي «شرح الصدر الشهيد»: إن الأخير أوجه الأقاويل وأحسنُها . ذكره في «البحر» (١) نقلاً . عن «خزانة المفتيين». وذكر فيه (٢) : إنه ذكر الصدر حكاية عن القاضي أبي عاصم : إنه كان يدرس، وخليفته يحكم ، فاتفق أن امرأة ادعت على زوجها نفقة العدة، فأنكر الزوج فطلب منه أن يحلف : ما عليك تسليم النفقة من الوجه الذي تدعي ، فلما تهيأ الرجلُ نظرتِ المرأةُ إليه. فعَلِمَ لماذا نظرت إليه فنادى خليفته، سل الرجل : من أي محلة هو، فإن كان من أصحاب الحديث : حلّفه بالله، ما هي معتدة منك ؛ لأنَّ الشافعي لا يرى النفقة للمبتوتة، وإن كان من أصحابنا : حلّفه بالله عليك تسليم النفقة إليها من الوجه الذي تدعي نظراً لها. انتهى.

[1] قوله : وكذا في سبب... الخ؛ يعني وكذا يحلفُ على السبب، إن كان السبب لا يرتفع برافع، كما أنَّ العبد المسلمَ ادَّعى عتقه الواقع في إسلامه على مولاه، وهو : ينكره ، فالمولى يحلف على السبب بالله ما أعتقه ولا ضرورة حينئذ إلى الحلف على الحاصل ؛ لأن السبب لا يرتفعُ فإنّه لا يتصوّر عوده إلى الرق؛ لأنه إذا ارتد يقتل والهرب إلى دار الحرب نادر.
Book of Claims: Its Definition and Rulings

And likewise, regarding a cause that cannot be reversed, such as a Muslim slave claiming his emancipation: the master swears by Allah 'I did not emancipate him.' There is no need to swear upon the state (al-hasil) because the cause cannot be reversed. For if a Muslim slave is emancipated, he cannot be enslaved again.

If it is said: The defendant suffers harm due to the nullification of preemption (shuf'a) by delaying the demand.
I say: The judge must harm one of the two parties, and it is better to harm the defendant because he is holding onto a cause of nullification (al-'arid), while the claimant is upon the original (asl) since he established his right by the cause requiring purchase. So the original must be held until evidence for the contingent cause is established. And from this it is learned that the school (madhhab) of the defendant is not considered. As for the school of the claimant, there is a difference of opinion:
- Some say: It is also not considered; rather, only the school of the judge is considered. So if a Shafi'i claims preemption of neighbor (shuf'at al-jiwar) before a Hanafi judge, it is heard.
- Some say: No.
- Some say: The judge asks him: Do you believe it is obligatory or not? And in Sharh al-Sadr al-Shahid: The last is the most likely and best of the opinions. Mentioned in al-Bahr, quoting from Khizanat al-Muftiyin. And in it he mentioned: al-Sadr narrated from al-Qadi Abu 'Asim that he used to teach and his deputy judged. It happened that a woman sued her husband for the maintenance of the waiting period ('idda). The husband denied, so he was asked to swear: 'You are not obliged to pay maintenance from the aspect she claims.' When the man prepared, the woman looked at him. He (the deputy) knew why she looked at him, so he called his deputy: Ask the man from which area he is from. If he is from the people of hadith: Swear him by Allah 'She is not in 'idda from you' because al-Shafi'i does not see maintenance for the irrevocably divorced woman (al-mubattata). If he is from our companions: Swear him by Allah 'You must pay maintenance to her from the aspect she claims' out of consideration for her. End.

[1] His statement: 'And likewise regarding a cause...' etc.; meaning he swears upon the cause if the cause cannot be reversed by something that removes it, just as the Muslim slave claimed his emancipation that occurred during his Islam against his master, and he denies it. So the master swears upon the cause: by Allah I did not emancipate him, and there is no need then to swear upon the state (al-hasil) because the cause cannot be reversed. For it is inconceivable that he returns to slavery, because if he apostatizes he is killed, and fleeing to dar al-harb is rare.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the form of oath when a cause cannot be reversed. The primary ruling is: if a Muslim slave claims his master emancipated him and the master denies, the master swears upon the cause (i.e., 'I did not emancipate him') rather than on the state (i.e., 'he is not free now'). This is because emancipation of a Muslim slave cannot be reversed—once free, he cannot be re-enslaved, as apostasy leads to execution and flight to enemy territory is rare. The passage then shifts to a dispute about whether the defendant's school (madhhab) is considered in the oath. It concludes that the defendant's school is not considered, but the claimant's school is debated: (1) only the judge's school matters, (2) not even that, (3) the judge asks the claimant if he believes the right exists. The preferred view is the third, illustrated by the story of Qadi Abu 'Asim: a woman claimed 'idda maintenance; the judge inquired about the husband's school—if Shafi'i, he was sworn differently than if Hanafi. This shows the judge adapts the oath to the claimant's belief to avoid injustice.

Key Terms
سبب لا يرتفع (sabab lā yarta fi'): A cause that cannot be reversed; once it occurs, it cannot be undone.
حلف على السبب (half 'alā al-sabab): Swearing an oath specifically about the causal act (e.g., 'I did not emancipate'), not about the current state.
شفعة الجوار (shuf'at al-jiwār): Preemption right based on neighborhood; a right to purchase adjoining property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the cause of emancipation can be reversed if the slave apostatizes.
B) Because the cause of emancipation cannot be reversed, so there is no point in swearing on the current state.
C) Because the slave's claim is about the past, not the present.
D) Because the master is the owner and has the right to choose the form of oath.

Key Takeaway
In cases of irreversible cause, the oath must be on the cause; the judge should formulate the oath by inquiring into the claimant's belief to ensure fairness.

Oath on the State: Reversible Causes in Slave Emancipation Claims
Ruling and rationale for oath on state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oath-taking when a female slave or non-Muslim slave claims emancipation, and explain why the oath is on the current state rather than on the cause.

Core Idea
When emancipation can be reversed, the master swears on the current state, not on the cause.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a slave claims to have been emancipated, the master typically swears an oath regarding the cause (the act of emancipation). However, this passage introduces an exception: if the slave is a female slave or a non-Muslim slave, the master swears on the current state (al-hasil) because the cause (emancipation) could be reversed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and legal implications of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
First, identify what the passage rules: for female slave and non-Muslim slave, the oath is on the current state (al-hasil). This means the master swears 'she is not free now' rather than 'I did not emancipate her'.
2
Understand the Rationale
The passage states the reason: because the cause (sabab) may be reversed in these two cases. Understand what 'cause' refers to: the act of emancipation that would have made the slave free.
Reversible means that even if emancipation occurred, it could be undone by subsequent events.
3
Trace the Reversal Scenarios
For the female slave, reversal can happen through apostasy (al-ridda) – she leaves Islam, joins dar al-harb, and is captured, leading to re-enslavement. For the non-Muslim slave, reversal occurs by breaking the covenant (of dhimma) and joining dar al-harb, then being captured.
The underlying principle: if the legal status (freedom) can be lost after being acquired, then the oath on the current state is appropriate.
4
Compare with Irreversible Cases
Contrast with the case of a Muslim slave (not in this passage but implied). For a Muslim slave, the cause (emancipation) is irreversible because a Muslim cannot be re-enslaved after becoming free. Therefore, the master swears on the cause.
This distinction shows the Hanafi method of considering the potential permanence of the legal effect.
5
Apply to a Hypothetical
Consider a female slave (umm walad) who claims her master set her free. The master denies it. According to the ruling, on what should the master swear? Answer: He swears that she is not free now (on the current state). If later she apostatizes and is recaptured, that would nullify the free status.
Ensure understanding: the oath is key in judicial proceedings to resolve the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الأمة والعبد الكافر على الحاصل (وفي الأمة" والعبد الكافر على الحاصل ; لأن السبب قد يرتفع فيهما , أما في الأمة فالبردة واللحاق إلى دار الحرب , ثُمَّ السَّبي , وأما في العبد الكافر فبنقض العهد واللحاق , ثُمَّ السَّبي.
And regarding the female slave and the non-Muslim slave, the oath is upon the state (al-hasil). (And regarding the female slave and the non-Muslim slave, the oath is upon the state, because the cause may be reversed in them. As for the female slave, through apostasy (al-ridda) and joining to dar al-harb, then being captured. And as for the non-Muslim slave, through breaking the covenant (of dhimma) and joining to dar al-harb, then being captured.)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that when a female slave (umm walad or regular) or a non-Muslim slave (dhimmi) claims emancipation, the master swears on the current state (al-hasil) rather than on the cause (the act of emancipation). The reason is that the cause (emancipation) can be reversed in these cases. Specifically, a female slave who is emancipated may later apostatize and flee to enemy territory, be captured, and re-enslaved. Similarly, a non-Muslim slave (dhimmi) may break his covenant and flee to enemy territory, be captured, and re-enslaved. Therefore, since the cause is not irreversible, the oath is properly made on the present condition: 'she is not free now' or 'he is not free now.' This contrasts with the Muslim slave case where the cause cannot be reversed.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-hāṣil): The current state or actual condition (e.g., 'she is not free now').
الردة (al-ridda): Apostasy from Islam, which can lead to re-enslavement if captured.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the master's testimony is stronger.
D) Because the female slave is not trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires oath on the current state (al-hasil) when the cause of emancipation can be reversed, as in the cases of female slaves and non-Muslim slaves.

Oath for Heirs (on Knowledge) and Donees/Purchasers (on Certainty)
Ruling on form of oath for heirs and for donees/purchasers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct form of oath for an heir versus a donee/purchaser in a property claim and explain the rationale based on the party's knowledge.

Core Idea
Heirs swear on their knowledge; donees and purchasers swear an absolute oath.

In property disputes without evidence, the defendant must take an oath. However, the form of the oath depends on how the defendant came into possession. This ruling from the Hanafi school distinguishes between heirs, who may lack full knowledge of the property's history, and direct recipients like donees or purchasers, who have firsthand knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of defendants
Note that the passage distinguishes between an heir who inherits property and a donee or purchaser who receives it directly.
Heir: limited connection to previous transactions. Donee/purchaser: direct involvement.
2
State the oath form for each category
The heir swears on his knowledge ('I know that...' or 'I do not know that...'), while the donee/purchaser swears an absolute oath ('By Allah, this is not your property').
The technical term for the absolute oath is 'al-battāt'.
3
Understand the rationale
The heir may not know the original transaction or the deceased's dealings, so he can only testify to his knowledge. The donee/purchaser has direct knowledge and can swear with certainty.
This is a dalīl 'aqlī (rational proof) based on the difference in knowledge.
4
Apply the ruling
As a judge, you must administer the appropriate oath form. If the defendant refuses to swear, judgment goes to the claimant.
The oath is administered by the judge, but the form is prescribed by the sharī'ah.

[٢]
( ويحلف على العلم من وَرِثَ شيئاً فادعاه آخر وعلى البتاتِ إن وُهِبَ له أو اشتراه) ، البتات : القطع ، فالموهوب له والمشتري يحلفان بالله ليس هذا ملكاً لك
[2]
(He swears upon knowledge: one who inherits something and another claims it; and upon certainty if something is gifted to him or he purchases it.) Al-battat: the definitive. So the donee and the purchaser swear by Allah: 'This is not your property.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains the form of oath for two different types of defendants. First, an heir who inherits property and is then sued by a claimant without evidence: the heir is sworn on his knowledge (i.e., 'do you know that this belongs to the claimant?'), not on absolute negation, because the heir may not have direct knowledge of the deceased's transactions. Second, a donee or purchaser who receives property directly: they swear an absolute oath ('by Allah, this is not your property') because they have direct knowledge of the transaction. The word 'battat' means definitive or decisive, indicating an oath without qualification.

Key Terms
البتات (al-battāt): An absolute, definitive oath without qualification or reference to knowledge.
الحلف على العلم (al-ḥalf 'alā al-'ilm): Swearing an oath based on one's knowledge, i.e., 'I know that...' rather than 'It is not...'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the heir is not the original owner and may not know the full history.
B) Because the heir is less trustworthy.
C) Because the heir is a minor.
D) Because the claim is weaker against an heir.

Key Takeaway
The form of the oath in property claims depends on the defendant's connection to the property: heirs swear based on their knowledge, while donees and purchasers swear an absolute oath.

Forfeiture of the Right to Oath via Ransom or Settlement
Commentary on forfeiting the right to oath after ransom or settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a plaintiff forfeits the right to demand an oath, and distinguish valid forfeiture from invalid waiver.

Core Idea
Ransom or settlement with money forfeits the oath right; unilateral waiver does not.

In the Hanafi school, the right to demand an oath is a judicial right that can be relinquished only through a compensatory transaction (ransom or settlement). This commentary explains that even a truthful oath may be avoided for honor, and the plaintiff cannot later demand the oath once he accepts payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
Read the text to determine that after ransom or settlement, the plaintiff cannot demand the oath. Note the conditions: the payment must be offered by the defendant and accepted by the plaintiff.
The ruling is limited to cases where the oath right is attached to a claim.
2
Understand the rationale behind avoiding truthful oaths
The text cites the incident of Sayyiduna Uthman who paid to avoid swearing truthfully, fearing that his oath might be misinterpreted as false. Al-Sadr al-Shahid declares avoiding truthful oaths wajib when it may cause harm to honor.
This is a unique Hanafi emphasis; other schools may not obligate avoidance.
3
Distinguish valid forfeiture from invalid waiver
The plaintiff cannot unilaterally drop the oath by saying 'I drop it' or 'I waive it' without consideration. Only through ransom (money paid by defendant) or settlement (compromise on the claim) does the right forfeit.
Sale of the oath is invalid because the oath is not property.
4
Analyze the dalil and authority weight
The proof includes the action of Sayyiduna Uthman and the Prophet's permission for truthful oaths. The authority is from later fatwa works (Fatawa Bazzaziyya), not Zahir al-Riwayah, indicating the ruling's later development.
The dalil is zanni in transmission and meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية ولا يحلف بعده ) : أي إذا توجه الحلف ، فقال : أعطيتُ هذه العشرة فداء عن الحلف على كذا، وقبل الآخر، أو قال المدعي : صالحتُ عن دعوى الحلف على كذا، وقبل الآخرُ صح، وسقط حق الحلف. بمال، وكذا عثمان له افتدى يمينه حين ادعى عليه أربعون درهماً، فقيل : ألا تحلف وأنت صادق، فقال : أخافُ أن يوافق قدر يميني، فيقال : هذا بيمينه الكاذبة ؛ ولأن فيه صون عرضه وهو مستحسن عقلاً وشرعاً ذكره الحموي، وذكر الصدرُ الشهيد أنَّ الاحتراز عن اليمين الصادقة واجب. انتهى. أي ثابت بدليل جواز الحلف صادقاً، وقد وقع من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تعليماً وتشريعاً، ثم الافتداء قد يكون بمال، مثل المدعي غالباً، وقد يكون بأقل منه، وأما الصلح منه فإنما يكون منه على مال، هو أقل من المدعي غالباً. كذا في «النهاية»، وغيرها. وقيد بالفداء والصلح ؛ لأنّ المدعي لو أسقط اليمين قصداً بأن قال : برئت من الحلف، أو تركته لك، أو وهبتُهُ لا يصح ، وله التحليفُ بخلاف البراءة عن المال ؛ لأنّ التحليف للحاكم. كذا في «كتاب الدعوى» من «الفتاوى البَزَّازية». [1] قوله : ولا يحلف بعده ؛ يعني ليس للمدَّعي أن يستحلفه على : تلك الـ ، اليمين أبداً ؛ لأنه أسقط حقه بالافتداء أو الصلح، وفيه إشعار بأنه لا يجوز أن يبيع اليمين ؛ لأنّ الشراء عقد تمليك المال بالمال واليمين ليست بمال، فله أن يستحلفه بعد ذلك. وجي
Cream of the Finale for the Support of the Care. 'And he does not swear after it': meaning when the oath is directed, he says 'I gave these ten as ransom for the oath on such and such' and the other accepts, or the plaintiff says 'I have settled for the claim of the oath on such and such' and the other accepts, it is valid and the right of the oath drops. With money, and similarly Uthman ransomed his oath when forty dirhams were claimed against him. It was said: 'Will you not swear while you are truthful?' He said: 'I fear that my oath may coincide with the truth, and it will be said: This is by his false oath.' And because it contains preservation of his honor, which is approved by intellect and law. Al-Hamawi mentioned it, and al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned that avoiding the truthful oath is obligatory. End. That is established by the evidence of the permissibility of swearing truthfully, and it occurred from the Prophet (peace be upon him) as teaching and legislation. Then the ransom may be with money, like the plaintiff mostly, and may be with less than that. As for settlement from it, it is only with money, which is usually less than what is claimed. Thus in al-Nihaya and others. And it is restricted to ransom and settlement because if the plaintiff drops the oath intentionally by saying 'I am free from the oath' or 'I leave it for you' or 'I give it to you,' it is not valid, and he has the right to administer the oath, unlike freeing from money, because administering the oath belongs to the judge. Thus in Kitab al-Da'wa from al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya. [1] His saying: 'And he does not swear after it' means that the plaintiff cannot make him swear that oath ever, because he has dropped his right by ransom or settlement. And it indicates that it is not permissible to sell the oath, because sale is a contract of exchanging property for property, and the oath is not property, so he can make him swear after that. Wajii?
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the ruling that after a defendant pays a ransom or makes a settlement to avoid an oath, the plaintiff can no longer demand that oath. The text clarifies the conditions: the payment must be accepted, and the plaintiff's right to the oath is forfeited. It cites the incident of Sayyiduna Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) who paid to avoid swearing an oath even though he was truthful, fearing that if his oath coincided with the truth, people might think it was false and thus suspect him of perjury. The commentator notes that the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted truthful oaths for teaching purposes, but avoiding even a truthful oath is considered wajib (obligatory) when it can lead to misunderstanding or harm to one's honor. The passage also emphasizes that the plaintiff cannot unilaterally waive the oath; it must be through a ransom or settlement that involves consideration. Selling the oath is invalid because it is not property. The authority weight is from later fatwa works (Fatawa Bazzaziyya), and the ruling is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
الافتداء (al-iftidā’): Paying money or something of value to avoid having to swear an oath.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement where the plaintiff agrees to drop the claim for the oath in exchange for money.
اليمين الصادقة (al-yamīn al-ṣādiqah): An oath that is truthful in content.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The right to the oath is forfeited permanently.
B) The plaintiff can still demand the oath later.
C) The defendant is automatically free from the claim.
D) The judge must drop the case.

Key Takeaway
The right to demand an oath is forfeited only through a compensated transaction (ransom or settlement) accepted by both parties; unilateral waiver is ineffective.
