Restricting the Oath to Negation in Tahāluf
Explains the preferred wording of the oath in mutual oath-taking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred wording of the oath in mutual oath-taking (tahāluf) and explain why the Hanafi school restricts it to negation.

Core Idea
In mutual oath-taking, the oath must be purely negatory to avoid evasion.

When two parties dispute over a sale and have no proof, the qadi orders mutual oaths. A key question is: what should the oath say? This passage from al-Hidayah explains the preferred wording—negation only—and the reasoning behind it, supported by the hadith of Qasamah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested scenario
After mutual oath-taking, the judge annuls the sale. The buyer and seller have conflicting claims about the price.
The scenario assumes both parties have no witnesses.
2
Examine the alternative wording
Some scholars allowed including affirmation with negation for emphasis, e.g., 'By Allah, I did not buy it for 2000, and indeed I bought it for 1000.'
This was considered by earlier Hanafis.
3
Analyze the problem with affirmation
Including affirmation could allow evasion: a party might swear truthfully on the negation but still be lying in effect, e.g., if the buyer actually bought for 1900, he truthfully denies 2000 but does not reveal the real price.
This undermines the purpose of the oath.
4
Study the preferred view
The most correct (al-asahh) is to restrict to negation only. The author cites al-Kifayah: if the seller sold for 1900, he would not claim 2000 because he knows the buyer would swear truthfully.
The oath should match the claim exactly, not include extraneous matter.
5
Review the proof
The hadith of Qasamah shows oaths in disputes were only negatory: 'By Allah, you did not kill, and you do not know a killer for him.'
This is a direct dalil for the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسخ القاضي البيع) : أي بعد التحالف
باعه بألف ، ولقد باعه بألفين، ويحلف المشتري : بالله ما اشتراه بألفين، ولقد اشتراه
بألف، يضم الإثبات إلى النفي تأكيداً. كذا في «الهداية».
وبيانُه : إنه لو حَلَفَ المشتري : بالله ما اشتراه بألفين، رُبَّما يحلف ويكون باراً في يمينه ، فلعله اشتراه بألف وتسعمئةٍ فيبطل حق البائع في الزيادة، وكذا البائع لو حلف : بالله ما باعه بألف رُبَّما يحلف الجواز أنه باعه بألف درهم، ويكون صادقاً في يمينه أنه لم
يبع بألف درهم. والأصح الاقتصار على النفي، ولا عبرة بذلك الوهم ؛ لأن البائع لو كان باعه بألف وتسعمئة لا يدعي البيع بألفين؛ لأنه يعلم أنَّ المشتري متى حلف على دعواه لا يُبالي بالحلف ؛ لأنه لا يحنث بيمينه، وكذا المشتري لو كان اشتراه بألف درهم لا يدعي الشراء بألف ؛ لأنه يعلمُ أنَّ البائع لا يبالي بالحلف على ألف ؛ لأنه لا يحنث في يمينه. كذا في «الكفاية»(٢). ولذا قال في «الهداية)) (۳) : والأصح الاقتصار على النفي؛ لأن الأيمان على ذلك وضعت ، دل عليه حديث القسامة : بالله ما قتلتُم، ولا علمتم له قاتلاً. انتهى.
And the judge annuls the sale): meaning after mutual oath-taking. He sold it for one thousand, and indeed he sold it for two thousand. The buyer swears: 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand, and indeed I bought it for one thousand.' He includes affirmation with negation for emphasis. Thus in al-Hidayah. Its explanation: If the buyer swears 'By Allah, I did not buy it for two thousand,' perhaps he swears and is truthful in his oath, for he might have bought it for one thousand nine hundred, and the seller's right to the increase is lost. Similarly, if the seller swears 'By Allah, I did not sell it for one thousand,' perhaps he swears by permission [literally 'permissibility'] that he sold it for one thousand dirhams, and he is truthful in his oath that he did not sell it for one thousand dirhams. The most correct is to restrict to negation, and no consideration is given to that illusion. Because if the seller had sold it for one thousand nine hundred, he would not claim the sale for two thousand, for he knows that when the buyer swears regarding his claim, he does not care [to be truthful] about the oath, because he would not violate his oath. Similarly, if the buyer had bought it for one thousand dirhams, he would not claim the purchase for one thousand, for he knows that the seller does not care [to be truthful] about the oath regarding one thousand, because he would not violate his oath. Thus in al-Kifayah. Therefore, he said in al-Hidayah: 'The most correct is to restrict to negation, because oaths were established for that.' This is supported by the hadith of Qasamah: 'By Allah, you did not kill, and you do not know a killer for him.' End.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter of mutual oath-taking (tahāluf) in sales disputes. The author clarifies the preferred wording of the oath: the buyer should swear only a negation of the seller's claim, not including his own affirmative claim. Some had suggested including both negation and affirmation ('I did not buy it for two thousand, rather I bought it for one thousand'), but the preferred view is to restrict to negation to avoid potential false oaths by evasion. The proof is the hadith of Qasamah, where the oath was only negatory. The author cites al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah to support this.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between parties to a dispute when both are both claimant and denier.
اقتصار على النفي (iqtiṣār 'alā al-nafy): Restricting the oath to negation of the opponent's claim, without including one's own affirmative claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The hadith of Qasamah where the oath was only negatory
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy to the oath of the defendant

Key Takeaway
In mutual oath-taking, the oath should be restricted to negation of the opponent's claim to prevent evasion and based on the sunnah from Qasamah.

The Judge's Annulment of the Sale After Mutual Oath-Taking
Discusses necessity and conditions of the judge's annulment of the sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge annuls a sale after mutual oath-taking and explain the practical difference between the Hanafi view and the view of automatic voidance.

Core Idea
The judge must annul a disputed sale upon request; it does not become void automatically.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies that after mutual oath-taking in a sale dispute, the contract is not automatically void. The judge plays a crucial role in annulling the sale to resolve the uncertainty, but only upon the request of one or both parties. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on void sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge's annulment of a sale after mutual oath-taking. The ruling is that the judge annuls upon request; some scholars held it becomes void automatically, but the correct Hanafi view is that judge's annulment is necessary.
The ruling is a judicial act, not an automatic consequence.
2
Analyze the condition for judge's action
The judge annuls only upon the request of one or both parties. Without request, the judge does not act. If both parties request, it is also valid. But if only one party requests, the judge must annul.
The condition is that the sale dispute persists after mutual oath-taking and a party seeks judicial resolution.
3
Distinguish between the two views
The passage presents two views: (1) the sale becomes void automatically upon mutual oath-taking, and (2) the judge must annul it. It declares the second as correct. The practical consequence is shown with the example of a slave-girl: if automatic voidance, she becomes unlawful immediately, whereas with judge's annulment, the ruling differs.
The example illustrates that automatic voidance would create immediate prohibitions that may be avoided by requiring a judicial act.
4
Understand the exception to judge's involvement
If both parties agree to annul the sale themselves, no judge is needed. They can mutually annul it. However, if only one party unilaterally annuls, that is insufficient.
This exception underscores that mutual consent can dissolve the contract without judicial intervention.
5
Note the authority of the relied-upon position
The text states that the first view (judge's annulment) is correct. This is from al-Hidayah and is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, as confirmed by later scholars like al-Marghinani.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفسخ القاضي البيع؛ أي بطلب أحدهما، أو بطلب كليهما، وقيل: ينفسخ، والأول هو الصحيح؛ لأنه لم يثبت ما ادعاه كلُّ واحدٍ منهما، فيبقى بيع مجهول، فيفسحه القاضي قطعاً للمنازعة، أو يقال : إنه إذا لم يثبت البدل بقي بيعاً بلا فاسد، ولا بُدَّ من الفسخ في البيع الفاسد، فلو كان المبيع جارية وطئها، ولو فسد بنفس التحالف لم تحل له. وقيدنا بطلب أحدهما؛ لأنه لا يفسخه القاضي بدون طلب أحدهما، ولو فسخاه انفسخ بلا توقف على القاضي، وإن فسخ أحدهما لا يكفي. ذكره في «البحر»، وغيره.
His statement: 'And the judge annuls the sale' means at the request of one of them, or at the request of both. And it is said: it becomes annulled [by itself], and the first is correct, because the claim of each has not been established, so the sale remains an unknown sale, so the judge annuls it to end the dispute. Or it is said: if the countervalue is not established, the sale remains a sale without a price, and annulment is necessary in a void sale. So if the sold item was a slave-girl and he had intercourse with her, and if it became void by the mutual oath-taking itself, she would not be lawful for him. And we restricted it to the request of one of them, because the judge does not annul it without the request of one of them. And if both annul it, it becomes annulled without waiting for the judge, but the annulment by one of them is not sufficient. Al-Bahr and others mentioned it.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'the judge annuls the sale' from the matn. The author explains that the judge's annulment occurs at the request of one or both parties; it does not happen automatically upon mutual oath-taking. Some held that the sale becomes void by itself, but the correct view is that the judge must annul it because the sale is now uncertain (majhul) due to unresolved claims. An example is given: if the sold item was a slave-girl and the buyer had intercourse, automatic voidance would make her unlawful to him, but since the judge annuls, the ruling is different. The condition that the judge acts only upon request is clarified: if both parties agree to annul, no judge is needed; one party's unilateral annulment is insufficient.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Judicial annulment of a contract due to unresolved dispute after mutual oath-taking.
بطلب أحدهما (bi-ṭalabi aḥadihimā): At the request of one of the two parties (buyer or seller).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
After mutual oath-taking in a sale dispute, the Hanafi school requires the judge's annulment upon the request of either party; the sale does not become void automatically.

Consequence of Refusal to Swear in Mutual Oath-Taking
States that refusal to swear results in liability for the opponent's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of nukūl in mutual oath-taking and distinguish it from cases where nukūl is not applicable due to a different primary dispute.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear binds the refuser to the opponent's claim, but only after the judge's ruling.

This passage from al-Hidayah's commentary explains what happens when one party refuses to take a required oath in a sale price dispute. It clarifies that nukūl does not automatically decide the case—the judge's ruling is needed to make it binding. It also addresses an important nuance: when the dispute is primarily about something else (like the identity of goods), the price disagreement is secondary and does not trigger mutual oath-taking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (nukūl) and what the consequence is (the refuser is bound by the other's claim).
The ruling applies to both buyer and seller in mutual oath-taking.
2
Understand the Legal Basis
The passage gives two possible bases: iqrar (acknowledgment) or badhl (rendering). The judge's ruling strengthens the nukūl to become binding.
The basis affects the timing of binding (requires judge's ruling).
3
Note the Role of the Judge
The judge's ruling (qaḍā') is necessary to make the nukūl binding. Without it, the refusal alone does not settle the case.
The text says 'lāzimahu daʿwā al-ākhar' but adds 'bi al-qaḍā''.
4
Distinguish Secondary Price Disputes
If the primary dispute is about something other than price (e.g., the identity of goods), the price issue is secondary and mutual oath-taking is not required.
Example: dispute over a skin of butter where the seller denies it is his skin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن نكلَ لَزِمَهُ دعوى الآخر) : أي إذا عرض اليمينُ أَوَّلاً على المشتري ، فإن نكلَ لَزِمَهُ دعوى البائع ، فإن حَلَفَ يعرض اليمين على البائع ، فإن حَلَفَ يفسخُ البيع، وإن نكل لزمه دعوى المشتري.
ثُمَّ اعلم أن الاختلاف إذا كان في الثمن فالتحالف قبل قبض المبيع موافق للقياس؛ لأنَّ البائع يدعي زيادةَ الثَّمن والمشتري يُنْكِرُها ، والمشتري يدعي وجوب تسليم المبيع بأقل الثَّمنين ، والبائع يُنْكِرُه ، فكل منهما مدَّع ومُنْكِرٌ فيتحالفان ، أمَّا بعد قبض المبيع فمخالف للقياس، فإن المشتري لا يدعي شيئاً؛ لأنَّ المبيع قد سلّم له، والبائع يدعى زيادة الثَّمن والمشتري يُنكره.
وقيدنا بطلب أحدهما؛ لأنه لا يفسخه القاضي بدون طلب أحدهما، ولو فسخاه انفسخ بلا توقف على القاضي، وإن فسخ أحدهما لا يكفي.
(1) قوله : من نكل ... الخ؛ يعني أنَّ مَن نكَلّ عن اليمين من البائع والمشتري لزمه دعوى صاحبه بالقضاء؛ لأنه صار مُقراً أو باذلاً، فَلَزِمه إذا اتصل به القضاء، وهو المراد بقوله : دعوى الآخر، أما على اعتبار البذل فالأمر بين. وأما على اعتبار أنه إقرار؛ فلأنه إقرارٌ فيه شبهة البذل، فلا يكون موجباً بانفراده بل بتقوية القضاء يكون حجَّةً ملزمة، وهذا كله إذا كان اختلافهما في البذل مقصوداً. أما إذا كان في ضمن شيء آخر، كما أنَّ رجلاً اشترى من رجل آخرَ سَمْناً في زق، وإنه مئة رطل مثلاً، ثمَّ جَاءَ بالزِّق للرد على صاحبه ووزنه عشرون رطلاً مثلاً، فقال البائع : ليس هذا زقي، وقال المشتري : هذا زقك، فالقولُ للمشتري في أنه الزق؛ لأنه اختلاف في المقبوض، والقولُ فيه قول القابض في نفس القبض والمقبوض فكذا في مقدار المقبوض أيضاً، وإن كان في ضمنه اختلاف في الثمن؛ لأنَّ الثمن يزداد بنقصان الزق، وينقص بزيادته، فالبائع يدعي زيادة الثمن، والمشتري ينكرها، ولم يعتبر هذا الاختلاف في إيجاب التحالف؛ لأن الاختلاف فيه وقع مقتضياً لاختلافهما في الزق).
And whoever refrains [from swearing] is bound by the other's claim): meaning if the oath is first presented to the buyer, then if he refrains, he is bound by the seller's claim. If he swears, the oath is presented to the seller; if he swears, the sale is annulled; and if he refrains, he is bound by the buyer's claim. Then know that if the dispute is about the price, mutual oath-taking before delivery of the sold item is in accordance with analogy, because the seller claims an increase in price and the buyer denies it, and the buyer claims the obligation to deliver the sold item at the lower of the two prices and the seller denies it, so each is both a claimant and a denier, so they take mutual oaths. As for after delivery, it is contrary to analogy, because the buyer does not claim anything, since the sold item has been delivered to him, and the seller claims an increase in price and the buyer denies it. And we restricted it to the request of one of them, because the judge does not annul it without the request of one of them, and if both annul it, it is annulled without waiting for the judge, and the annulment by one of them is not sufficient. (1) His statement: 'Whoever refrains... etc.' means that whoever among the seller and buyer refrains from the oath, the claim of the other is binding upon him by the judge's ruling, because he becomes either an acknowledger or a renderer, so it becomes binding when the ruling is connected to it. That is what is meant by 'the other's claim.' As for considering it as rendering, the matter is clear. As for considering it as acknowledgment, it is an acknowledgment with a semblance of rendering, so it is not binding by itself, but with the strengthening of the judge's ruling it becomes a binding proof. All this is if their dispute regarding the price is intended. But if it is within something else, such as a man buying from another man clarified butter in a skin, and it was a hundred pounds, then he brought the skin for return to its owner and weighed it twenty pounds, and the seller said, 'This is not my skin,' and the buyer said, 'This is your skin,' then the statement is the buyer's concerning that it is the skin, because it is a dispute about the received item, and the statement in that is of the receiver regarding the receipt itself and the received item. Similarly regarding the quantity of the received item as well, even if within it is a dispute about the price, because the price increases with the decrease of the skin and decreases with its increase. So the seller claims an increase in price and the buyer denies it, and this dispute is not considered in requiring mutual oath-taking, because the dispute therein occurred as necessitating their dispute about the skin.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling of nukūl (refusal to swear) in mutual oath-taking. If one party refuses the oath after it is presented, he is bound by the opponent's claim. The commentary then explains that this is because the refuser is considered either an acknowledger (muqirr) or a renderer (bādhi), and the judge's ruling makes it binding. It then distinguishes between disputes about the price itself and disputes that incidentally involve price, such as the example of a skin of clarified butter: here, the primary dispute is about the identity and quantity of the item, and the price difference is secondary, so mutual oath-taking is not required. The text also notes that the judge's annulment requires request.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath when required to do so in a judicial proceeding.
بذل (badhl): Rendering the opponent's claim without compulsion, i.e., the refuser is considered to have conceded.
تحالف (taḥāluf): Mutual oath-taking: both parties swear regarding their conflicting claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After nukūl, what is the legal consequence for the party who refuses to swear?
A) The case is dismissed
B) He is bound by the opponent's claim upon the judge's ruling
C) He is given another chance to swear
D) The sale is annulled automatically

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, refusal to swear (nukūl) in mutual oath-taking binds the refuser to the opponent's claim, but this only takes effect through the judge's ruling.

No Mutual Oath for Disputes Over Term, Option, or Partial Payment
Ruling on when seller and buyer differ over ancillary terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on when mutual oath is not required in sale disputes and evaluate the opposing argument from Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The term of payment is not a description of the price; therefore, disputes over it do not trigger mutual oath—only the denier swears.

When a seller and buyer disagree about the payment term, an option condition, or whether part of the price was paid, should both parties take a mutual oath? The Hanafi school answers no: only the party who denies the claim must swear. This ruling rests on a precise interpretation of the prophetic hadith and a key distinction between the price itself and its ancillary conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenarios
The passage lists three specific types of disputes: over the term (ajal), over the condition of option (khiyar al-shart), and over receipt of part of the price. Understand each scenario.
The scenarios are ancillary to the core elements of the sale (mabee' and thaman).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
For these scenarios, no mutual oath (tahalf) is required. Instead, the denier (munkir) takes an oath alone. Determine who the denier is in each case.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud is cited: 'When the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still extant, the statement is the seller's, or they rescind.' The Hanafi school interprets this as limiting mutual oath to disputes about the sold item or the price.
The term 'mutabayi'an' (two parties to a sale) is derived from 'bay' (sale), so the ruling applies only to the essential elements of the sale.
4
Apply the reasoning to each scenario
For the term: it is not a description of the price because the price remains after the term expires. For option: it is an external condition. For partial payment: it is about performance, not the price obligation itself. Thus, these disputes are akin to a reduction in price, where no mutual oath is required.
The passage contrasts with disputes over the description (quality, type) of the price, which do require mutual oath.
5
Consider the opposing view and Hanafi response
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the term affects monetary value, so it is like a difference in the price amount, and the general wording of the hadith does not specify. The Hanafi response reiterates that the term is not a description and that the hadith is understood restrictively.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi procedure uses a single oath; the opposing view uses mutual oaths.

Source Text

ولا تحالف 
[١] قوله : ولا تحالف ... الخ ؛ يعني أنهما لو اختلفا في الأجل ؛ سواء كان في نفس الأجل أو قدره، أو اختلفا في شرط الخيار سواءً كان في وجوده، بأن قال أحدهما : البيع بالخيار، والآخر ينكره، أو في مدته، أو اختلفا في قبض بعض الثمن أو كله ، بأن قال المشتري : أديت بعضه أو كله ، والبائع ينكرُ، فلم يتحالفا، والقولُ يكون للمنكير مع يمينه ؛ لأنَّ الشرعَ عَلَّقَ وجوب التحالف باختلاف المتبايعين. وهو اسم مشتق من البيع، فيتعلق وجوب التحالف باختلافهما فيما يثبت به البيع، والبيع يثبت بالمبيع والثمن، وهذا اختلاف في غير المبيع والثمن ؛ لأن بانعدام ما نحن فيه من الأجل، وشرط الخيار، واستيفاء بعض الثمن أو كله لا يختل ما به قوام العقد، فأشبه الاختلاف في الحط من الثمن، وفيهما لا يجب التحالف، فكذا هاهنا. بخلاف الاختلاف في وصف الثمن ؛ مثل الجودة والرداءة، أو جنس الثمن ؛ مثل الدراهم والدنانير، فإنّ هذا الاختلاف بمنزلة الاختلاف في القدر، حتى يجري فيه التحالف ؛ لأنَّ ذلك يرجع إلى الاختلاف في نفس الثمن، فإنَّ الثمن دين في الذمة، وهو يعرفُ بالوصف بأنه جيّد أو وسط أو رديء، والأجل ليس كذلك ؛ لأنه ليس بوصف له ، ألا ترى أنَّ الثمن موجود بعد مضي الأجل، ولو كان وصفاً لتبعه. (۱) من حديث ابن مسعود ، وقد ورد بألفاظ مختلفة : منها : (إذا اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة فالقول قول البائع أو يترادان)، في سنن الدارمي (۲) : (۳۲۵) ، وسنن ابن ماجه»(۲ : ۷۳۷)، و سنن الدارقطني (۳): (۲۰) ، و(مسند الشاشي) (۱) : (۳۲۸) ، و(المعجم الكبير (١٨ : ١٧٤) واللفظ له، وفي الموطأ» (۲) : (٦٧١) بلاغاً، وقد صححه الحاكم، وحسنه البيهقي، وقال ابن عبد البر: هو منقطع إلا أنه مشهور الأصل عند جماعة العلماء تلقوه بالقبول وبنوا عليه كثيرا من فروعه، وقال صاحب التنقيح) والذي يظهر أنه حديث ابن مسعود ه بمجموع طرقه له أصل، بل هو حديث حسن يحتج به لكن في لفظه اختلاف والله أعلم، وأيده الزيلعي في «نصب الراية( ٤ : ١٠٥)، وقال صاحب (معتصر المختصر (۲): (١٣٤) : إنه من الأحاديث التي استغنى عن طلب الإسناد فيها لصحتها عند العلماء، وينظر : تلخيص الحبير(۳: ۳۱) والتحقيق (۲) :) (١٨٤) ، و(الخلاصة (٢ : ٧٦)، وغيرها.
في الأجل، وشرط الخيار، وقبض بعض الثمن، وحلف المنكر
في الأجل، وشرط الخيار، وقبض بعض الثَّمن" ، وحلف المنكر)، سواء اختلفا في أصل الأجل، أو في قذره، فقال المشتري : الثَّمنُ مؤجل، وأنكر البائع ، أو قال المشتري : الثَّمنُ مؤجل إلى سنة ، وقال البائع : بل نصف سنة، حلف منكر الزيادة، أو قال أحدهما : البيع بشرط الخيار، وأنكر الآخر، وقال أحدهما : لي الخيار إلى ثلاثة أيام، وقال الآخر : بل إلى يومين
وقال زفره والشافعي له : يتحالفان في الأجل إذا اختلفا في أصله أو قدره ؛ لأن هذا في معنى الاختلاف في مقدار مالية الثمن، فإنّ المؤجل أنقص من الحال في المالية ؛ ولأنَّ النص أوجب التحالف عند اختلاف المتبايعين، ولم يفصل. والجواب : إنَّ الأجل ليس بوصف الثمن، إذ لو كان وصفاً له فذهب عند ذهابها، إذ الشيء لا يبقى بدون وصفه، ووجوب التحالف للمتبايعين، وهو اسم مشتق من البيع إلى آخر ما قرَّرناه آنفا، فصار كأنه قال : إذا اختلف المتبايعان في المبيع أو الثمن تحالفا.
[۱] قوله : في الأجل ؛ أطلقه ليشمل الاختلاف في أصل الأجل وقدره، كما نبه عليه الشارح الله بقوله : سواء اختلفا في أصل الأجل وقدره. انتهى. وخرج الاختلاف في مضيه، فإنَّ القول فيه للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الأجل حقه، وهو منكر استيفاء حقه.
ويستثنى من الاختلاف في الأجل ما لو اختلفا في أجل السلم، بأن ادّعاه أحدهما ونفاه الآخر، فإنَّ القول فيه لمدَّعيه عند الإمام ؛ لأنه شرط ، وتركه مفسد، وإقدامهما يدل على الصحة، فشهد الظاهرُ لمدَّعيه بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنّ القول لما فيه. ذكره

العلامة الحموي. [٢] قوله : وقبض بعض الثمن ؛ التقييد ببعض الثمن اتفاقي، إذ الاختلاف في قبض كله كذلك كما صرحوا به، ولعله لم يذكر قبض كل الثمن ؛ لأنه مفروغ عنه باعتبار أنه صار بمنزلة سائر الدعاوي. وقيّد بالثمن ؛ لأنهما لو اختلفا في قبض بعض المبيع حلفا. كما صرح في «الكافي»، وغيره. [۳] قوله : حلف منكر الزيادة ؛ لأنّ الأجل، وكذا خيار الشرط يثبتان بعارض
And no mutual oath… [1] His saying: 'And no mutual oath' etc.; meaning that if they differ over the term—whether in the term itself or its measure—or they differ over the condition of option—whether in its existence, such as one saying the sale is with option and the other denies it, or in its duration—or they differ over receipt of part of the price or all of it, such as the buyer saying 'I paid part or all of it' and the seller denies it, then they do not take mutual oath, and the statement is given to the denier with his oath, because the Law attached the obligation of mutual oath to the difference of the two parties to the sale. And it (tahalf) is a noun derived from 'bay' (sale), so the obligation of mutual oath is attached to their difference concerning that which establishes the sale, and the sale is established by the sold item and the price. This is a difference concerning other than the sold item and the price, because by the absence of what we are discussing—the term, the condition of option, and payment of part or all of the price—the foundation of the contract is not impaired. So it resembles the difference over a reduction from the price, and in those two (difference over reduction and over these matters) mutual oath is not required, so likewise here. Contrary to the difference over a description of the price, such as quality and poor quality, or the type of the price, such as dirhams and dinars, for this difference is like the difference in amount, so mutual oath applies in it, because that relates back to difference in the price itself, for the price is a debt in the liability, and it is known by description—good, medium, or poor—and the term is not like that, because it is not a description of it. Do you not see that the price exists after the passing of the term, and if it were a description, it would follow it? (1) From the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, narrated with different wordings: among them: 'When the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still extant, the statement is the seller's, or they rescind.' in Sunan al-Darimi (2:325), Sunan Ibn Majah (2:737), Sunan al-Darqutni (3:20), Musnad al-Shashi (1:328), al-Mu'jam al-Kabir (18:174) with the wording, and in al-Muwatta' (2:671) as a balagh. Al-Hakim declared it authentic, al-Bayhaqi deemed it good, and Ibn 'Abd al-Barr said: it is disconnected but well-known in principle and accepted by a group of scholars who built many of their branches upon it. The author of al-Tanqih said: it appears that the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, through all its chains, has a basis; rather it is a good hadith that can be used as evidence, though its wording differs, and Allah knows best. Al-Zayla'i supported it in Nasb al-Raya (4:105), and the author of Mu'tasar al-Mukhtasar (2:134) said: it is among the hadiths for which seeking chain is unnecessary due to their authenticity according to scholars. See: Talkhis al-Habir (3:31), al-Tahqiq (2:184), al-Khulasah (2:76), etc. Regarding the term, the condition of option, and receipt of some of the price, and the denier's oath… [in the margin:] Regarding the term, the condition of option, and receipt of some of the price, and the denier's oath, whether they differ in the basis of the term or its measure, so the buyer says the price is deferred, and the seller denies, or the buyer says the price is deferred for one year and the seller says half a year – the denier of the increase takes oath. Or one says the sale is with condition of option and the other denies, or one says I have option for three days and the other says for two days. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: they take mutual oath regarding the term if they differ in its basis or measure, because this resembles a difference in the amount of the monetary value, for the deferred is less in value than the immediate; and because the text obligated mutual oath when the two parties differ, without specifying. The answer: the term is not a description of the price, because if it were a description of it, it would disappear when it disappears, since a thing does not remain without its description. And the obligation of mutual oath is for the two parties to the sale (mutabayi'an), which is a noun derived from 'bay' as we established earlier, so it is as if he said: If the two parties to a sale differ regarding the sold item or the price, they take mutual oath. [1] His saying: 'regarding the term' – it is general to include the difference in the basis of the term and its measure, as the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) pointed out by saying: whether they differ in the basis of the term or its measure. End. And the difference regarding its expiration is excluded, for the statement is the buyer's, because the term is his right, and he is denying the fulfillment of his right. And an exception from the difference over the term is when they differ over the term in salam: that one claims it and the other denies it, for the statement is for the claimant according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because it is a condition, and its omission is invalidating (mufsid), and their undertaking indicates validity, so the apparent evidence supports the claimant, contrary to our case, for the statement is for the denier. Mentioned by al-Allamah al-Hamawi. [2] His saying: 'and receipt of some of the price' – the restriction to some of the price is incidental, for the difference over receipt of all of it is likewise as they stated, and perhaps he did not mention receipt of all of the price because it is taken for granted as being like other claims. And it is restricted to the price, because if they differ over receipt of part of the sold item, they take mutual oath, as stated in al-Kafi and others. [3] His saying: 'the denier of the increase takes oath' – because the term, and likewise the condition of option, are established by a contingent [matter].
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute within the chapter of claims (kitab al-da'wa) regarding the application of mutual oath (tahalf) when sellers and buyers differ over ancillary terms of a sale contract. The Hanafi position, established in the zahir al-riwayah, is that mutual oath is not required when the dispute concerns the term (ajal), the condition of option (khiyar al-shart), or the receipt of part of the price. Instead, the denier's statement is taken with his oath. The reasoning is that the hadith which prescribes mutual oath (from Ibn Mas'ud) specifically addresses disputes concerning the essence of the sale—the sold item (mabee') and the price (thaman). These ancillary matters do not affect the existence of the contract itself; they are akin to a reduction in price, which also does not trigger mutual oath. The passage contrasts these with disputes over the description or type of the price (e.g., quality, dirhams vs. dinars), which do affect the very nature of the price and thus require mutual oath. The Hanafi school further argues that the term is not a description of the price, because the price exists independently after the term expires. Zufar and al-Shafi'i dissented, arguing that the term affects monetary value, so it is like a difference in the price amount, and that the general wording of the hadith does not specify. The Hanafi response reiterates that the term is not a description. The passage also notes exceptions: if the dispute is over the expiration of the term, the buyer's word is taken because the term is his right and he denies its fulfillment. In salam sales, if they differ over the term, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the claimant's word is taken because the term is a condition; omitting it would invalidate the contract, and the fact they proceeded indicates validity. Additionally, if they differ over receipt of part of the sold item itself (not the price), mutual oath does apply. The denier of the increase in the term or option takes the oath. This detailed treatment shows the precise boundaries of the tahalf rule in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual oath between the seller and buyer when they dispute over the terms of a sale, each swearing to their claim.
الأجل (al-ajal): The deferred period in which payment is due; the term of credit.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option right given to a party to confirm or cancel a sale within a specified period.
المنكر (al-munkir): The party who denies the claim of the other; the denier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the buyer claims the price was deferred for one year and the seller claims it was deferred for six months, what is the ruling?
A) Both buyer and seller must take mutual oaths.
B) The buyer's statement is accepted with his oath.
C) The seller's statement is accepted with his oath.
D) The sale is invalid due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mutual oath (tahalf) is reserved for disputes over the sold item or the price itself; ancillary terms like payment period or option do not trigger it.

No Tahaluf Between Master and Mukatab in Kitabah
Proving the Hanafi position that there is no mutual oath-taking in kitabah disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that there is no mutual oath-taking (tahaluf) in kitabah disputes and evaluate the opposing arguments from the Sahibayn and Shafi'is.

Core Idea
Kitabah is not a binding commutative contract like sale, so mutual oath-taking does not apply.

In this lesson, we examine a key controversy in Hanafi fiqh regarding the contract of kitabah (manumission by payment). When the master and mukatab dispute over the amount or terms, should they take mutual oaths (tahaluf) as in a sale? Imam Abu Hanifa holds no, while the two companions and Imam al-Shafi'i argue yes. We will dissect the reasoning behind each position and see why the Hanafi school favors the Imam's view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Imam Abu Hanifa rules: there is no mutual oath-taking; the saying is the mukatab's.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that tahaluf applies and the contract is rescinded.
They analogize kitabah to sale.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Outline the qiyas: sale is a commutative contract that admits rescission, and both parties have claims; kitabah is similar, so tahaluf should apply.
The opponent argues that this is in accordance with qiyas.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Imam Abu Hanifa's distinctions: the consideration is for removal of disability, the slave does not claim anything, the contract is not binding on the slave (he can rescind via ajz), and the debt is not binding (kafala invalid).
Thus sale's binding nature is absent, so the analogy fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the mukatab's word (with oath) is taken; under the other view, both take oaths and the contract is rescinded.
This affects the stability of the kitabah contract and the slave's path to freedom.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في بدل الكتابة يعني إذا اختلف المولى والمكاتب فلا تحالف عند الإمام، وقالا : يتحالفان، وتفسخ الكتابة، وهو قول الشافعي له ؛ لأنَّ الكتابة عقد معاوضة تقبل الفسخ، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما مع على الآخر؛ لأنَّ المولى يدعي بدلاً زائداً، والعبد ينكره، والعبد يدعي استحقاق العتق على المولى عند أداء ما يقربه، والمولى ينكره ، فيتحالفان ، كما إذا اختلفا في الثمن، وهو قبل القبض موافق للقياس، فيتعدّى إليه. وللإمام : إنَّ البدل في الكتابة مقابل بفك الحجر، وهو ملك التصرف واليد للحال، وقد سُلّم ذلك للعبد، ولا يدعي على مولاه شيئاً، وقد تقرر أن التحالف بعد القبض يكون على خلاف القياس فلا يتحالفان فيكون القولُ قول العبد ؛ لأنه منكر، وإنما يصير مقابلاً بالعتق عند الأداء، وقبله لا يقابله أصلاً، حتى يقال فيه : إنه اختلاف قبل القبض، وهذا نظير الأجرة، فلها مقابلة بالعين المستأجرة عند العقد، ثم ينتقل إلى المنفعة عند الاستيفاء. والفارق بين البيع والكتابةِ أنَّ البيع لازم من الجانبين، فالمصير إلى التحالف فيه مفيد، حتى إذا نكل أحدهما لزمه دعوى الآخر، ولا يتحقق ذلك في الكتابة ؛ لأنَّ المكاتب إذا نكَل لا يلزمُه شيءٌ لتمكنه من الفسخ بالعجز، والدين فيه غير لازم، حتى لا تجوز الكفالة به، لا أنها معاوضةٌ مطلقاً فغيرُ مُسَلَّم ؛ لأنَّ الكل للمولى من وجه، فلا يكون في معنى البيع.
And not regarding the consideration for kitabah: meaning, if the master and the mukatab disagree, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa there is no mutual oath-taking. But the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say they do take mutual oaths, and the kitabah is rescinded, and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because kitabah is a commutative contract that admits rescission, and each of them is against the other: because the master claims an additional consideration, and the slave denies it; and the slave claims entitlement to freedom upon paying what he acknowledges, and the master denies it; so they take mutual oaths, just as when they disagree over the price, and it is before delivery, it is in accordance with qiyas, so it extends to this. And for Imam Abu Hanifa: The consideration in kitabah is in exchange for the removal of disability (fakk al-hajr), which is the right of disposition and hand [of the slave], and that has been granted to the slave; he does not claim anything against his master. And it has been established that mutual oath-taking after delivery is contrary to qiyas, so they do not take mutual oaths, and the saying is the slave's saying because he is the denier. The consideration only becomes in exchange for freedom at the time of payment; before that it is not in exchange at all, so it is said that this is a dispute before delivery. This is analogous to hire: the hire is in exchange for the leased property at the time of the contract, then it transfers to the usufruct at the time of its use. The difference between sale and kitabah is that sale is binding on both sides, so resorting to mutual oath-taking is beneficial: if one fails to swear, he is bound by the other's claim. That is not realized in kitabah, because if the mukatab fails to swear, nothing is binding on him, due to his ability to rescind by inability; the debt is not binding, so much so that suretyship for it is not valid. It is not conceded that it is an absolute commutative contract, because everything belongs to the master from one perspective, so it is not in the category of sale.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute concerning mutual oath-taking (tahaluf) in the contract of kitabah (manumission by payment). The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa) is that there is no tahaluf; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that there is, and the contract is rescinded. The reasoning: The opponents analogize kitabah to sale, arguing that both are commutative contracts involving mutual claims. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes kitabah on several grounds: (1) The consideration in kitabah is for the removal of legal disability (fakk al-hajr), not for an object. (2) The slave does not claim anything from the master; he is simply under an obligation. (3) Mutual oath-taking is only beneficial in contracts that are binding on both sides; in kitabah, the slave can rescind at any time by claiming inability (ajz), so the debt is not binding. (4) In sale, tahaluf leads to a binding outcome (if one fails to swear, he is liable); in kitabah, failure to swear does not bind the slave to anything. (5) At the time of dispute, the consideration is not yet exchanged for freedom (since freedom occurs only upon full payment). Thus, the Hanafi school considers kitabah to be fundamentally different from sale, and therefore no tahaluf applies.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitabah): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave agrees to pay a sum of money in installments in exchange for eventual freedom.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): The slave who enters into a kitabah contract; he is legally not a free man but has certain rights.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The consideration (money) that the mukatab agrees to pay for his freedom.
التحالف (al-tahaluf): Mutual oath-taking in disputes; each party swears to their claim, and if one fails, the other's claim is upheld.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is there no mutual oath-taking (tahaluf) in kitabah disputes?
A) Because the mukatab can always rescind by claiming inability, so the debt is not binding.
B) Because the master is always the true owner of the property.
C) Because the contract is not commutative (muawadah).
D) Because the slave has no right to make a claim.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, kitabah is not a fully binding commutative contract, so disputes between master and mukatab are resolved without mutual oath-taking, and the mukatab's word is taken.

Dispute in Kitaba Contract: No Tahaluf and Evidence Rules
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahaluf and evidence in kitaba contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on tahaluf in kitaba, analyze their proofs, and apply the evidence rules (beyyina) when no tahaluf occurs.

Core Idea
In kitaba, tahaluf is not applied; the slave's statement is accepted because the contract lacks mutual binding force.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on an exception in the matn of al-Wiqaya. It first clarifies that the earlier exception about the buyer's oath in sale does not extend to kitaba. Then it dives into a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when master and slave under kitaba disagree on the amount due, do they perform mutual oaths (tahaluf) like in a sale? Imam Abu Hanifa says no, while his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, say yes. Understanding their reasoning illuminates the unique nature of the kitaba contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine the exact legal question: whether tahaluf occurs between the master and the mukatab when they dispute the amount of the substitute (badal).
This is a procedural question, not about the validity of the contract itself.
2
Contrast the two Hanafi positions
State the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa (no tahaluf, slave's word) and the ruling of the Sahibayn (tahaluf, contract rescinded).
The Sahibayn's view aligns with al-Shafi'i.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's qiyas on sale
Their proof: kitaba is a commutative contract that admits rescission, analogous to a sale dispute before delivery. Each party claims against the other, so they should take oaths.
They extend the qiyas from sale to kitaba.
4
Examine Imam's distinguishing arguments
Imam counters: the badal is for fakk al-hajr (removal of interdiction), already granted; the slave claims nothing from the master; tahaluf after qabd is an exception to qiyas, not extended; kitaba is not binding like sale (slave can rescind by inability).
The difference in bindingness is key.
5
Extract the evidence (beyyina) rules when no tahaluf occurs
If no evidence, the slave's statement (as denier) is taken. If one party has evidence, it is accepted. If both have evidence, the master's evidence is preferred for proving increase, but the slave's evidence for payment frees him.
These rules follow from the absence of tahaluf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى/باب التحالف ولا في بدل الكتابة (ولا في بدل الكتابة) يعني : فعلى قوله هؤلاء المشايخ ، الاستثناء المذكور في المتن ينصرفُ إلى يمين المشتري إلى التحالف معنى الكلام أنَّ المتبايعين لا يتحالفان عند الإمام، ويكون القول قول المشتري مع يمينه إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يأخذ الحي، ولا يأخذ من ثمن الهالك زائداً على ما أقر به المشتري. فحينئذ لا يمين على المشتري ؛ لأنَّ الاستحلاف إنما شرع في حق المشتري إذا كان منكراً ما يدعيه البائع من الزيادة، فإذا أخذ البائع الحي وترك دعوى الزيادة، ورضي به المشتري فلا حاجة إلى تحليف المشتري. ١ ] قوله : ولا في بدل الكتابة ؛ يعني إذا اختلف المولى والمكاتب فلا تحالف عند الإمام، وقالا : يتحالفان، وتفسخ الكتابة، وهو قول الشافعي له ؛ لأنَّ الكتابة عقد معاوضة تقبل الفسخ، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما مع على الآخر؛ لأنَّ المولى يدعي بدلاً زائداً، والعبد ينكره، والعبد يدعي استحقاق العتق على المولى عند أداء ما يقربه، والمولى ينكره ، فيتحالفان ، كما إذا اختلفا في الثمن، وهو قبل القبض موافق للقياس، فيتعدّى إليه. وللإمام : إنَّ البدل في الكتابة مقابل بفك الحجر، وهو ملك التصرف واليد للحال، وقد سُلّم ذلك للعبد، ولا يدعي على مولاه شيئاً، وقد تقرر أن التحالف بعد القبض يكون على خلاف القياس فلا يتحالفان فيكون القولُ قول العبد ؛ لأنه منكر، وإنما يصير مقابلاً بالعتق عند الأداء، وقبله لا يقابله أصلاً، حتى يقال فيه : إنه اختلاف قبل القبض، وهذا نظير الأجرة، فلها مقابلة بالعين المستأجرة عند العقد، ثم ينتقل إلى المنفعة عند الاستيفاء. والفارق بين البيع والكتابةِ أنَّ البيع لازم من الجانبين، فالمصير إلى التحالف فيه مفيد، حتى إذا نكل أحدهما لزمه دعوى الآخر، ولا يتحقق ذلك في الكتابة ؛ لأنَّ المكاتب إذا نكَل لا يلزمُه شيءٌ لتمكنه من الفسخ بالعجز، والدين فيه غير لازم، حتى لا تجوز الكفالة به، لا أنها معاوضةٌ مطلقاً فغيرُ مُسَلَّم ؛ لأنَّ الكل للمولى من وجه، فلا يكون في معنى البيع. وإذا انعدم التحالف أوجب اعتبار الدعوى والإنكار، فيكون القول للمنكر، وهو العبد، وإن أقام أحدهما فوجب قبول بينتِهِ على ذلك. صرَّحَ به ، وغيره.
The Book of Claims / Chapter of Tahaluf: And not in the substitute of kitaba. Meaning: According to the opinion of these shaykhs, the exception mentioned in the matn applies to the buyer's oath, not to tahaluf. The meaning of the speech is that the two parties to a sale do not perform tahaluf according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), and the statement is that of the buyer with his oath, unless the seller is satisfied to take the living (animal) and does not take from the price of the perished one more than what the buyer acknowledged. Then there is no oath on the buyer because the administration of the oath is legislated for the buyer only when he denies what the seller claims of excess. When the seller takes the living and abandons the claim of excess, and the buyer accepts that, there is no need to administer an oath to the buyer. 1] His saying: 'And not in the substitute of kitaba' means: if the master and the slave under kitaba dispute, there is no tahaluf according to Imam, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: they perform tahaluf and the kitaba is rescinded, and this is also the saying of al-Shafi'i for him. Because kitaba is a commutative contract that admits rescission, and each of them claims against the other; for the master claims an increased substitute and the slave denies it, while the slave claims entitlement to emancipation upon payment of what he acknowledges, and the master denies it. So they perform tahaluf, just as when they dispute over the price, and it is before delivery which is in accordance with qiyas, so it extends to it. And for Imam: The substitute in kitaba is exchanged for the removal of interdiction, which is the right of disposal and possession currently, and that has been granted to the slave, and he does not claim anything against his master. And it has been established that tahaluf after delivery is contrary to qiyas, so they do not perform tahaluf, and the statement is that of the slave because he is the denier. Rather, it becomes exchanged for emancipation at the time of payment, and before that it is not exchanged for it at all, so it is said about it: it is a dispute before delivery. And this is analogous to the hire which is exchanged for the hired property at the time of contract, then it transfers to the benefit at the time of utilization. And the difference between sale and kitaba is that sale is binding from both sides, so resorting to tahaluf in it is beneficial, because if one of them refuses, the claim of the other becomes binding. And that is not realized in kitaba because if the slave refuses, nothing becomes binding on him due to his ability to rescind by inability, and the debt in it is not binding, to the extent that suretyship for it is not permissible. The claim that it is an absolute commutative contract is not conceded because everything belongs to the master in one respect, so it is not equivalent to sale. And when tahaluf is absent, it necessitates considering the claim and denial, so the statement is of the denier, who is the slave. If one of them produces evidence, it is accepted and acted upon because it supports his claim. If both produce evidence, the master's evidence is preferred because it establishes the increase, except that when he pays the amount he proved, the slave is freed because he proved freedom for himself upon payment of that amount, so acceptance of his evidence is necessary. This is clearly stated by him and others.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on tahaluf in kitaba. It begins by clarifying a previous point about the buyer's oath in a sale dispute. Then it moves to the main issue: the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether tahaluf occurs when the master and the slave under kitaba dispute the amount due. The Sahibayn, along with al-Shafi'i, hold that they do perform tahaluf, because kitaba is a commutative contract that can be rescinded, and each party claims against the other—the master claims more, the slave claims less and also claims eventual emancipation. They analogize it to a sale dispute before delivery, which follows qiyas. The Imam, however, argues that there is no tahaluf because the substitute in kitaba is not equivalent to the price in sale; it is given for the removal of restraint (fakk al-hajr) which allows the slave to trade, and the slave currently does not claim anything from the master. Moreover, tahaluf after delivery is already an exception to qiyas, so it is not extended to kitaba. The slave is the denier, so his statement is taken. Furthermore, the slave can always rescind the contract by declaring inability, so tahaluf would not be binding. Thus, the key difference is that sale is binding from both sides, making tahaluf meaningful, while kitaba is not fully binding. The passage then concludes with the evidence rules when there is no tahaluf: if no evidence, the slave's statement is taken; if one party has evidence, it is accepted; if both have evidence, the master's evidence is preferred because it proves an increase, but the slave's evidence that he paid the agreed amount frees him. This completes the kitaba discussion.

Key Terms
البدل (al-badal): The substitute or consideration in a kitaba contract; the amount to be paid by the slave for freedom.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master to pay a sum for his freedom.
التحالف (al-tahaluf): A procedure where both parties in a dispute take oaths; if both swear, the contract is rescinded.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or delivery of the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The master's statement is accepted with his oath.
C) The slave's statement is accepted with his oath, and no tahaluf occurs.
D) The contract is automatically rescinded without oaths.

Key Takeaway
In kitaba, no tahaluf according to Imam Abu Hanifa; the slave's statement with oath is accepted, and evidence rules favor the master if both have proof, but the slave's payment evidence frees him.

Resolving Disputes Over Household Goods: Each Spouse Gets What Suits Them
Ruling on dispute between spouses over household goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on disputes over household goods to determine which items belong to the wife and which to the husband, based on customary suitability.

Core Idea
In a dispute, each spouse gets what customarily suits them; the husband's word prevails for the rest.

When spouses disagree over who owns household items, the Hanafi school provides a practical rule: each gets what is suitable for their use. This avoids endless quarrels and is grounded in custom ('urf). The commentary expands the scope to all couples and defines 'mataa' as anything beneficial, including gold and silver.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its basis
The base text says: 'for the wife is what is suitable for her.' The husband gets what is suitable for him. This is based on the presumption that the husband owns the household goods, but custom dictates that certain items are specifically for the wife.
The ruling applies whether they agree or disagree, during marriage or after.
2
Identify the scope of the ruling
The commentary states that 'spouses' includes all couples: Muslim or dhimmi, free or slave, adult or minor (if the minor can have intercourse). It also covers disputes during marriage and after separation, and whether the house is owned by both or one.
Exception: if one spouse is free and the other a slave, all goods go to the free one during life, and to the living one after death.
3
Define the subject matter: 'mataa'
'Mataa' means anything beneficial, from food and furnishings to gold and silver. The commentary clarifies that it includes all movable property in the house, regardless of value.
Real estate is also included, but the ruling focuses on movables.
4
Apply the principle to examples
The wife gets items like head covering (khimar), dress (dir'), anklet (khalkhal), and jewelry. The husband gets items like turbans, shirts, and tools. The division is based on what each typically uses.
If an item could be used by both (e.g., a blanket), the husband's word is taken because the house is considered his domain.
5
Determine the procedural outcome
When the spouses dispute, the qadi (judge) asks each to swear an oath. The wife takes an oath for items suitable for her, and the husband for the rest. If one refuses, the other gets the item.
This rule prevents chaos and is a mercy from the Shari'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزوجان في متاع البيت، فلها ما صلح لها
قوله : الزوجان ؛ أطلق الزوجين، فشمل المسلمين، والمسلم مع الذمية، والحريّن، والمملوكين، والمكاتبين، والزوجين الكبيرين، والصغيرين إذا كان الصغير يجامع.
وأما إذا كان أحدهما حرَّاً والآخرُ مملوكاً ، فالكل للحر في الحياة، وللحي بعد الموت، كما سيأتي، وشمل اختلافهما حالَ بقاء النكاح، وما بعد الفرقة، وما إذا كان البيت ملكاً لهم، أو لأحدهما خاصة. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
قوله : في متاع البيت ؛ أي فيما ينتفع به من نفسه أو مما حصل منه كالعقار وغيره، والمتاعُ في اللغة كما في «المصباح» : كلُّ ما ينتفع به كالطعام والبر وأثاث البيت، وأصله ما يُتَبَلَّغ به من الزاد، وهو اسم من منعته بالتثقيل إذا أعطيته ذلك ، والجمع أمتعة، والمراد بالمتاع هنا : ما كان في البيت ولو ذهباً أو فضة، صرح به في «المنح»، وغيره.
قوله : فلها ما صلح لها ؛ كالخمارِ والدرع والخلخال والحلي، فالقول فيها
The two spouses regarding the household goods: for her is what is suitable for her. He said: 'the two spouses' – he included Muslims, a Muslim with a dhimmi, two free persons, two slaves, two mukatabs, two adults, and two minors if the minor engages in intercourse. As for when one is free and the other a slave, then everything belongs to the free one during life, and to the living one after death, as will come. It includes their dispute during the marriage and after separation, and whether the house is owned by them or by one of them. So in 'Al-Bahr', etc. He said: 'in the household goods' – i.e., regarding what is used for benefit itself or from what is obtained from it, like real estate and others. 'Mata' in language, as in 'Al-Misbah', is everything that is used for benefit, like food, grain, and household furnishings. Its origin is what one sustains oneself with from provisions. It is a noun from 'mana'tahu' with tashdeed when you give him that. The plural is 'amti'ah'. What is meant by 'mata' here is whatever is in the house, even if gold or silver, as stated in 'Al-Minh', etc. He said: 'for her is what is suitable for her' – like the head covering, shift, anklet, and jewelry, so the word is hers
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Wiqayah, commented upon by the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The ruling addresses a common dispute between spouses over the ownership of household items when they disagree. According to the Hanafi school, each spouse is given what is suitable for their use: the wife gets items like headscarves, dresses, anklets, and jewelry, while the husband gets items like turbans, shirts, and tools. The commentary clarifies that the ruling applies broadly to all couples, regardless of religion, freedom status, or whether the marriage is ongoing or ended, as long as the house is attributed to the husband (or the wife in some cases). The commentator also defines 'mata' (household goods) as anything usable, including gold and silver. The key principle is that the house is considered the husband's domain, so his word is taken for general items unless they are specifically suited to the wife. This ruling prevents endless quarrels and is based on custom (urf) and necessity.

Key Terms
متاع (mataa'): Household goods and furnishings, including movable property like clothes, utensils, and even gold/silver found in the house.
صلح (saluha): Suitable or appropriate for someone's use according to customary practice (urf).
الزوجان (al-zawjaan): The husband and wife, including valid and invalid marriages according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The jewelry is divided equally between them
B) The jewelry goes to the wife because it is suitable for her
C) The jewelry goes to the husband because he bought it
D) The jewelry is given to the poor as charity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling resolves spousal disputes over household goods by assigning each item to the spouse for whom it is customarily suitable, with the husband's word prevailing for ambiguous items.

Dispute Over Household Goods: The Hanafi Rule of Ascription by Suitability
Judicial rule for spousal dispute over household goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of 'al-qawl bi-mā yaṣluḥ' to determine which spouse's claim is accepted in a dispute over household goods and identify the exceptions.

Core Idea
Household goods go to the spouse they suit, but custom on the wedding night overrides the default.

What happens when a husband and wife disagree over ownership of the furniture, jewelry, or even the money in their home? Without evidence, the judge cannot resort to guesswork. The Hanafi school provides a clear default: the apparent suitability of the item indicates its owner. This lesson unpacks the central ruling of 'Umda al-Ri'aya' and its practical exceptions, including the crucial role of custom on the wedding night.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine that the dispute is between spouses, over items in the house, and that neither has evidence (bayyinah). The rule applies only when both claim ownership and no proof exists.
2
Classify the item by suitability
Ask: is the item typically used by men (e.g., turban, sword), women (e.g., veil, jewelry), or both (e.g., bed, furniture)? The answer decides whose word is accepted.
The 'apparent circumstance' (zāhir) guides the judge.
3
Apply the default rule
Items that suit only the wife go to her word; items that suit only the husband go to his word; items that suit both go to the husband's word, because the wife's possession is considered derivative of the husband's.
This is the foundational principle from the matn.
4
Check for exceptions
Look for special circumstances: if one spouse is a professional selling such items, the default flips; if the dispute is between landlord and tenant, the tenant's word is taken; if the item is not 'household goods' (e.g., cobbler's tools), it is divided equally.
These are derived from the commentator's expansions.
5
Consider the wedding night exception
On the wedding night, custom (ʿurf) gives the furnishings, women's jewelry, etc., to the wife, even if they suit both. This is the muftā bihī (operative fatwa), overriding the default.
Al-Akmal and al-Bahr rely on prevalent custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف الزوجان في متاع البيت، فلها ما صلح لها وله ما صلح له أو لهما وللاحتراز عن إسكاف وعطار، اختلفا في آلة الأساكفة وآلة العطارين، وهي في أيديهما، فإنه يقضي بها بينهما، ولا ينظر إلى ما يصلح لأحدهما ؛ لأنه قد يتخذ لنفسه أو للبيع، فلا يصلح مرجحاً. وللاحتراز عما إذا اختلف المؤجُرُ والمستأجرُ في متاع البيت، فإن القول فيه للمستأجر ؛ لكون البيت مضافاً إليه بالسكني. وللاحتراز عن اختلاف الزوجين في غير متاع البيت، وكان في أيديهما، فإنهما كالأجنبيين يقسم بينهما. كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره). [1] قوله : الزوجان ؛ أطلق الزوجين، فشمل المسلمين، والمسلم مع الذمية، والحريّن، والمملوكين، والمكاتبين، والزوجين الكبيرين، والصغيرين إذا كان الصغير يجامع. وأما إذا كان أحدهما حرَّاً والآخرُ مملوكاً ، فالكل للحر في الحياة، وللحي بعد الموت، كما سيأتي، وشمل اختلافهما حالَ بقاء النكاح، وما بعد الفرقة، وما إذا كان البيت ملكاً لهم، أو لأحدهما خاصة. كذا في «البحر» (۳)، وغيره. [٢] قوله : في متاع البيت ؛ أي فيما ينتفع به من نفسه أو مما حصل منه كالعقار وغيره، والمتاعُ في اللغة كما في «المصباح» : كلُّ ما ينتفع به كالطعام والبر وأثاث البيت، وأصله ما يُتَبَلَّغ به من الزاد، وهو اسم من منعته بالتثقيل إذا أعطيته ذلك ، والجمع أمتعة، والمراد بالمتاع هنا : ما كان في البيت ولو ذهباً أو فضة، صرح به في «المنح»، وغيره. [٣] قوله : فلها ما صلح لها ؛ كالخمارِ والدرع والخلخال والحلي، فالقول فيها قولُ المرأة؛ لأنَّ الظاهر شاهد لها، إلا إذا كان الرجلُ يصنع ، أو يبيع هذه الأشياء، ذكر الطحطاوي (۱) نقلاً . في (۱) نقلاً عن «البدائع» : محلُّ كون القول لها إذا لم تقر المرأةُ أنَّ هذا المتاع اشتراه ، فإن أقرت بذلك سقط قولها؛ لأنها أقرت بأن الملك للزوج ، ثم ادعت الانتقال إليها، فلا يثبت الانتقال إلا بالبينة انتهى. وكذا إذا ادعت أنها اشترته منه مثلاً ، فلا بُدَّ من بينة علي الانتقال إليها منه بهبة أو نحو ذلك، ولا يكون استمتاعها بمشتريه ورضاه بذلك دليلاً على أنه ملكها، ذلك كما تفهمه النساء والعوام، قال في «البحر» (۳) : وقد أفتيتُ بذلك مراراً. انتهى. [١]قوله : وله ما صلح له أو لهما ؛ أما ما صلح له كالعمامة والقلنسوة والطيلسان والسلاح والمنطقة والكتب، فالقولُ فيها قولُ الزوج مع اليمين؛ لأنَّ الظاهر شاهد له إلا إذا كانت المرأة تبيع هذه الأشياء. وأما ما صلح لهما كالفرش والأمتعة والأواني والرقيق والعقار والمنزل والمواشي والنقود، فالقول فيها أيضاً للزوج؛ لأنَّ المرأة وما في يدها في يد الزوج، فكأَنَّ الأموال كلها في يد الزوج ، وشمل كلامه ما إذا كان في ليلة الزفاف، فيكون القول له. لكن قال الأكمل في «الخزانة»: لو ماتت المرأة في ليلتها التي زُفّت إليه في بيته لا يستحسن أن يجعل متاعُ الفرش وحلي النساء وما يليقُ بهن للزوج ، إلا أن يكون الرجلُ معروفاً بتجارة جنس منهما، فهو له. انتهى. وكذلك إذا اختلفا حال الحياة فيما يصلح لهما ، فالقولُ له إلا إذا كان الاختلاف ليلة الزفاف، فالقول لها في الفرش ونحوها ؛ لجريان العرف غالباً أنَّ الفرش والصناديق والخدم تأتي به المرأة، ينبغي اعتماده للفتوى، إلا أن يوجد نص في حكمِهِ ليلة الزفاف بخلافه فيتبع. كذا في «البحر» [٢] قوله : ولا بيّنة لأحدهما ؛ قيَّد به لأنَّه إن كانت لأحدهما بينة فيقضى بما يثبت بها.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath. The two spouses regarding the goods of the house: to her belongs what suits her, and to him belongs what suits him or both. And to avoid the case of a cobbler and a perfumer who dispute over the tools of cobblers and tools of perfumers while they are in their hands: then it is decided between them equally, and no consideration is given to what suits one of them because he may have taken it for himself or for sale, so it does not serve as a preponderant. And to avoid the case when the lessor and lessee dispute over the goods of the house: then the statement is that of the lessee because the house is attributed to him by tenancy. And to avoid the dispute of spouses over other than household goods while in their hands: then they are like strangers, and it is divided between them. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. [1] His statement: 'the two spouses' – he used the general term 'spouses', so it includes Muslims, a Muslim with a dhimmi woman, two free persons, two slaves, two mukatabs, two adult spouses, and two minors if the minor can have intercourse. But if one is free and the other is a slave, then everything belongs to the free one during life, and to the living after death, as will come. And it includes their dispute during the continuance of marriage, after separation, and whether the house is owned by them or by one of them specifically. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. [2] His statement: 'regarding the goods of the house' – that is, what is used for benefit from itself or what is obtained from it like real estate and other things. 'Mataa'' in language as in 'al-Misbah' is everything that is benefited from, like food, wheat, and house furnishings; its origin is what one sustains oneself with from provisions, and it is a noun derived from 'mana'tahu' with tashkil meaning 'I gave him that'; the plural is 'amti'ah'. What is meant by 'mataa'' here is what is in the house, even if gold or silver, as explicitly stated in 'al-Minah' and others. [3] His statement: 'to her belongs what suits her' – like the khimar, the shift, the anklet, and jewelry; so the statement is hers because the apparent circumstance witnesses for her, unless the man manufactures or sells these things. Al-Tahtawi mentioned, quoting from 'al-Bada'i': the condition for her statement to be accepted is that she does not confess that the husband bought this goods; if she confesses that, her statement falls because she has admitted that ownership belongs to the husband, then she claims transfer to herself, so transfer cannot be proven except by evidence (bayyinah). Similarly, if she claims that she bought it from him, for example, evidence is necessary for transfer to her from him by gift or the like; her enjoyment of his purchase and his satisfaction with that is not evidence that it is her property, as women and common folk understand. Al-Bahr said: I have given fatwa to that effect many times. End. [1] His statement: 'and to him belongs what suits him or for both' – as for what suits him like the turban, cap, taylasan, weapon, belt, and books: then the statement is that of the husband with his oath because the apparent circumstance witnesses for him, unless the woman sells these things. As for what suits both like beds, furnishings, vessels, slaves, real estate, the house, livestock, and money: then the statement is also that of the husband because the woman and what is in her hand are in the hand of the husband, so it is as if all the wealth is in the hand of the husband. And his statement includes if it is on the wedding night; then the statement is his. But al-Akmal said in 'al-Khizanah': if the woman dies on the night she was brought to his house, it is not considered good (yastahsanu) to make the furnishings, women's jewelry, and what befits them belong to the husband, unless the man is known to trade in that type; then it is his. End. Similarly, if they dispute during life regarding what suits both, then the statement is his unless the dispute is on the wedding night; then the statement is hers regarding the bed and the like, because customarily the beds, chests, and servants come with the woman. This should be relied upon for fatwa, unless there is a text ruling otherwise on the wedding night, then it is followed. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His statement: 'and there is no evidence (bayyinah) for either of them' – he qualified with this because if there is evidence for one, then judgment is given according to what it proves.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' explains the rule when spouses dispute over household goods (متاع البيت) without evidence. The base ruling is that goods are assigned by custom: women get what suits them (e.g., khimar, dress, jewelry), and men get what suits them (e.g., turban, sword) or both (e.g., bedding, money). The commentator notes that this applies generally to all spouses, free or slave, Muslim or dhimmi, etc. But exceptions exist: if one spouse is a professional (e.g., cobbler) and the tools are in both hands, it's divided equally. In rental disputes, the tenant's word is taken. Disputes over non-household goods are like strangers. The key point is that the default is based on الظاهر (apparent circumstance); if a woman admits husband bought it, she needs evidence for transfer. For goods suitable for both, the husband's statement prevails because he is the head of household. However, on the wedding night, custom (عرف) gives the furnishings to the wife, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. The ruling requires no evidence; if evidence exists, it decides.

Key Terms
متاع البيت (mataa' al-bayt): All items in the house used for benefit, including furnishings, vessels, money, gold, silver, etc.
القول قول من (al-qawl qawl man): The legal presumption of whose claim is accepted in court due to apparent evidence (zahir) or default assumption.
ظاهر (zahir): The apparent circumstance or external indication that the law uses as evidence for one party's claim in the absence of proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband and wife dispute over a bed in their house, and it is their wedding night, whose statement is accepted?
A) The husband's, because he owns the house.
B) The wife's, because customarily the bed comes with the bride.
C) It is divided equally between them.
D) The one who brings evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for spousal disputes over household goods is based on apparent suitability, with custom on the wedding night overriding the default for furnishings.
