Hanafi Internal Dispute: Goods Suitable for Both Spouses
Explains Hanafi internal differences on al-mushkil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding household goods suitable for both sexes after a spouse's death, and explain the rational evidence (dalil aqli) for each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, the hand (possession) of the surviving spouse outweighs the mere suitability of goods.

When a spouse dies and household goods could be used by either gender, who gets them? The three senior Hanafi jurists gave different answers, each based on rational principles of possession, custom, and status. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed category
Recognise that the dispute concerns goods suitable for both men and women (al-mushkil), not goods specific to one gender.
Goods suitable for one gender are uncontested: they go to that spouse.
2
State each Hanafi position
List the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Note: Abu Hanifa gives all to the living spouse with an oath; Abu Yusuf gives the wife her trousseau amount; Muhammad gives all to the husband (or his heirs).
These are the three main views within the school.
3
Describe each position's evidence
Associate each view with its rational basis: priority of hand (Abu Hanifa), custom of trousseau (Abu Yusuf), and husband's guardianship (Muhammad).
All use dalil aqli – rational arguments.
4
Compare the bases
Analyse how each imam weighs possession, custom, and marital status. Abu Hanifa prioritises the living spouse's hand; Abu Yusuf favours custom; Muhammad prioritises the husband's role.
Contrast the justifications.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Note that the commentary states 'this is the school of the Imam', meaning Abu Hanifa's view is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Later Hanafi works adopt this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مات أحدهما ، فالمشكل للحي (وإن مات أحدهما ، فالمشكل للحي) ، المراد بالمشكل ما يصلح للرجال والنساء، فهو للحي مع يمينه هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وقال أبو يوسف : يدفعُ إلى المرأة ما يُجهز به مثلها، والباقي للزوج مع يمينه، والحياة والموتُ سواء لقيام الورثة مقام المورث ، وعند محمد : إن كانا حيَّيْنِ فكما قال أبو حنيفة ان الله ، وبعد الموت ما يصلح لهما لورثةِ الزَّوج.
[١] قوله : وإن مات... الخ ؛ تفصيلُ المسألة : إنَّ أحد الزوجين إذا مات واختلف الحي منهما مع ورثة الآخر، فإن كان المتاعُ مما يصلح للرجال والنساء فهو للحي، وإن كان مما يصلح لأحدهما فقط، فهو على ما كان قبل الموت، وتقوم ورثته فيه، وهذا مذهب الإمام. وقال أبو يوسف الله : يدفع إلى المرأة مما يصلح للرجال والنساء مما يجهز به مثلُها ، والباقي للزوج مع يمينه ، ولورثته بعد موته. وقال محمد ه : مثل ما قال أبو حنيفة له : إنَّ ما يصلح لأحدهما فهو له، وما يصلح لهما فهو للزوج إلا أن قوله هذا لا يختلف بين أن يكون في حياتهما وبعد موت أحدهما.
والحاصل : إنهم اتفقوا أنَّ ما يصلح لأحدهما فهو لمـن يـصـلـحُ له في الحياة والموت، حتى تقوم ورثته مقامه، واختلفوا فيما يصلح لهما، فالإمام جعله للزوج في حال حياتهما، وللباقي منهما بعد موت أحدهما.
ومحمد ه : جعله للزوج في كلتا الحالتين.
وأبو يوسف : جعل منه للمرأة قدر ما يجهز به مثلُها في الحالتين ؛ لأنها تأتي بالجهاز عادة، فكان الظاهر شاهداً لها، ا، وهو أقوى من ظاهر يد الزوج بجريان العادة بذلك، فيبطل به ظاهر يد الزوج ، ولا معارض في الباقي بظاهرِهِ فيعتبر لقوة يده عليها ؛ لأنه قوام عليها. ولهما : في الاستواء بين الحالتين أنَّ الورثة يقومون مقام الميت ؛ لأنهم خلفاؤه فيما له، فكما أن المشكل له في حياته مع يمينه ، فكذا كان القول لورثته. وللإمام: إن يد الباقي منهما أسبق إلى المتاع ؛ لأن الوارث ثبتت يده بعد موت المورث، فيقع به الترجيح كما يقعُ به الصلاحية للاستعمال بل أولى؛ لأنَّ لليد رجحاناً مطلقاً حتى يرجح به في غير هذا الباب، بخلاف الصلاحية ؛ ولأنَّ يد الباقي منهما يد نفسه ، ويد الوارث خلف عن يد المورث فلا يعارض الأصل.
وعنه :
وقال زفره : المشكل بينهما نصفان والباقي مثل ما قال أبو حنيفة له ، إنَّ المتاع كله بينهما نصفين، وبه قال مالك والشافعي ؛ لأنهما استويا في سبب الاستحقاق، إذ هما ساكنان في بيت واحد، والبيت مع ما فيه في أيديهما، ولا اعتبار بالشبهة في الخصومات وكذا لو اختلف إسكافٌ وعطار في آلة الأساكفة وآلة العطارين، وهي في أيديهما قضى بينهما نصفين، ولا ينظر إلى ما يصلح لأحدهما ؛ لأنه قد يتخذه لنفسه أو للبيع، فلا يصلح مرجحاً.
وقال الحسن البصري له : إنَّ المتاع كله للمرأة، وليس للرجل إلا ما عليه من ثياب بدنه ؛ لأنَّ المرأة هي الساكنة فيه، ويد صاحب البيت على ما في البيت أقـوى وأظهر من يد غيره ، وفي الدعاوي يقدم صاحب اليد.
وقال ابن أبي ليلى الله له : إنَّ المتاع كله للزوج ، وكيف ما كان؛ لأنَّ المرأة في يدِ الزوج، فما في البيت أيضاً يكون في يده، وإن كان البيت لها ، ألا ترى أنه صاحب البيت، وأنَّ البيت يضاف إليه، فصار بمنزلة المؤجر مع المستأجر، وإذا اختلفا في متاع المنزل فإنَّ القول للمستأجر ؛ لكونه مضافاً إليه بالسكنى وليس للمؤجر سوى ما عليه من ثياب بدنه، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ (١) ، وغيره، فهذه المسألة هى المُسَبَّعة، فتذكرها وتشكّر.
And if one of them dies, then the problematic (goods) are for the living. (And if one of them dies, then the problematic (goods) are for the living). What is meant by 'problematic' is that which is suitable for men and women. So it is for the living with his oath. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He gives to the woman what equips her like her, and the rest is for the husband with his oath, and life and death are the same because the heirs stand in the place of the deceased. According to Muhammad: If both are alive, then as Abu Hanifa said, and after death, what is suitable for both is for the heirs of the husband. [1] His saying: 'And if one dies...' etc.; the detail of the issue: When one of the spouses dies and the living one differs with the heirs of the other, if the goods are suitable for men and women, then they are for the living; if they are suitable for only one of them, then it is as it was before death, and his heirs stand in his place. This is the Imam's school. Abu Yusuf said: He gives to the woman from that which is suitable for men and women what equips her like her, and the rest is for the husband with his oath, and for his heirs after his death. Muhammad said: Like what Abu Hanifa said: What is suitable for one of them is for that person, and what is suitable for both is for the husband, except that his statement does not differ between during their lives and after the death of one of them. The summary: They agreed that what is suitable for one of them is for the one for whom it is suitable in life and death, so his heirs stand in his place. They differed regarding what is suitable for both. The Imam made it for the husband during their lives, and for the surviving one after the death of one of them. Muhammad made it for the husband in both cases. Abu Yusuf made from it for the woman the extent that equips her like her in both cases, because she usually brings the trousseau, so the apparent evidence testifies for her, and it is stronger than the apparent evidence of the husband's hand due to the custom, so it invalidates the apparent evidence of the husband's hand. There is no opponent in the rest by his apparent evidence, so it is considered due to the strength of his hand over her because he is her guardian. And for both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding equality between the two cases: the heirs stand in the place of the deceased because they are his successors in what he owns, so just as the problematic is for him during his life with his oath, so the statement was for his heirs. For the Imam: The hand of the surviving one is earlier to the goods because the heir's hand is established after the death of the deceased, so it serves as a preference just as suitability for use does, rather it is more appropriate, because the hand has absolute preference even in other chapters, unlike suitability. And because the hand of the surviving one is his own hand, while the hand of the heir is a substitute for the hand of the deceased, so it does not oppose the original. And from him: Zufar said: The problematic is divided between them in halves, and the rest is as Abu Hanifa said: all the goods are between them in halves. This was also said by Malik and al-Shafi'i, because they are equal in the cause of entitlement, since both are residents in one house, and the house with what is in it is in their hands, and there is no consideration of similarity in lawsuits. Similarly, if a shoemaker and a perfume seller differ regarding the tools of shoemakers and the tools of perfume sellers, and they are in their hands, he judges between them in halves, and no consideration is given to what is suitable for one of them, because he may have taken it for himself or for sale, so it does not serve as a differentiator. Al-Hasan al-Basri said: All the goods are for the woman, and the man has nothing except the clothes on his body, because the woman is the resident in it, and the hand of the owner of the house over what is in the house is stronger and more apparent than the hand of others, and in lawsuits the possessor is given precedence. Ibn Abi Layla said: All the goods are for the husband, however it may be, because the woman is under the hand of the husband, so what is in the house is also under his hand, even if the house is for her. Do you not see that he is the owner of the house and the house is attributed to him, so he becomes like the lessor with the lessee. And if they differ regarding the furniture of the house, then the statement of the lessee is accepted because it is attributed to him by residence, and the lessor has only the clothes on his body. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned this. This is the musabb'a (seven-issue) case, so remember it and be grateful.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage explains the dispute between spouses or their heirs after the death of one spouse regarding household goods that could be used by either men or women (al-mushkil). The Hanafi school has internal differences: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that all such goods go to the surviving spouse with an oath because his or her physical possession is prior and stronger than the deceased's heirs' possession. Abu Yusuf says the wife receives enough for her trousseau up to the standard of her like, and the rest goes to the husband with an oath, reasoning that the wife customarily brings the trousseau so the evidence (zahir) supports her. Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa that goods suitable for one spouse go to that spouse, but goods suitable for both go to the husband (in their lifetimes) and to his heirs after his death. The commentary summarizes that all agree on goods suitable for one spouse; the dispute is only over goods suitable for both. Abu Hanifa's proof: the hand of the surviving spouse is earlier because the heir's hand only arises after death, and the hand has a stronger evidentiary weight than mere suitability. Then the passage presents the views of other scholars: Zufar, Malik, and al-Shafi'i hold that all goods are divided in half because both spouses were equally resident and had equal access; al-Hasan al-Basri gives all to the wife as the resident; Ibn Abi Layla gives all to the husband as the one in charge. The passage closes by noting that this is the famous 'musabb'a' (seven-issue) case, likely referring to a set of seven similar disputes.

Key Terms
المشكل (al-mushkil): Household goods that could be used by either men or women, making ownership disputed.
يد (yad): Physical possession or control; a legal indicator of ownership in disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Nothing – possession is enough
B) Provide witnesses that the goods were purchased during marriage
C) Swear an oath that the goods are not from the deceased's separate property
D) Prove the goods were used by both

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the living spouse gets al-mushkil goods with an oath because their hand is prior to the heir's.

Cross-Madhhab Dispute: Seven Opinions on Shared Goods
Compares multiple schools' rulings on al-mushkil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) with those of Zufar, Malik, al-Shafi'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, and Ibn Abi Layla on the same issue, and evaluate the Hanafi tarjih.

Core Idea
The famous 'musabb'a' (seven opinions) on this issue shows that possession (yad) is a key principle distinguishing Hanafi from other schools.

The dispute over household goods after a spouse's death generated at least seven distinct opinions across early schools. This lesson maps each position and its rational evidence, then explains the Hanafi response that leads to the reliance on Abu Hanifa's view.

Guided Path
1
List all seven opinions
Identify the seven views mentioned: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar/Malik/Shafi'i (splitting), al-Hasan al-Basri (all to wife), Ibn Abi Layla (all to husband).
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are also counted among the seven; the passage calls this 'musabb'a'.
2
Categorize by underlying principle
Group views by key principle: possession (Abu Hanifa, al-Hasan, Ibn Abi Layla), custom (Abu Yusuf), guardianship (Muhammad), equal residence (Zufar/Malik/Shafi'i).
Each principle leads to a different outcome.
3
Identify each school's key evidence
State the main dalil for each view: Abu Hanifa uses priority of hand; Zufar uses equal residence; al-Hasan uses wife's residence; Ibn Abi Layla uses husband's control over the house.
All use rational (aqli) arguments.
4
Apply Hanafi critique
Explain the Hanafi rejection of the other views: hand is absolute even over equal residence; custom is subordinate; the wife's residence is not possession; the husband's control does not extend after death.
The commentary provides the Imams's response.
5
Summarize thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical difference: in Hanafi courts, the living spouse takes all; in Shafi'i/Maliki courts, it is halved; under al-Hasan, wife takes all; under Ibn Abi Layla, husband takes all.
This shows the impact of different usul principles.

وإن مات أحدهما ، فالمشكل للحي (وإن مات أحدهما ، فالمشكل للحي) ، المراد بالمشكل ما يصلح للرجال والنساء، فهو للحي مع يمينه هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وقال أبو يوسف : يدفعُ إلى المرأة ما يُجهز به مثلها، والباقي للزوج مع يمينه، والحياة والموتُ سواء لقيام الورثة مقام المورث ، وعند محمد : إن كانا حيَّيْنِ فكما قال أبو حنيفة ان الله ، وبعد الموت ما يصلح لهما لورثةِ الزَّوج.
[١] قوله : وإن مات... الخ ؛ تفصيلُ المسألة : إنَّ أحد الزوجين إذا مات واختلف الحي منهما مع ورثة الآخر، فإن كان المتاعُ مما يصلح للرجال والنساء فهو للحي، وإن كان مما يصلح لأحدهما فقط، فهو على ما كان قبل الموت، وتقوم ورثته فيه، وهذا مذهب الإمام. وقال أبو يوسف الله : يدفع إلى المرأة مما يصلح للرجال والنساء مما يجهز به مثلُها ، والباقي للزوج مع يمينه ، ولورثته بعد موته. وقال محمد ه : مثل ما قال أبو حنيفة له : إنَّ ما يصلح لأحدهما فهو له، وما يصلح لهما فهو للزوج إلا أن قوله هذا لا يختلف بين أن يكون في حياتهما وبعد موت أحدهما.
والحاصل : إنهم اتفقوا أنَّ ما يصلح لأحدهما فهو لمـن يـصـلـحُ له في الحياة والموت، حتى تقوم ورثته مقامه، واختلفوا فيما يصلح لهما، فالإمام جعله للزوج في حال حياتهما، وللباقي منهما بعد موت أحدهما.
ومحمد ه : جعله للزوج في كلتا الحالتين.
وأبو يوسف : جعل منه للمرأة قدر ما يجهز به مثلُها في الحالتين ؛ لأنها تأتي بالجهاز عادة، فكان الظاهر شاهداً لها، ا، وهو أقوى من ظاهر يد الزوج بجريان العادة بذلك، فيبطل به ظاهر يد الزوج ، ولا معارض في الباقي بظاهرِهِ فيعتبر لقوة يده عليها ؛ لأنه قوام عليها. ولهما : في الاستواء بين الحالتين أنَّ الورثة يقومون مقام الميت ؛ لأنهم خلفاؤه فيما له، فكما أن المشكل له في حياته مع يمينه ، فكذا كان القول لورثته. وللإمام: إن يد الباقي منهما أسبق إلى المتاع ؛ لأن الوارث ثبتت يده بعد موت المورث، فيقع به الترجيح كما يقعُ به الصلاحية للاستعمال بل أولى؛ لأنَّ لليد رجحاناً مطلقاً حتى يرجح به في غير هذا الباب، بخلاف الصلاحية ؛ ولأنَّ يد الباقي منهما يد نفسه ، ويد الوارث خلف عن يد المورث فلا يعارض الأصل.
وعنه :
وقال زفره : المشكل بينهما نصفان والباقي مثل ما قال أبو حنيفة له ، إنَّ المتاع كله بينهما نصفين، وبه قال مالك والشافعي ؛ لأنهما استويا في سبب الاستحقاق، إذ هما ساكنان في بيت واحد، والبيت مع ما فيه في أيديهما، ولا اعتبار بالشبهة في الخصومات وكذا لو اختلف إسكافٌ وعطار في آلة الأساكفة وآلة العطارين، وهي في أيديهما قضى بينهما نصفين، ولا ينظر إلى ما يصلح لأحدهما ؛ لأنه قد يتخذه لنفسه أو للبيع، فلا يصلح مرجحاً.
وقال الحسن البصري له : إنَّ المتاع كله للمرأة، وليس للرجل إلا ما عليه من ثياب بدنه ؛ لأنَّ المرأة هي الساكنة فيه، ويد صاحب البيت على ما في البيت أقـوى وأظهر من يد غيره ، وفي الدعاوي يقدم صاحب اليد.
وقال ابن أبي ليلى الله له : إنَّ المتاع كله للزوج ، وكيف ما كان؛ لأنَّ المرأة في يدِ الزوج، فما في البيت أيضاً يكون في يده، وإن كان البيت لها ، ألا ترى أنه صاحب البيت، وأنَّ البيت يضاف إليه، فصار بمنزلة المؤجر مع المستأجر، وإذا اختلفا في متاع المنزل فإنَّ القول للمستأجر ؛ لكونه مضافاً إليه بالسكنى وليس للمؤجر سوى ما عليه من ثياب بدنه، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ (١) ، وغيره، فهذه المسألة هى المُسَبَّعة، فتذكرها وتشكّر.
And if one of them dies, then the problematic (goods) are for the living. (And if one of them dies, then the problematic (goods) are for the living). What is meant by 'problematic' is that which is suitable for men and women. So it is for the living with his oath. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He gives to the woman what equips her like her, and the rest is for the husband with his oath, and life and death are the same because the heirs stand in the place of the deceased. According to Muhammad: If both are alive, then as Abu Hanifa said, and after death, what is suitable for both is for the heirs of the husband. [1] His saying: 'And if one dies...' etc.; the detail of the issue: When one of the spouses dies and the living one differs with the heirs of the other, if the goods are suitable for men and women, then they are for the living; if they are suitable for only one of them, then it is as it was before death, and his heirs stand in his place. This is the Imam's school. Abu Yusuf said: He gives to the woman from that which is suitable for men and women what equips her like her, and the rest is for the husband with his oath, and for his heirs after his death. Muhammad said: Like what Abu Hanifa said: What is suitable for one of them is for that person, and what is suitable for both is for the husband, except that his statement does not differ between during their lives and after the death of one of them. The summary: They agreed that what is suitable for one of them is for the one for whom it is suitable in life and death, so his heirs stand in his place. They differed regarding what is suitable for both. The Imam made it for the husband during their lives, and for the surviving one after the death of one of them. Muhammad made it for the husband in both cases. Abu Yusuf made from it for the woman the extent that equips her like her in both cases, because she usually brings the trousseau, so the apparent evidence testifies for her, and it is stronger than the apparent evidence of the husband's hand due to the custom, so it invalidates the apparent evidence of the husband's hand. There is no opponent in the rest by his apparent evidence, so it is considered due to the strength of his hand over her because he is her guardian. And for both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding equality between the two cases: the heirs stand in the place of the deceased because they are his successors in what he owns, so just as the problematic is for him during his life with his oath, so the statement was for his heirs. For the Imam: The hand of the surviving one is earlier to the goods because the heir's hand is established after the death of the deceased, so it serves as a preference just as suitability for use does, rather it is more appropriate, because the hand has absolute preference even in other chapters, unlike suitability. And because the hand of the surviving one is his own hand, while the hand of the heir is a substitute for the hand of the deceased, so it does not oppose the original. And from him: Zufar said: The problematic is divided between them in halves, and the rest is as Abu Hanifa said: all the goods are between them in halves. This was also said by Malik and al-Shafi'i, because they are equal in the cause of entitlement, since both are residents in one house, and the house with what is in it is in their hands, and there is no consideration of similarity in lawsuits. Similarly, if a shoemaker and a perfume seller differ regarding the tools of shoemakers and the tools of perfume sellers, and they are in their hands, he judges between them in halves, and no consideration is given to what is suitable for one of them, because he may have taken it for himself or for sale, so it does not serve as a differentiator. Al-Hasan al-Basri said: All the goods are for the woman, and the man has nothing except the clothes on his body, because the woman is the resident in it, and the hand of the owner of the house over what is in the house is stronger and more apparent than the hand of others, and in lawsuits the possessor is given precedence. Ibn Abi Layla said: All the goods are for the husband, however it may be, because the woman is under the hand of the husband, so what is in the house is also under his hand, even if the house is for her. Do you not see that he is the owner of the house and the house is attributed to him, so he becomes like the lessor with the lessee. And if they differ regarding the furniture of the house, then the statement of the lessee is accepted because it is attributed to him by residence, and the lessor has only the clothes on his body. Al-Zayla'i and others mentioned this. This is the musabb'a (seven-issue) case, so remember it and be grateful.

Commentary
This passage explains the dispute between spouses or their heirs after the death of one spouse regarding household goods that could be used by either men or women (al-mushkil). Ruling When One Spouse is a Slave
States the Hanafi ruling on household goods when one spouse is a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-school dispute on possession of household goods when one spouse is a slave and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
For possession disputes between free and slave spouses, Abu Hanifa gives all to the free; the Sahibayn except authorized and mukatab slaves.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding household goods when one spouse is a slave. You will see how Abu Hanifa's general rule is qualified by his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who distinguish between ordinary slaves and those with a recognized legal capacity. Understanding this intra-madhhab ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to trace the reasoning behind diverging opinions.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's general position
Identify the rule: all household goods go to the free spouse during life, and to the surviving spouse after death, regardless of slave status.
This is based on the principle that a free person's hand is a hand of ownership, while a slave's hand is not.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict the general rule: the authorized slave (ma'dhun) and the mukatab are treated as free because they have a legally recognized hand in lawsuits.
The Sahibayn argue that these slaves have a form of legal capacity that ordinary slaves lack.
3
Follow the reasoning of each side
Abu Hanifa uses the absolute weakness of a slave's hand. The Sahibayn focus on the legal effects of the slave's authorization or mukatabah contract.
This is an aqli (rational) argument relying on the concept of yad (possession/control).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, any slave spouse loses all goods; under the Sahibayn, a ma'dhun or mukatab spouse keeps their share.
The dispute only concerns these two categories; ordinary slaves remain under the general rule.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the Sahibayn's view, as indicated by the commentary's emphasis on 'wa 'indahuma' (according to the two).
This tarjih is based on later Hanafi works that favor the Sahibayn's more nuanced approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان أحدهما عبداً، فالكل للحرّ في الحياة، وللحي بعد الموت (وإن كان أحدهما عبداً، فالكل للحرّ في الحياة، وللحي" بعد الموت)، وعندهما: العبد المأذون والمكاتب كالحر.
(1) قوله : فالكل للحر في الحياة ؛ لكون يد الحرِّ أقوى من يد المملوك ؛ لأن يده يدُ ملك، ويد المملوك ليست بيد ملك.
[٢]قوله : وللحي بعد الموت ؛ لأنَّ الميت لا بُدَّ له، فخلت يد الحي عن المعارض. [٣]قوله : وعندهما ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ هذا على إطلاقه قولُ أبي حنيفة ، وعند صاحبيه العبد المأذون له في التجارة، والمكاتب كالحرّ ؛ لأن لهما يداً معتبرة في الخصومات.
And if one of them is a slave, then all is for the free during life, and for the living after death. (And if one of them is a slave, then all is for the free during life, and for the living after death). And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the authorized slave and the mukatab are like the free. (1) His saying: 'All is for the free during life'; because the hand of the free person is stronger than the hand of the slave, because his hand is a hand of ownership, and the hand of the slave is not a hand of ownership. [2] His saying: 'And for the living after death'; because the dead has no hand, so the hand of the living is free of opposition. [3] His saying: 'And according to the two...' etc.; meaning that this is in its absolute sense the statement of Abu Hanifa, and according to his two companions, the slave authorized for trade and the mukatab are like the free, because they have a hand considered in lawsuits.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where one spouse is a slave. Abu Hanifa rules that all household goods go to the free spouse during their joint lifetime, and after the death of one spouse, all goods go to the surviving one (regardless of slave status). The reasoning is that the hand (possession) of a free person is a hand of ownership, while a slave's hand is not. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) introduce an exception: if the slave is a ma'dhun (authorized to trade) or a mukatab (contracted for manumission), then he/she is treated as free in this matter because they have a legally recognized hand in lawsuits. The commentary clarifies that Abu Hanifa's ruling applies generally, but the two companions restrict it to ordinary slaves.

Key Terms
العبد المأذون (al-'abd al-ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission, paying for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they have a higher social status
B) Because they have a hand that is recognized in lawsuits
C) Because they are on the verge of freedom
D) Because the master has delegated ownership to them

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an authorized slave (ma'dhun) or a mukatab slave is treated as free in possession disputes with a free spouse, though Abu Hanifa's general rule gives all to the free party.

Dispute Over Household Goods When One Spouse Is a Slave
Scholarly dispute on dividing household goods between spouses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on household goods disputes when one spouse is a slave, and evaluate the opposing schools' arguments.

Core Idea
In household goods disputes, the free spouse's hand prevails; a slave's hand is not fully independent.

This lesson examines a famous disputed issue known as 'al-mas'alah al-musabba'ah' (the sevenfold issue), where multiple scholars offer different rulings on dividing household goods when one spouse is a slave. The Hanafi position, defended by Abu Hanifa, emphasizes the strength of the free person's hand (yad) and the weakness of the slave's hand. Understanding this case reveals how Hanafi jurists weigh possession and suitability to resolve property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Recognize that the issue is about dividing household goods when spouses disagree and one is a slave. Abu Hanifa gives all to the free; others split equally or give all to one spouse.
Also note the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat authorized slaves and mukatab as free.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof based on yad
Abu Hanifa argues that the free person's hand is stronger because it is a hand of ownership, while the slave's hand is shared with the master. Suitability (salahiyyah) also gives preference.
Contrast with other lawsuits where suitability is not considered.
3
Examine the opposing views
Zufar (with Malik and Shafi'i) divides equally based on equal possession. Al-Hasan al-Basri gives all to the woman as occupant. Ibn Abi Layla gives all to the husband as head of household.
Each opposing view relies on a different weighting of residence, hand, or customary attribution.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The commentator defends Abu Hanifa by asserting that the slave's hand is not fully independent, and that suitability gives the free spouse an edge in household goods cases unlike other disputes.
The response uses analogy: what applies to suitability applies even more to freedom.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah)
In a Hanafi court, if one spouse is a slave, the free person gets everything. Other schools would divide equally or give all to one party based on different criteria.
This case illustrates how differences in legal principles lead to different outcomes.

Source Text

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وإن كان أحدهما عبداً ، فالكل للحرّ في الحياة، وللحي بعد الموت (وإن كان أحدهما عبداً، فالكل للحرّ في الحياة، وللحي" بعد الموت)، وعندهما: العبد المأذون والمكاتب كالحر. المورث، فيقع به الترجيح كما يقعُ به الصلاحية للاستعمال بل أولى؛ لأنَّ لليد رجحاناً مطلقاً حتى يرجح به في غير هذا الباب، بخلاف الصلاحية ؛ ولأنَّ يد الباقي منهما يد نفسه ، ويد الوارث خلف عن يد المورث فلا يعارض الأصل.
وعنه :
وقال زفره : المشكل بينهما نصفان والباقي مثل ما قال أبو حنيفة له ، إنَّ المتاع كله بينهما نصفين، وبه قال مالك والشافعي ؛ لأنهما استويا في سبب الاستحقاق، إذ هما ساكنان في بيت واحد، والبيت مع ما فيه في أيديهما، ولا اعتبار بالشبهة في الخصومات وكذا لو اختلف إسكافٌ وعطار في آلة الأساكفة وآلة العطارين، وهي في أيديهما قضى بينهما نصفين، ولا ينظر إلى ما يصلح لأحدهما ؛ لأنه قد يتخذه لنفسه أو للبيع، فلا يصلح مرجحاً.
وقال الحسن البصري له : إنَّ المتاع كله للمرأة، وليس للرجل إلا ما عليه من ثياب بدنه ؛ لأنَّ المرأة هي الساكنة فيه، ويد صاحب البيت على ما في البيت أقـوى وأظهر من يد غيره ، وفي الدعاوي يقدم صاحب اليد.
وقال ابن أبي ليلى الله له : إنَّ المتاع كله للزوج ، وكيف ما كان؛ لأنَّ المرأة في يدِ الزوج، فما في البيت أيضاً يكون في يده، وإن كان البيت لها ، ألا ترى أنه صاحب البيت، وأنَّ البيت يضاف إليه، فصار بمنزلة المؤجر مع المستأجر، وإذا اختلفا في متاع المنزل فإنَّ القول للمستأجر ؛ لكونه مضافاً إليه بالسكنى وليس للمؤجر سوى ما عليه من ثياب بدنه، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ (١) ، وغيره، فهذه المسألة هى المُسَبَّعة، فتذكرها وتشكّر. كتاب الدعوى باب التحالف
ألا ترى أنَّ الحر والمكاتب لـو اختصما في شيء في أيديهما، قضى بينهما ؛ لاستوائهما في اليد، ولو كان في يد ثالث وأقاما البينة استويا فيه، فكما لا يترجح الحرُّ بالحرية في سائر الخصومات، فكذا في متاع البيت.
والجواب من جانب الإمام : إنَّ يد المملوكِ لا تكون متساوية ليد الحرّ، فإنَّ يدَه يدُ نفسه من كل وجه، ويد المملوك يد الغير وهو المولى من وجه، فترجحت به في حق متاع البيت، ألا ترى أنها تترجّح بالصلاحية، فهذا أولى أن يترجح به، بخلاف سائر
الخصومات، فإنها لا تترجّح بالصلاحية. فكذا بالحرية.
Cream of the finality for the core of the care
And if one of them is a slave, then everything is for the free in life, and for the living after death (and if one of them is a slave, everything is for the free in life, and for the living after death). And according to the two: the authorized slave and the mukatab are like the free. The inheritor: thus preference is given by it just as preference is given by suitability for use, rather more so; because the hand has absolute predominance such that it is given preference in other chapters, unlike suitability; and because the hand of the survivor among them is his own hand, and the hand of the heir is a substitute for the hand of the deceased, so it does not oppose the original.
And from him:
And Zufar said: The ambiguous between them are half each, and the remainder is as Abu Hanifa said for him. Indeed, the goods are half between them, and Malik and al-Shafi'i said the same; because they are equal in the cause of entitlement, since both are residents in one house, and the house with what is in it is in their possession, and there is no consideration of presumption in lawsuits. Similarly, if a cobbler and a perfumer disagree about the tools of cobblers and the tools of perfumers, and they are in their possession, it is judged between them half each, and no consideration is given to what is suitable for one of them; because he might acquire it for himself or for sale, so it is not a valid weighting factor.
And al-Hasan al-Basri said: Indeed, all the goods belong to the woman, and the man only has the clothes on his body; because the woman is the resident in it, and the hand of the owner of the house over what is in the house is stronger and more apparent than the hand of others, and in lawsuits the possessor is given precedence.
And Ibn Abi Layla said: Indeed, all the goods belong to the husband, however it may be; because the woman is in the husband's hand, so what is in the house is also in his hand, even if the house is hers. Do you not see that he is the owner of the house, and the house is attributed to him? So he is like the lessor with the lessee. And if they differ about the furnishings of the house, then the statement is of the lessee because it is attributed to him by residency, and the lessor only has the clothes on his body. This was mentioned by al-Zayla'i and others. So this issue is the one called 'the sevenfold', so remember it and be grateful.
(1) His statement: So everything is for the free in life because the hand of the free is stronger than the hand of the slave, because his hand is a hand of ownership, and the hand of the slave is not a hand of ownership.
[2] His statement: And for the living after death because the dead has no hand, so the hand of the living is left without opposition.
[3] His statement: And according to the two... etc. Means that this in its absolute form is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and according to his two companions, the authorized slave and the mukatab are like the free because they have a hand considered in lawsuits.
(1) In Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:312-313).

Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath
Do you not see that if a free person and a mukatab dispute over something in their hands, it is judged between them because they are equal in hand? And if it is in the hand of a third party and they bring evidence, they are equal in it. So just as the free person is not given preference by freedom in other lawsuits, so too in household goods.
And the answer from the side of the Imam: The hand of the slave is not equal to the hand of the free, for his hand is his own hand in all respects, and the hand of the slave is the hand of another, i.e., the master in some respects, so preference is given by it regarding household goods. Do you not see that preference is given by suitability? So this is more deserving of preference by it, unlike other lawsuits, for they are not given preference by suitability. So too by freedom.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of a disputed legal issue: the division of household goods when spouses differ, especially when one spouse is a slave. It is taken from a Hanafi commentary (sharh) on Al-Wiqayah, and it presents multiple scholarly opinions, including those of the Hanafi imams, Zufar, Malik, al-Shafi'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, and Ibn Abi Layla.

The author first states Abu Hanifa's ruling: if one spouse is a slave and they dispute household goods, everything goes to the free spouse during their lifetime, and to the surviving spouse after death. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that an authorized slave (ma'dhun) and a mukatab are treated like free persons in this matter, because they have a valid hand (yad) in lawsuits.

Then the text introduces Zufar's view, with which Malik and al-Shafi'i agree: the goods are divided equally between them because both are residents and have equal possession. The author gives an analogy of a cobbler and perfumer disputing tools — since both are in possession, it is split equally, regardless of suitability for their trades.

Al-Hasan al-Basri rules that all goods belong to the wife, as she is the resident, and the hand of the occupant is stronger. Ibn Abi Layla gives all to the husband, analogizing him to a lessor who has superior hand over the house. The text notes this issue is known as 'the sevenfold' (musabba'ah), a famous point in Hanafi fiqh.

Commentary on the matn follows: explaining why the free person gets everything (stronger hand), and why the survivor gets it (only the living has a hand). It clarifies that the two companions differ on slaves who have commercial capacity. Then the author includes a rebuttal from Abu Hanifa's side: the hand of a slave is not fully independent; it is shared with the master, so in household goods the free spouse's hand is stronger. This is unlike other lawsuits where both are equal. The distinction is that in household goods, suitability for use also plays a role, and the free person's hand is more complete.

The overall discussion illustrates how Hanafi jurists weigh factors like possession (yad) and suitability (salahiyyah) to resolve disputes, and how they defend their positions against objections from other schools.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over property, used as a legal basis for preference in disputes.
صلاحية (salahiyyah): Suitability of an item for a particular person's trade or use, used as a weighting factor in household goods disputes.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave under a contract of manumission who earns and pays installments for freedom; considered to have a valid hand.
المسألة المسبعة (al-mas'alah al-musabba'ah): The 'sevenfold issue' — a famous problem in Hanafi fiqh regarding household goods disputes with multiple opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a free husband and a slave wife dispute household goods, who gets the goods?
A) Everything goes to the free husband.
B) Everything goes to the slave wife.
C) The goods are divided equally.
D) It depends on who is the survivor.

Key Takeaway
When one spouse is a slave, the free spouse gets all disputed household goods because the free person's hand is stronger and the slave's hand is not fully independent.

Dispute over Household Goods: Free Man vs Mukatab
Imam's response to objection on yad equality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the free man's possession is preferred over the mukatab's in household goods disputes and explain the rationale based on salahiyyah and the nature of possession.

Core Idea
In household goods disputes, the free man's hand is entirely his own, while the mukatab's hand is shared with his master; thus, the free man is preferred.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents an intra-Hanafi debate: should a free man's possession (yad) automatically outweigh a mukatab's when they dispute household goods? An objector argues for equality in all matters. The Imam's response, however, distinguishes household goods based on the concept of salahiyyah (competence). You will analyze the Imam's qiyas and understand why this ruling applies specifically to household items.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the relied-upon ruling: in a dispute over household goods between a free man and a mukatab, the free man's possession gives him preference.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The objector (within the Hanafi school) argues that possession is equal in all disputes, including household goods.
They cite cases where the qadi treats them equally.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their evidence: if both have possession, the qadi decides equally; if a third party holds and both have evidence, they are equal. Freedom does not give preference in other disputes, so why here?
This is a qiyas from other disputes to household goods.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Imam's response: the free man's hand is completely his own, while the mukatab's hand is also his master's. Additionally, household goods are tied to salahiyyah (competence), which belongs to the free man. Therefore, his hand is stronger.
The Imam uses qiyas from salahiyyah to freedom.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, the qadi will award household goods to the free man; in other disputes, they are treated equally.
This is the practical consequence of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والجواب من جانب الإمام : إنَّ يد المملوكِ لا تكون متساوية ليد الحرّ، فإنَّ يدَه يدُ نفسه من كل وجه، ويد المملوك يد الغير وهو المولى من وجه، فترجحت به في حق متاع البيت، ألا ترى أنها تترجّح بالصلاحية، فهذا أولى أن يترجح به، بخلاف سائر
الخصومات، فإنها لا تترجّح بالصلاحية. فكذا بالحرية.
Do you not see that if a free man and a mukatab dispute over something in their possession, the judge decides between them equally because their possession is equal; and if it is in the possession of a third party and they both present evidence, they are equal in it. So just as the free man is not preferred due to freedom in other disputes, similarly in household goods. And the answer from the side of the Imam: The possession of a slave is not equal to the possession of a free man, because his (the free man's) hand is his own from every aspect, while the hand of the slave is the hand of another (the master) from one aspect. Therefore, it (the free man's hand) is preferred in the case of household goods. Do you not see that it is preferred by competence (صلاحية), so this is more deserving of preference. Unlike other disputes, for they are not preferred by competence. Similarly, it is by freedom.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi legal argument concerning a dispute over household goods (متاع البيت) between a free man and a mukatab (a slave with a contract of manumission). The objector argues that in other disputes between them, they are treated equally because their possession (yad) is considered equal, so likewise in household goods they should be equal. The Imam (Abu Hanifa) responds that the possession of a free man is stronger because it is his own from every aspect, whereas the slave's possession is also that of his master from one aspect. Therefore, in household goods — which are typically associated with the free person's household competence (صلاحية) — the free man's hand is preferred. This analogy (qiyas) is based on the principle of صلاحية (competence/eligibility): just as a free man is preferred in matters of صلاحية, so too he is preferred in freedom. The Imam distinguishes this from other disputes where صلاحية does not apply, so equality holds there. The ruling: in a dispute over household goods, the free man's claim is given priority over the mukatab's.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over property; it is a legal basis for claims and presumptions.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) and is allowed to earn money to purchase his freedom.
صلاحية (salahiyyah): Competence or eligibility to manage household affairs; it is a quality attributed to free persons, which gives their possession (yad) priority in matters related to household goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the free man has stronger evidence.
B) Because the free man's hand is entirely his own, while the mukatab's hand also represents his master, and the principle of salahiyyah gives preference to the free man.
C) Because the free man is always truthful in such disputes.
D) Because the mukatab's possession is invalid altogether.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the free man's possession is superior to a mukatab's in household goods disputes due to the complete ownership of his hand and the principle of salahiyyah, but not in other disputes.

Dismissal of Claim When Possessor Proves Third-Party Right
Explains when a possessor is not a litigant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a possessor's claim of a third-party right results in dismissal of the lawsuit, and distinguish the ruling when the property is existent versus destroyed.

Core Idea
A possessor who proves the property belongs to an absent third party is not a litigant, but only if the property is still in existence.

This lesson explains a procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh: who cannot be a litigant in a property dispute. The key is that mere possession does not automatically make someone a proper defendant. If the possessor can prove that the disputed property belongs to an absent third party through a recognized legal relationship (e.g., deposit, loan, lease), and the property is still in existence, the claimant's lawsuit against the possessor is dismissed. However, if the property is destroyed, the possessor remains a litigant because the claim transforms into a debt.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the procedural outcome: when the possessor (dhul yad) proves a third-party right over existent property, the claimant's lawsuit is dropped (sqt al-khusuma).
This is a special exception to the general rule that a possessor is the defendant.
2
Identify the conditions
The possessor must claim one of the specified relationships (deposit, loan, lease, pawn, usurpation) and provide legal proof (bayyinah). Additionally, the property must be existent (qaim) – capable of being pointed to.
The condition of existence is derived from the implication of the word 'this' in the matn.
3
Analyze the exception
If the property is destroyed (halik), the possessor's claim is not accepted, and he remains a litigant. The commentary gives the example of a slave dying in the possessor's hands.
The reason is that the claim becomes a debt, and deposit/lease of a debt is impossible.
4
Understand the effect of the absent party's confirmation
If the absent party later appears and confirms the relationship, the possessor can recover compensation paid to the claimant, except in cases of usurpation (ghasb) where no recovery is allowed.
This shows that the possessor is not liable in the long run if the relationship is genuine.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Test hypothetical cases: e.g., a car in A's possession claimed by B. A proves it was loaned by C (absent). If the car exists, case dismissed. If the car was totaled, A must defend.
Practice identifying whether the property is existent or destroyed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيمن لا يكون خصماً ولو قال ذو اليد : هذا الشيء أودعنيه، أو أعارنيه، أو آجرنيه، أو رهنيه زيد، أو غصبته منه، وبرهن عليه، سقطت خصومة المدعي [1] قوله : فصل ؛ هذا فصل في بيانِ مَن لا يكون خصماً، ذكره بعد بيان من يكون خصماً، لمناسبة المضادة بينهما، وقدم الأول لكون ذكره عمدة في المقام ؛ لأنَّ الكتاب كتاب الدعوى، وهي عبارة عن الخصومة، وذكر مَن لا يكون خصماً إِنما هو لاتضاح المقام ؛ لأن الأشياء تعرف بأضدادها. [2] قوله : ولو قال.... الخ ؛ تصوير المسألة : أنَّ داراً وثوباً في يد إنسان ادعى رجل عليه أنهما له ، فقال ذو اليد : هذا الشيء أودعنيه فلان الغائب، أو أعارنيه أو أجرنيه أو رهنه عندي، أو غصبتُه منه ، وأقام البينة على ذلك، فلا خصومة بين ذي اليد الذي هو المدعى عليه وبين المدعي. [3] قوله : هذا الشيء ؛ ظاهر قوله : هذا الشيء ؛ أنه قائم ؛ لأن الإشارة الحسية لا تكون إلا إلى موجود في الخارج فمفهومه أنه لا تندفع لو كان المدعي هالكاً، وبه صرح في «العناية») أخذاً من «خزانة الأكمل» فقال : عبدٌ هَلَكَ في يد رجلٍ أَقامَ رجلٌ البيِّنَةَ أنه عبده، وأقامَ الذي مات في يده أنه أودعه فلان، أو غصبه أو أجره لم تقبل، وهو خصم، فإنّه يدعي الدين عليه، وإيداع الدين لا يمكن. ثم إذا حضر الغائب وصدقه في الإيداع والإجارة والرهن رجع عليه بما ضَمِنَ للمدعي، أما لو كان غصباً لم يرجع ، وكذا في العارية والإباق مثل الهلاكِ هاهنا ، عاد العبد يوماً يكون عبداً لمن استقرَّ عليه الضمان. كذا في «البحر ) فإن
A chapter on who is not a litigant. And if the person in possession says: this thing was deposited with me, or loaned to me, or leased to me, or pawned to me by Zaid, or I usurped it from him, and he proves it, the claimant's lawsuit drops. [1] His saying: chapter; this is a chapter on clarifying who is not a litigant, mentioned after clarifying who is a litigant, due to the opposition between them, and he presented the first because its mention is essential in this place; because the book is the book of claims, and it is an expression of litigation, and mentioning who is not a litigant is only for clarity of the matter; because things are known by their opposites. [2] His saying: and if he said... etc.; the depiction of the case: a house and a garment are in the hand of a man, another man claims them, so the possessor says: this thing was deposited with me by so-and-so the absent, or loaned to me, or leased to me, or pawned with me, or I usurped it from him, and he brings proof of that, then there is no litigation between the possessor who is the defendant and the claimant. [3] His saying: this thing; the apparent meaning of his saying 'this thing' is that it exists; because physical indication is only to something existing in the external world, so its implication is that it does not apply if the claimed property is destroyed, and it is stated explicitly in al-'Inayah, taken from Khizanat al-Akmal, where he said: a slave perishes in the hand of a man, a man brings proof that he is his slave, and the one in whose hand he died brings proof that he was deposited with him by so-and-so, or usurped, or leased, it is not accepted, and he is a litigant, because he claims a debt against him, and depositing a debt is not possible. Then if the absent person appears and confirms the deposit, lease, or pawn, he (the possessor) recovers from him what he guaranteed to the claimant. But if it was usurpation, he does not recover; similarly for loan and absconding like destruction here; the slave returns one day to be a slave of the one upon whom the guarantee is settled. Likewise in al-Bahr. Then
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining who cannot be a litigant in a property dispute. The matn states that if the person in physical possession (ذو اليد) claims that the disputed property is held by him through a specific legal relationship with an absent third party—such as deposit (إيداع), loan for use (عارية), lease (إجارة), pledge (رهن), or usurpation (غصب)—and he proves this with legal evidence (بينة), then the direct claimant's case against him is dismissed. The reason is that the possessor is not the true owner or obligor for the property; he is merely a custodian or bailee. The commentary elaborates that the property must be in existence (قائم) for this ruling to apply, because if the property is destroyed (هالك), the possessor cannot point to it, and the claim becomes a debt claim against him. Drawing from al-'Inayah and Khizanat al-Akmal, the commentary provides an example: if a slave dies in someone's possession, and a claimant proves ownership, while the possessor claims the slave was deposited or leased from an absent person, his claim is not accepted because it is now a debt claim. However, if the absent person later appears and confirms the relationship, the possessor can recover what he paid to the claimant, except in cases of usurpation or where the property perished in a way that changes ownership. This shows the Hanafi position that the possessor's claim of a third-party right only removes him from litigation if the property is identifiable and existent.

Key Terms
ذو اليد (dhul yad): The person who has physical possession of the property in dispute, regardless of ownership.
خصم (khasm): A litigant, a party in a lawsuit, specifically the person against whom a claim is made.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal proof, typically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, used to establish facts in court.
قائم (qaim): Existent, capable of being pointed to physically.
هالك (halik): Destroyed, perished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the claimant's lawsuit drop against the person in possession?
A) Whenever the possessor claims the property belongs to an absent person.
B) Only if the possessor proves that the property was deposited, loaned, leased, pawned, or usurped from an absent person, and the property is existent.
C) Only if the absent person appears in court and confirms the possessor's claim.
D) Only if the property is destroyed and the possessor proves a contract with the absent person.

Key Takeaway
A possessor who proves a third-party right over existent property ceases to be a litigant; if the property is destroyed, the claim proceeds against the possessor.

Possessor's Claim from Absent Person Drops Lawsuit
Ruling with rational proof from commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a possessor's proof of possession from an absent person drops the claimant's lawsuit, and identify the condition that the property must be extant.

Core Idea
A possessor who proves the property is from an absent person is not a litigant; the lawsuit drops.

In claims (da'wa), the person in possession is typically the defendant. But what if the possessor proves that the property came into his hands through a lawful contract or even usurpation from a third party who is absent? This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains that such a possessor is no longer a proper litigant—his only role is as a trustee or custodian. The ruling turns on the existence of the property and the validity of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the general ruling
Analyze the text: the possessor claims the property came from an absent person via deposit, loan, lease, pledge, or usurpation, and provides proof. The ruling: the claimant's lawsuit against the possessor is dropped.
The key is that the possessor does not claim ownership, but rather a custodial or unlawful (usurpation) relationship with an absent third party.
2
Isolate the key condition
Notice the phrase 'this thing' (هذا الشيء) which implies the property must be physically existing. If the property is destroyed, the possessor's claim is not accepted, and he remains a litigant because he is then claiming a debt (guarantee) against the absent person, which is not permissible in deposit etc.
This condition is derived from the commentary's discussion of a slave that died.
3
Analyze the legal consequence for different bases
If the absent person later appears and confirms the contract (deposit, lease, pledge), the possessor can claim compensation from the absent person for what he had to pay the claimant. However, if the possessor claimed usurpation (ghasb), he has no recourse against the absent person because the usurper is a guarantor and cannot claim reimbursement for his own wrongful act.
The distinction shows that the possessor's liability differs based on the nature of his possession: trust (amanah) vs. unlawful taking.
4
Synthesize the principle
The overarching principle is that the possessor is not a litigant because his hand is not a hand of ownership; he is merely a custodian or a wrongdoer who must hand over the property or its value. The claimant must pursue the absent person who is the real owner or the real defendant.
This is a rational legal rule (dalil 'aqli) based on the nature of possession and litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٢) «البحر الرائق (٧: ٢٢٩). (٢)
Section on who is not a litigant. And if the possessor says: This thing was deposited with me by him, or lent to me by him, or leased to me by him, or pledged to me by Zaid, or I usurped it from him, and he provides proof on it, the lawsuit of the claimant is dropped. [1] He (the commentator) says: 'Section' – this is a section explaining who is not a litigant. He mentioned it after explaining who is a litigant, due to the opposition between them, and he presented the first because its mention is fundamental in this context; because the book is the book of claims, and it is an expression of litigation, and mentioning who is not a litigant is only to clarify the context; because things are known by their opposites. [2] He says: 'And if he says... etc.' – an illustration of the issue: there is a house and a garment in the hand of a person; a man claims that they are his; then the possessor says: This thing was deposited with me by so-and-so the absent, or lent to me, or leased to me, or was pledged to me by him, or I usurped it from him, and he provides evidence on that. Then there is no litigation between the possessor who is the defendant and the claimant. [3] He says: 'This thing' – the apparent meaning of his statement 'this thing' is that it exists; because sensory indication is only to an externally existing thing, so its implication is that it is not repelled if the property is destroyed. And it is explicitly stated in Al-Inayah, taken from Khizanat al-Akmal, saying: A slave died in the hand of a man; a man provided evidence that he is his slave, and the one in whose hand he died provided evidence that he deposited him with so-and-so, or usurped him, or leased him, it is not accepted, and he is a litigant, because he claims a debt against him, and deposit of a debt is not possible. Then if the absent person appears and confirms the deposit, lease, or pledge, he (the possessor) can claim from him (the absent person) what he had to compensate the claimant. But if it was usurpation, he does not have recourse. Similarly in borrowing and absconding, like destruction here, the slave would one day become the slave of the one on whom the guarantee is settled. As stated in Al-Bahr. (1) Al-Inayah (7:226). (2) Al-Bahr al-Raiq (7:229). (2)
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. It states the general rule: if a person in possession of property claims that it came into his possession through a contract (deposit, loan, lease, pledge) or through usurpation from an absent third party, and he provides proof, then the claimant's lawsuit against him is dropped. The reasoning is that the possessor's hand is not a hand of litigation; he is merely a trustee or custodian. The commentator explains the structure of the section and gives an example. Then he clarifies that the property must be existing (not destroyed) for the rule to apply. He cites a case of a slave who died, where the possessor's claim is not accepted because the property is no longer extant. The commentary also discusses the consequence: if the absent person confirms the contracts, the possessor can seek indemnity from him, except in case of usurpation where he has no recourse. This shows the principle that one who guarantees property cannot claim reimbursement for an unlawful act.

Key Terms
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who is physically in possession of the property in question.
خصم (khasm): A litigant or opposing party in a lawsuit.
إيداع (ida'): A contract of deposit where the possessor holds property as a trust (amanah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If the property is destroyed.
B) If the property is extant and he provides proof.
C) If he claims to have purchased it.
D) If the claimant admits it.

Key Takeaway
A possessor who proves the property came from an absent person via contract or usurpation is not a litigant; the lawsuit drops only if the property exists.

Exceptions Where Possessor Remains a Litigant
Exception cases where possessor remains a litigant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two cases where a possessor remains a litigant despite receiving property from an absent person, and explain the legal reasoning behind each exception.

Core Idea
Claiming purchase admits ownership; alleging tort admits liability – both make the possessor a litigant.

This lesson examines two exceptions to the general rule that a possessor who claims to have received property from an absent person is not a litigant. The passage explains that if the possessor claims to have purchased the property, or if the claimant accuses the possessor of usurpation or theft, the lawsuit proceeds. Understanding these exceptions clarifies the legal basis for establishing litigation in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The default rule is that a possessor who provides proof of receipt from an absent person (e.g., deposit, loan) is not a litigant because his hand is not a hand of litigation.
2
Analyze the first exception: claim of purchase
If the possessor says 'I bought it from the absent person,' he admits that his possession is based on ownership, making his hand a hand of litigation. Hence the lawsuit is not dropped.
The key is that purchase transfers ownership, unlike other forms of receipt.
3
Analyze the second exception: claimant's allegation of a tort
If the claimant accuses the possessor of a tort (usurpation or theft), the lawsuit is upheld. The possessor becomes a defendant because the claim is based on a wrongful act, not mere receipt.
Even if the possessor proves receipt, the tort allegation creates liability.
4
Note the linguistic subtlety
The phrase 'it was stolen from me' is put in the passive voice ('suriqa minni') to avoid directly accusing the possessor of theft, out of deference.
This shows legal sensitivity in language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] وإن قال : اشتريتُهُ من الغائب، وقال المدعي : غصبته ، أو سرقته، أو سُرِقَ مِنِّي لا ، وإِن بَرْهَنَ ذو اليد على إيداع زيد؛ لأنَّ ذا اليد إذا قال : اشتريته من الغائب، فقد أقر أن يدَهُ يد خصومة، فلا يسقط عنه الخصومة، كذا إذا ادعى المدعي الفعل على ذي اليد كما إذا قال : غصبته مني ، أو سرقته منّي لا يسقط الخصومة، وكذا إذا قال : سَرَقَ منّى. And if he says: I bought it from the absent person, and the claimant says: I usurped it, or I stole it, or it was stolen from me – no, even if the possessor proves the deposit of Zaid; because the hand of these is not a hand of litigation. [2] And if he says: I bought it from the absent person, and the claimant says: I usurped it, or I stole it, or it was stolen from me – no, even if the possessor proves the deposit of Zaid; because when the possessor says: I bought it from the absent person, he has admitted that his hand is a hand of litigation, so the lawsuit is not dropped from him. Similarly, if the claimant claims an action against the possessor, as when he says: He usurped it from me, or he stole it from me – the lawsuit is not dropped, and also if he says: It was stolen from me. [4] He says: 'It was stolen from me' – only he made it passive (majhul) in construction for the sake of concealment, so that...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage continues the section on who is not a litigant, now listing exceptions where the possessor remains a litigant. First, if the possessor claims to have purchased the property from the absent person, he admits that his possession is based on ownership, so he becomes a litigant. Second, if the claimant accuses the possessor of an action like usurpation or theft, the lawsuit is upheld because the claim is based on a wrongful act, not mere ownership. The commentary explains that the phrase 'it was stolen from me' is put in the passive form to avoid directly accusing the possessor of theft, out of deference. The reasoning is that the possessor's admission of purchase or the claimant's allegation of a tort makes the possessor liable as a defendant.

Key Terms
شرى (shara): To purchase; here the possessor claims to have bought from the absent person.
سرق (saraqa): To steal; the claimant alleges theft by the possessor.
غصب (ghasaba): To usurp; the claimant alleges wrongful possession by the possessor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He claims purchase.
C) He claims lease.
D) He claims pledge.

Key Takeaway
A possessor who claims purchase from an absent person, or a claimant who alleges a tort by the possessor, both establish the possessor as a litigant.

Ruling on Lawsuit When Possessor Claims Purchase from Absent Person
Explains ruling when possessor claims purchase from absent person and plaintiff claims theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a possessor claims purchase from an absent person and explain why the lawsuit is not dismissed.

Core Idea
Claiming purchase from an absent person admits a litigating hand, so the lawsuit continues.

This passage addresses a common situation in property disputes: the person in possession of an item claims he bought it from someone who is absent (not in court). The plaintiff, however, accuses the possessor of theft or usurpation. The Hanafi school holds that the possessor's claim does not free him from being a party to the lawsuit; rather, it confirms his role as an opponent. Understanding this ruling is essential for judges and litigants in property cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
Determine who is the possessor, who is the plaintiff, and what each claims. The possessor says 'I bought it from an absent person'; the plaintiff says 'he stole it' or 'he usurped it' or 'it was stolen from me'.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that the lawsuit is not dismissed; the possessor remains a defendant. Even if the possessor proves deposit from a known person, the suit continues because the plaintiff's claim of direct action (like theft) makes the possessor a party.
The phrase 'then no' in the text means the lawsuit is not dismissed.
3
Identify the legal basis (dalil)
The possessor's claim of purchase is an admission that his hand is a hand of litigation (yad khusuma). This admission makes him an opponent in the suit. Alternatively, the plaintiff's specific accusation of theft or usurpation directly makes the possessor a party because it is a claim of action against him.
The commentary elaborates on two implications of the plaintiff's evidence: establishing ownership for the absent person (which has no opponent) and repelling the plaintiff's lawsuit (which does have an opponent).
4
Understand the detailed reasoning (talil)
The commentary explains that the possessor's claim of purchase is an admission of ownership, making his hand a hand of litigation. Also, the plaintiff's claim of a specific tort (like theft or usurpation) targets the possessor as the actor, so he becomes a party. The passive voice in 'was stolen from me' is used to avoid naming the thief, but it still implicates the possessor.
The distinction between claim of ownership (which requires possession) and claim of action (which does not) is critical.

والثاني: دفع خصومة المدعي، وهو خصم فيه فيثبت ؛ لأن مقصود ذي اليد إثبات أن يدَه يد حفظ، والمدعي خصم له فيه، فتقبلُ بينته ويثبت يد الحفظ، وصار كالوكيل بنقل المرأة إذا أقامت البينة على الطلاق.

Also, the implication of the evidence is two things: One: establishing ownership for the absent one, and there is no opponent in it, so it is not established. The second: repelling the plaintiff's lawsuit, and he is an opponent in it, so it is established, because the purpose of the possessor is to prove that his hand is a hand of preservation, and the plaintiff is an opponent to him in that, so his evidence is accepted and the hand of preservation is established, and it becomes like the agent of a woman's transfer when she produces evidence of divorce. (2) His saying: And if he says... etc.: meaning if the plaintiff claims something against the possessor, and the possessor says: I bought it from the absent one, the lawsuit is not repelled. Likewise, if the plaintiff says: he usurped it from me, or he stole it from me, or was stolen from me, the lawsuit is not repelled, even if the possessor produces evidence of deposit. (3) His saying: Because the possessor... etc.: its detail: The possessor, when he says: I bought it from the absent one, he admits that his hand is a hand of ownership, so he acknowledges that he is an opponent, so the lawsuit is not repelled. And when the plaintiff says to the possessor: he usurped it from me, the possessor becomes an opponent, by virtue of the claim of action against him, which is usurpation. Not by his hand. Do you not see that the claim of usurpation, as it is valid against the possessor, is valid against a non-possessor, so that if someone claims against another that he usurped his slave, and the slave is not in his hand, his claim is valid, and the value is obligatory upon him, unlike the claim of absolute ownership, for the possessor is an opponent in it by virtue of his hand, so his claim is not valid against a non-possessor, and the claim of action is valid against him. [4] His saying: stole from me; he used the passive voice for concealment, so that...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage from 'Zubdat al-Nihayah' explains a key rule in the chapter of lawsuits. It states that if a person in possession of an object says he bought it from an absent person, the lawsuit against him is not dismissed, even if the plaintiff claims usurpation or theft. The reason is that his claim of purchase from an absent person is an admission that he has a litigating hand (yad khusuma), so he remains a party to the case. Similarly, if the plaintiff directly accuses the possessor of a specific act like usurpation or theft, the suit continues. The passive construction 'was stolen from me' (سُرِق مني) is intentionally used to avoid explicitly naming the thief, out of compassion and to avoid the hadd punishment. The commentary elaborates that the possessor cannot escape litigation by alleging a purchase from an absent party, and even if he proves a deposit from a known person (like Zayd), the lawsuit is not dismissed because those hands (the hands of the absent and Zayd) are not litigation hands—meaning they are not subject to the suit. The discussion underscores the Hanafi principle that possession alone can make one a party to a property dispute.

Key Terms
يد خصومة (yad khusuma): A hand that subjects the holder to being a party in a lawsuit due to the nature of the claim against them.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): An absent person, specifically one not present in court whom the possessor claims to have bought from or received the property from.
إيداع (ida'): Deposit; a contract where one person entrusts property to another for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lawsuit is dismissed because the possessor is not the original defendant.
B) The lawsuit is not dismissed because the possessor's claim makes his hand a hand of litigation.
C) The lawsuit is dismissed only if the possessor proves the deposit.
D) The lawsuit is dismissed because the claim of theft is not valid.

Key Takeaway
Claiming purchase from an absent person does not free the possessor from a lawsuit; it confirms his role as a defendant.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Dismissal of Lawsuit When Depositor Is Unknown
Intra-Hanafi dispute on lawsuit dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on lawsuit dismissal when the depositor is unknown, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf preserve the lawsuit; Imam Muhammad dismisses it.

This passage from the chapter of Lawsuit presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement. The key question: when the person in possession claims a named person deposited the item, or witnesses say the depositor is unknown, should the lawsuit against the possessor be dismissed? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no; Imam Muhammad says yes. The ruling hinges on whether the plaintiff's passive-voiced claim of theft implies the possessor is the thief, and whether tajhil (concealment) is excused to avoid hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Understand the factual scenario: the possessor claims a named person deposited the item, or witnesses say the depositor is unknown. The ruling is contested.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's reasoning
They argue that the plaintiff's claim of theft, even if phrased passively (e.g., 'it was stolen from me'), necessarily implies the possessor is the thief. Otherwise, the plaintiff's right would be lost.
They use the passive voice as a concession to avoid hadd, not to deny the accusation.
3
Examine Imam Muhammad's reasoning
He argues that the plaintiff did not directly accuse the possessor, but rather an unknown person. A claim against an unknown person is invalid, so the lawsuit falls.
He analogizes to the case of ghasb (usurpation) where passive voice is not excused.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that the analogy to ghasb is invalid because ghasb has no hadd punishment, so there is no excuse for concealment in ghasb. The hadd consideration justifies the passive voice in theft claims.
This distinction shows sensitivity to the gravity of hadd punishments.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If Muhammad's view is followed, the possessor is free from litigation. If Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view is followed, the lawsuit remains, and the judge can adjudicate the item to the plaintiff without imposing hadd.
The practical consequence is whether the possessor must answer the lawsuit or is discharged.

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of 'Lawsuit' and 'Tahāluf' (oath-taking) presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the dismissal of a lawsuit. It considers a scenario where the person in possession of an object claims that a named person deposited it with him, or where witnesses state that an unknown person deposited it. The core ruling: According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the lawsuit is not dismissed; according to Imam Muhammad, it is dismissed. The reasoning: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the plaintiff's claim of theft (even if phrased passively) implies the possessor is the thief, so the lawsuit continues. If the possessor is not considered a thief, the lawsuit would be dismissed and the plaintiff's right would be lost. Imam Muhammad argues that the plaintiff did not claim an action against the possessor directly, but against an unknown person, which is invalid, so the lawsuit falls. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) respond by distinguishing this from the case of usurpation (ghasb), which carries no hadd punishment, so the passive voice is not excused there. The passage also notes that when witnesses say 'we know him by face but not by name,' it is as if the possessor admitted theft. The final note clarifies that when witnesses refer to a known individual, the lawsuit is not dismissed by agreement because the plaintiff may be that individual.

Key Terms
أودعه (awda'ahu): He deposited it with him; used to describe the act of giving something for safekeeping.
الخصومة (al-khusuma): The lawsuit or legal dispute between parties before a judge.
التجهيل (al-tajhil): Concealment or making something unknown; here, using the passive voice to avoid naming the thief.

The Effect of Witnesses' Knowledge of the Depositor on the Lawsuit: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with reasoning and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the validity of a lawsuit when witnesses do not know the depositor, and explain the underlying rationale regarding the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
Fear of the hadd penalty for theft justifies vagueness in the claim, preserving the lawsuit to protect the rightful owner.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a claimant sues a possessor for a deposited item, the witnesses' knowledge of the depositor affects the lawsuit's validity. This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the lawsuit continues even if witnesses do not know the depositor, while Muhammad al-Shaybani argues it should be dropped. Understanding their reasoning, especially the role of the hadd penalty, is essential for grasping Hanafi procedural law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the two main scenarios: (1) witnesses say they do not know the depositor, and (2) witnesses say they know the depositor by face but not by name. Focus first on scenario (1).
The possessor is the person currently holding the item (dhu al-yad).
2
State the Positions
For scenario (1), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the lawsuit is NOT dropped; Muhammad rules that it IS dropped. Note the disagreement.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf are collectively referred to as 'al-Shaykhān'.
3
Understand the Reasoning of Each Side
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: The claimant might be the real depositor; by treating the possessor as a thief (without actually applying the hadd), the lawsuit continues so the property can be returned, and the hadd penalty is avoided. Muhammad's reasoning: The claim is against an unknown person, which is invalid by analogy with a claim of usurpation (ghasb) without naming the usurper.
The hadd penalty for theft is a fixed punishment; its severity justifies the claimant's vagueness.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Muhammad
The response from the two shaykhs: The analogy with usurpation fails because usurpation has no fixed hadd penalty, so the claimant is not excused for vagueness. Here, the potential hadd penalty makes the vagueness excusable.
This shows how the presence or absence of a fixed penalty changes the legal analysis.
5
Identify the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If the lawsuit is dropped (Muhammad's view), the possessor keeps the property. If the lawsuit continues (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the judge can award the property to the claimant. The preferred view (muftā bihī) is that of the two shaykhs.
The difference is outcome-determinative for the ownership.

Source Text

(كما لو قال الشُّهودُ : أودعَهُ مَن لا نعرفه) ، فإنَّه لا تندفع الخصومة ، لاحتمال أن يكون المدعي هو الذي أودعه عنده ، (بخلاف قولهم نعرفه بوجهه لا باسمه ونسبه) يقطع ، فكأنه قال له : سرقته مني. يستدعي [١] قوله : عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ؛ لهما : إن ذكر الفعل وهو السرقة الفاعل، وهو السارق لا محالة ، ومن الظاهر أنَّ السارق هو الذي في يده إلا أنَّ المدعي لم يعينه دفعاً للحد وشفقة عليه، وتحصيلاً لثواب الستر، فإنا إذا جعلناه سارقاً لا تندفع الخصومة، والقاضي يقضي بالعين للمدعي. فمتى ظهر سرقة بعد ذلك بيقين لا يقطع يده ؛ لأنه ظهرت سرقته بعد وصول المسروق إلى المالك، ولو لم نجعله سارقاً تندفع الخصومة عنه، ولا يقضي بالعين للمدعي ، فمتى ظهر سرقته بعد ذلك بيقين تقطع يده ؛ لأنه ظهرت سرقته قبل وصول العين إلى المالك، فكان في جعله سارقاً احتيالاً لدفع الحد، ولم يتعلق به عقوبة سوى الضمان. [٣) قوله : فإنه لا تندفع الخصومة ؛ عن ذي اليد بالاتفاق ؛ لأنَّ الشهود لما أحالوا المدعي على رجل معروف تمكن مخاصمته، ولعل المدعي هو ذلك الرجل، فلو اندفعت الخصومة لبطل حقه. و تسقط الخصومة عند أبي حنيفة له ، فإنَّ الشَّهود عالمون بأن المودع ليس هو الذي يدعي، وعند محمد : لا يسقط الخصومة حيث لم يذكروا شخصاً معيناً أودعه عنده.
