Conflicting Marriage Claims: Evidence, Dates, and Precedence
Commentary on Hanafi rulings for two claimants to a woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a woman's confirmation is overridden by possession or consummation, and that evidence (bayyinah) is stronger than confession (iqrar) in adjudicating conflicting marriage claims.

Core Idea
In marriage claims, the woman's confirmation is secondary to possession or consummation, and evidence always trumps confession.

When two men each claim to be married to the same woman, the judge must weigh multiple factors: the woman's own confirmation, who possesses her or has consummated, and whether dates or evidence clarify precedence. The commentary expands on the matn to detail these layers, explaining how a prior judgment stands but can be overturned if later evidence proves an earlier marriage. This lesson focuses on the marriage scenario, which illustrates the Hanafi hierarchy of proofs in family disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary rule for marriage claims
The woman belongs to the one she confirms, but this applies only if the other claimant does not have possession or have consummated with her. If the other has possession or consummation, her confirmation is disregarded because it indicates precedence of his contract.
Possession or consummation is a stronger indicator of a prior marriage than mere acknowledgment.
2
Analyze the role of dates
If both claimants specify a marriage date, the earlier marriage is preferred. The later contract and its proof are invalid because there is no opposition at that earlier time.
If only one dates, the woman's confirmation decides, unless the other has possession.
3
Distinguish evidence from confession
If the woman confirms one claimant who has no proof, but the other brings valid evidence (bayyinah), judgment is given to the one with evidence. Bayyinah is a transitive proof (hujjah muta'addiyah), while iqrar is limited (hujjah qasirah).
Evidence overrides personal acknowledgment in court.
4
Understand the finality of a prior judgment
Once a judge passes judgment based on evidence, that judgment stands. A later claimant bringing similar evidence cannot overturn it unless he proves his marriage preceded the first.
Certainty of error in the first judgment is required to overturn it.
5
Examine the comparison with a possessor
If a woman is in possession of a man and his marriage is apparent, an outsider claiming her must prove his own marriage is earlier. Her confirmation is not considered because possession indicates a prior right.
The principle here mirrors the earlier rule: possession and consummation override mere confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي لمن صدقته وإن أرّخا فالسابق أحق، فإن أقرت من لا حجة له فهي له ، فإن بَرْهَنَ الآخرُ قُضي له، ولو بَرْهَنَ أحدُهما وقُضي له ، ثُمَّ بَرْهَنَ الآخرُ لم يقض له (وهي لمن صدقته وإن أرخا فالسابق أحق ، فإن أقرت من لا حجة له فهي له ، فإِن بَرْهَنَ الآخِرُ قُضي له ، ولو بَرْهَنَ أحدُهما وقُضي له ، ثُمَّ بَرْهَنَا ثُم بَرْهَنَ الآخر لم يقض له [1] [٢ 11 ] قوله : وهي ؛ أي المرأة لمن صدقته ؛ لأنَّ النكاح مما يحكــم بــه بــصادق الزوجين، وهذا إذا لم تكن المرأة المتنازع فيها في يد من كذبته، ولم يكن دخل بها من كذبته، وأما إذا كان في يد الآخر، أو دخل بها فلا اعتبار بالتصديق ؛ لأنه دليل على سبق عقده. كذا في «المنح»). وإن أرخا : أي المدعيان لنكاحها، فالسابق أحق بها من الآخر؛ لأنه لا معارض في هذا الزمان، فيكون القضاء للسابق إذ عقد اللاحق وبرهانه باطل، ولا يعتبر ما ذكر من كونها في يده أو دخل بها ، ولو أرح أحدهما فقط، فإنَّها لن أقرت له ، كما لو أرخ أحدهما، وللآخريد، فإنَّها لذي اليد. كما في «البزازيّة». فإن أقرت المرأة بالزوجية لمن لا حجة فهي ؛ أي المرأة له لتصادقهما على النكاح وهو يثبت بتصادق الزوجين عليه. فإن برهن الآخر؛ أي الذي لم تقرّ له ، قضى له ؛ أي الذي برهن ؛ لأنَّ البينة أقوى من الإقرار، فإنَّ البينة حجّة متعدية، والإقرار حجةٌ قاصرة. ولو برهن أحدهما على النكاح وقضى له؛ أي وقضى القاضـي لـه بـبرهانه بنكاحها. ثم برهن الآخر؛ على أنه نكحها لم يقض له ؛ لأنَّ القضاء الأول قد صح فلا ينقض بما هو مثله ، بل هو دونه ؛ لاتصال القضاء بالأول والثاني. إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ؛ أي سبق نكاحه إيَّاها على نكاح الأول، فحينئذ يقضى له ؛ لتيقن الخطأ في الأول. [٢] قوله : فالسابق أحق ؛ كما إذا دخل أحدهما بها، أو كانت في يده، وفيه إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ، كما لم يقض بحجة الخارج على ذي يدٍ ظَهَرَ نَكَاحُهُ إِلا إذا أثبت سبقه (إذا كانت امرأة في يد رجل ونكاحه ظاهر، وادعى الخارج أنَّها زوجته، وأقام البيئة لم يقض له إلا إذا أثبت أن نكاحه سابق. فإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء من ذي يد، فلكل نصفه بنصف الثّمن، أو تَرْكُه) : أي لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما الخيار إن شاء أخذ نصف ذلك الشَّيء بنصف الثَّمن، وإن شاءَ تَرَك إشعار بأن مجرد دعوى السبق يكفي كما قال بعض المشايخ، وذهب آخرون إلى أنه لا بُدَّ من بيان] نحو إن الأول في رجب، والثاني في شعبان وتمامه في «الفصول العمادية»، وذكر القهستاني(۱) نقلاً عن «الخزانة» : لو وقت أحدهما شهراً والآخر ساعة، فالساعة أولى. انتهى. [١]قوله : إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ؛ أي سبق نكاحه على نكاح ذي اليد بالبينة، فإنّه يقضي له ؛ لأنَّ التصريح فوق الدلالة، فلا تعتبر معه. [٢] قوله : فإن برهنا ؛ أي الخارجان على شراء شيء ؛ أي تمام لشراء شيء. من ذي يد؛ ولم يؤرخا أو أرخا على السواء. فلكل نصفه ؛ أي لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما نصف ذلك الشيء بنصف الثمن إن شاء ورجع به على البائع بنصف ثمنه. أو تركه ؛ أي ترك النصف إن شاء ؛ لأنهما لما استويا في السبب وجب على القاضي أن يقضي به بينهما ؛ لتعذر القضاء بكلّه، فيتخيرُ كل منهما لتغير شرط عقده عليه ؛ فلعل رغبته في تملك الكلّ ، فيرده ويأخذ كل الثمن، فصار كفضوليين باع كل منهما من رجل، وأجاز المالك البيعين، فإنَّ كلاً منهما يتخير ؛ لأنه تغير عليه شرط عقده، فلعل رغبته في تملك الكل. وبه قال مالك له في رواية والشافعي الله في قول، وقال في قول آخر : يقرع، وبه قال أحمد ، وعن الشافعي الله أيضاً : تسقط البينات، ويرجع إلى البائع، فإن صدق أحدهما سلّم ذلك الشيء له.
She belongs to the one she confirms; and if they both dated, the earlier is more entitled. If she confirms one who has no proof, she is his. If the other brings proof, judgment is given for him. If one brings proof and judgment is given for him, then the other brings proof, it is not given for him. [Commentary:] His saying: 'She' means the woman belongs to the one she confirms, because marriage is judged by the spouses' confirmation. This is if the disputed woman is not in possession of the one she denies, and he has not consummated with her. But if she is in the possession of the other, or he consummated, then confirmation is not considered, because it indicates precedence of his contract. As in al-Munah. And if they both dated, meaning the two claimants to her marriage, the earlier is more entitled to her than the other, because there is no opposition in that time, so judgment is for the earlier since the later's contract and proof are invalid, and what was mentioned about possession or consummation is not considered. If only one dated, then she is for the one she confirmed, as in al-Bazzaziyya. If the woman confirms marriage to one who has no proof, she is his, due to their mutual confirmation of marriage, which is established by their mutual confirmation. If the other brings proof, judgment is for the one who brought proof, because proof is stronger than confession; proof is a transitive argument, confession is a limited argument. If one brings proof of marriage and judgment is given for him, then the other brings proof that he married her, judgment is not given for him, because the first judgment is valid and cannot be overturned by something similar, rather it is weaker, due to the connection of judgment to the first and second. Unless his precedence is established, i.e., his marriage to her before the first, then judgment is given for him, due to certainty of error in the first. His saying: 'The earlier is more entitled' as when one has consummated or she is in his possession. Therein: Unless his precedence is established, just as judgment is not given based on the outsider's evidence against the possessor whose marriage is apparent unless he proves his precedence. (If a woman is in possession of a man and his marriage is apparent, and an outsider claims she is his wife and brings evidence, judgment is not given for him unless he proves his marriage is earlier.) If two bring evidence of purchase of something from a possessor, each gets half with half the price, or they may leave it. That is, each has the option: if he wishes, he takes half of that thing with half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Indication that mere claim of precedence suffices, as some shaykhs said. Others held that it is necessary to specify, e.g., the first in Rajab and the second in Sha'ban. Al-Qahistani narrated from al-Khazanah: If one specifies a month and the other an hour, the hour is more entitled. End. His saying: 'Unless his precedence is established' means the precedence of his marriage over the marriage of the possessor with evidence, then judgment is given for him, because explicit evidence is above implication, so it is not considered with it. His saying: 'If two bring evidence' means the two outsiders on purchase of something from a possessor, and they did not date or dated equally; so each gets half of that thing with half the price, if he wishes, and he may claim half the price from the seller. Or he may leave it, i.e., leave the half if he wishes, because when they are equal in the cause, the judge must adjudicate between them, as it is impossible to give all to one. So each has a choice because the condition of his contract has changed; perhaps he desired to own all, so he returns it and takes all the price. It is like two unauthorized agents each sold to a man and the owner ratified both sales; then each has a choice because the condition of his contract changed; perhaps he desired to own all. This is the ruling of Malik in one narration, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and another opinion says lottery, which is also Ahmad's view. From al-Shafi'i also: the proofs are dropped and one refers to the seller; if he confirms one, that thing is handed over to him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (expansion_probe) on a matn from al-Wiqaya concerning conflicting claims. It opens by stating the ruling: when two men claim a woman, she belongs to the one she confirms, provided the other has no contrary possession or consummation. If they both give dates, the earlier marriage is preferred. The commentator explains that marriage is established by mutual acknowledgment, but if the woman is in the possession of one or if there was consummation, her confirmation is not considered because that indicates prior contract. If she confirms one who has no evidence, that claimant gets her because their mutual confession establishes marriage. If the other then brings proof, judgment goes to the proof, as evidence is stronger than confession. However, if judgment has already been passed, another proof later does not overturn it unless the later claimant proves his marriage was earlier. The commentary then compares to a possessor whose marriage is apparent: an outsider claiming her must prove his precedence. Next, the commentator discusses a parallel scenario: two outsiders claim a purchased item from a possessor. If both bring evidence without dates or with equal dates, each gets half and may choose to take or leave the half. This is because they are equal in cause, so the judge splits it. Each has an option due to the changed condition of their contract. The commentator notes a disagreement among later scholars: some say mere claim of precedence suffices, while others require specification of dates. He also relates al-Khahistani's opinion that the more specific time (hour vs. month) takes priority. Finally, he cites the views of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad on the purchase case: Malik and al-Shafi'i (in one view) agree with Hanafi splitting; another view from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad says lottery; another al-Shafi'i view drops both proofs and refers to the seller's confirmation. This reflects the Hanafi preference for splitting when evidence is equal, based on qiyas.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, which is stronger than confession (iqrar) in court.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right, which establishes the claim only against the confessor, not third parties.
سابق (sabiq): The earlier claimant in time, whose contract or right precedes the other's.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a woman confirms one claimant (who has no proof) and the other brings evidence (bayyinah), who gets judgment?
A) The one she confirmed
B) The one with evidence
C) The earlier if dated
D) The one in possession

Key Takeaway
In conflicting marriage claims, the woman's confirmation is decisive only when no possession, consummation, or contrary evidence exists; evidence (bayyinah) overrides confession (iqrar).

Conflicting Purchase Claims: Splitting Evidence and the Option to Take or Leave
Commentary on Hanafi ruling for two claimants to a purchased item with equal evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that when two outsiders each claim purchase of an item from a possessor with equal evidence, the item is split equally with an option to take or leave, and you will be able to compare this with the rulings of other schools.

Core Idea
Equal evidence in competing purchase claims leads to equal sharing with an option, based on qiyas to unauthorized agents.

When two unrelated buyers each claim to have purchased the same item from a seller (possessor), and both bring equal evidence without dating, the judge cannot give the whole to either. The Hanafi school resolves this by splitting the item and price, giving each an option to take or leave the half. This ruling is grounded in qiyas on the case of two unauthorized agents whose sales are ratified. Other schools offer different solutions: lottery or reliance on the seller's confirmation. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the cross-madhhab debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two outsiders (i.e., neither is the current possessor) both claim they purchased the same item from the possessor (seller). They present equal evidence (bayyinah) and either do not give dates or give equal dates.
If one had earlier evidence, he would win; but here evidence is equal.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Each claimant gets half of the item by paying half the price. Additionally, each has the option (khiyar) to either take the half or leave it (i.e., refuse and claim full price from the seller).
The option is due to the change in the contract's condition—they originally contracted for the whole item.
3
Understand the qiyas basis
The ruling is analogized to two unauthorized agents (fuduliyyun) who each sell the same item to a buyer, and the owner ratifies both sales. In that case, each agent has an option to take or leave half the price. Here, the cause (illa) is equality in the cause (istiwa' fi al-sabab), making it impossible to give the whole to one.
The asl (original case) is the ratified dual sale by fuduliyyin; the far' (new case) is the dual purchase claim.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on date specification
Some Hanafi scholars (shaykhs) hold that a mere claim of precedence (without specifying a date) suffices to break the tie. Others require specifying the month or year. The commentary cites al-Qahistani that if one specifies a month and the other an hour, the hour is more entitled.
This difference shows that even within the Hanafi school, there is some flexibility.
5
Compare with other schools' rulings
Malik (in one narration) and al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) agree with splitting. Another Shafi'i opinion and Ahmad say lottery (qur'a). A third Shafi'i opinion drops both proofs and refers to the seller's confirmation. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon ruling for the madhhab.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is significant: splitting vs. lottery vs. seller's word.

Source Text

وهي لمن صدقته وإن أرّخا فالسابق أحق، فإن أقرت من لا حجة له فهي له ، فإن بَرْهَنَ الآخرُ قُضي له، ولو بَرْهَنَ أحدُهما وقُضي له ، ثُمَّ بَرْهَنَ الآخرُ لم يقض له (وهي لمن صدقته وإن أرخا فالسابق أحق ، فإن أقرت من لا حجة له فهي له ، فإِن بَرْهَنَ الآخِرُ قُضي له ، ولو بَرْهَنَ أحدُهما وقُضي له ، ثُمَّ بَرْهَنَا ثُم بَرْهَنَ الآخر لم يقض له [1] [٢ 11 ] قوله : وهي ؛ أي المرأة لمن صدقته ؛ لأنَّ النكاح مما يحكــم بــه بــصادق الزوجين، وهذا إذا لم تكن المرأة المتنازع فيها في يد من كذبته، ولم يكن دخل بها من كذبته، وأما إذا كان في يد الآخر، أو دخل بها فلا اعتبار بالتصديق ؛ لأنه دليل على سبق عقده. كذا في «المنح»). وإن أرخا : أي المدعيان لنكاحها، فالسابق أحق بها من الآخر؛ لأنه لا معارض في هذا الزمان، فيكون القضاء للسابق إذ عقد اللاحق وبرهانه باطل، ولا يعتبر ما ذكر من كونها في يده أو دخل بها ، ولو أرح أحدهما فقط، فإنَّها لن أقرت له ، كما لو أرخ أحدهما، وللآخريد، فإنَّها لذي اليد. كما في «البزازيّة». فإن أقرت المرأة بالزوجية لمن لا حجة فهي ؛ أي المرأة له لتصادقهما على النكاح وهو يثبت بتصادق الزوجين عليه. فإن برهن الآخر؛ أي الذي لم تقرّ له ، قضى له ؛ أي الذي برهن ؛ لأنَّ البينة أقوى من الإقرار، فإنَّ البينة حجّة متعدية، والإقرار حجةٌ قاصرة. ولو برهن أحدهما على النكاح وقضى له؛ أي وقضى القاضـي لـه بـبرهانه بنكاحها. ثم برهن الآخر؛ على أنه نكحها لم يقض له ؛ لأنَّ القضاء الأول قد صح فلا ينقض بما هو مثله ، بل هو دونه ؛ لاتصال القضاء بالأول والثاني. إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ؛ أي سبق نكاحه إيَّاها على نكاح الأول، فحينئذ يقضى له ؛ لتيقن الخطأ في الأول. [٢] قوله : فالسابق أحق ؛ كما إذا دخل أحدهما بها، أو كانت في يده، وفيه إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ، كما لم يقض بحجة الخارج على ذي يدٍ ظَهَرَ نَكَاحُهُ إِلا إذا أثبت سبقه (إذا كانت امرأة في يد رجل ونكاحه ظاهر، وادعى الخارج أنَّها زوجته، وأقام البيئة لم يقض له إلا إذا أثبت أن نكاحه سابق. فإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء من ذي يد، فلكل نصفه بنصف الثّمن، أو تَرْكُه) : أي لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما الخيار إن شاء أخذ نصف ذلك الشَّيء بنصف الثَّمن، وإن شاءَ تَرَك إشعار بأن مجرد دعوى السبق يكفي كما قال بعض المشايخ، وذهب آخرون إلى أنه لا بُدَّ من بيان] نحو إن الأول في رجب، والثاني في شعبان وتمامه في «الفصول العمادية»، وذكر القهستاني(۱) نقلاً عن «الخزانة» : لو وقت أحدهما شهراً والآخر ساعة، فالساعة أولى. انتهى. [١]قوله : إلا إذا ثبت سبقه ؛ أي سبق نكاحه على نكاح ذي اليد بالبينة، فإنّه يقضي له ؛ لأنَّ التصريح فوق الدلالة، فلا تعتبر معه. [٢] قوله : فإن برهنا ؛ أي الخارجان على شراء شيء ؛ أي تمام لشراء شيء. من ذي يد؛ ولم يؤرخا أو أرخا على السواء. فلكل نصفه ؛ أي لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما نصف ذلك الشيء بنصف الثمن إن شاء ورجع به على البائع بنصف ثمنه. أو تركه ؛ أي ترك النصف إن شاء ؛ لأنهما لما استويا في السبب وجب على القاضي أن يقضي به بينهما ؛ لتعذر القضاء بكلّه، فيتخيرُ كل منهما لتغير شرط عقده عليه ؛ فلعل رغبته في تملك الكلّ ، فيرده ويأخذ كل الثمن، فصار كفضوليين باع كل منهما من رجل، وأجاز المالك البيعين، فإنَّ كلاً منهما يتخير ؛ لأنه تغير عليه شرط عقده، فلعل رغبته في تملك الكل. وبه قال مالك له في رواية والشافعي الله في قول، وقال في قول آخر : يقرع، وبه قال أحمد ، وعن الشافعي الله أيضاً : تسقط البينات، ويرجع إلى البائع، فإن صدق أحدهما سلّم ذلك الشيء له.
She belongs to the one she confirms; and if they both dated, the earlier is more entitled. If she confirms one who has no proof, she is his. If the other brings proof, judgment is given for him. If one brings proof and judgment is given for him, then the other brings proof, it is not given for him. [Commentary:] His saying: 'She' means the woman belongs to the one she confirms, because marriage is judged by the spouses' confirmation. This is if the disputed woman is not in possession of the one she denies, and he has not consummated with her. But if she is in the possession of the other, or he consummated, then confirmation is not considered, because it indicates precedence of his contract. As in al-Munah. And if they both dated, meaning the two claimants to her marriage, the earlier is more entitled to her than the other, because there is no opposition in that time, so judgment is for the earlier since the later's contract and proof are invalid, and what was mentioned about possession or consummation is not considered. If only one dated, then she is for the one she confirmed, as in al-Bazzaziyya. If the woman confirms marriage to one who has no proof, she is his, due to their mutual confirmation of marriage, which is established by their mutual confirmation. If the other brings proof, judgment is for the one who brought proof, because proof is stronger than confession; proof is a transitive argument, confession is a limited argument. If one brings proof of marriage and judgment is given for him, then the other brings proof that he married her, judgment is not given for him, because the first judgment is valid and cannot be overturned by something similar, rather it is weaker, due to the connection of judgment to the first and second. Unless his precedence is established, i.e., his marriage to her before the first, then judgment is given for him, due to certainty of error in the first. His saying: 'The earlier is more entitled' as when one has consummated or she is in his possession. Therein: Unless his precedence is established, just as judgment is not given based on the outsider's evidence against the possessor whose marriage is apparent unless he proves his precedence. (If a woman is in possession of a man and his marriage is apparent, and an outsider claims she is his wife and brings evidence, judgment is not given for him unless he proves his marriage is earlier.) If two bring evidence of purchase of something from a possessor, each gets half with half the price, or they may leave it. That is, each has the option: if he wishes, he takes half of that thing with half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Indication that mere claim of precedence suffices, as some shaykhs said. Others held that it is necessary to specify, e.g., the first in Rajab and the second in Sha'ban. Al-Qahistani narrated from al-Khazanah: If one specifies a month and the other an hour, the hour is more entitled. End. His saying: 'Unless his precedence is established' means the precedence of his marriage over the marriage of the possessor with evidence, then judgment is given for him, because explicit evidence is above implication, so it is not considered with it. His saying: 'If two bring evidence' means the two outsiders on purchase of something from a possessor, and they did not date or dated equally; so each gets half of that thing with half the price, if he wishes, and he may claim half the price from the seller. Or he may leave it, i.e., leave the half if he wishes, because when they are equal in the cause, the judge must adjudicate between them, as it is impossible to give all to one. So each has a choice because the condition of his contract has changed; perhaps he desired to own all, so he returns it and takes all the price. It is like two unauthorized agents each sold to a man and the owner ratified both sales; then each has a choice because the condition of his contract changed; perhaps he desired to own all. This is the ruling of Malik in one narration, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and another opinion says lottery, which is also Ahmad's view. From al-Shafi'i also: the proofs are dropped and one refers to the seller; if he confirms one, that thing is handed over to him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (expansion_probe) on a matn from al-Wiqaya concerning conflicting claims. It opens by stating the ruling: when two men claim a woman, she belongs to the one she confirms, provided the other has no contrary possession or consummation. If they both give dates, the earlier marriage is preferred. The commentator explains that marriage is established by mutual acknowledgment, but if the woman is in the possession of one or if there was consummation, her confirmation is not considered because that indicates prior contract. If she confirms one who has no evidence, that claimant gets her because their mutual confession establishes marriage. If the other then brings proof, judgment goes to the proof, as evidence is stronger than confession. However, if judgment has already been passed, another proof later does not overturn it unless the later claimant proves his marriage was earlier. The commentary then compares to a possessor whose marriage is apparent: an outsider claiming her must prove his precedence. Next, the commentator discusses a parallel scenario: two outsiders claim a purchased item from a possessor. If both bring evidence without dates or with equal dates, each gets half and may choose to take or leave the half. This is because they are equal in cause, so the judge splits it. Each has an option due to the changed condition of their contract. The commentator notes a disagreement among later scholars: some say mere claim of precedence suffices, while others require specification of dates. He also relates al-Khahistani's opinion that the more specific time (hour vs. month) takes priority. Finally, he cites the views of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad on the purchase case: Malik and al-Shafi'i (in one view) agree with Hanafi splitting; another view from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad says lottery; another al-Shafi'i view drops both proofs and refers to the seller's confirmation. This reflects the Hanafi preference for splitting when evidence is equal, based on qiyas.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, which is stronger than confession (iqrar) in court.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right, which establishes the claim only against the confessor, not third parties.
سابق (sabiq): The earlier claimant in time, whose contract or right precedes the other's.
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized agent who acts without prior permission but may be ratified later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what is the consequence if two outsiders bring equal evidence of purchase from a possessor without dating?
A) The earlier claimant wins
B) Each gets half with an option
C) The judge draws lots
D) The item is returned to the seller

Key Takeaway
When two claimants present equal evidence for the same purchased item, Hanafi law splits the item equally and grants each an option to take or leave, based on qiyas on ratified sales by two unauthorized agents.

Abandonment After Judgment in Two-Buyer Claim
Ruling on one buyer abandoning claim after judge divides property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on abandonment after judgment in a two-buyer claim and explain the rational proof and analogy to pre-emption.

Core Idea
After judgment, abandonment does not give the whole; the judge's division is final.

When two claimants both prove they bought the same item, the Hanafi school divides it equally. A critical question: what if one abandons his share after the judge's ruling? This lesson explains why the other claimant only keeps his half, the rational basis for this, and how it parallels the law of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Two buyers each present evidence of purchase; judge divides equally. After division, one abandons his claim. The ruling: the other does not take the whole, only his half.
This is the core rule.
2
Understand the rational proof
The buyer's consent was conditional on receiving the entire item. Splitting the transaction vitiates his consent, giving him an option to rescind. Since abandonment occurs after judgment, the sale is already rescinded in half.
Proof based on buyer's implicit condition.
3
Respond to the objection
The opponent argues that two sales of the same item at the same time are impossible, so both evidences are false. The Hanafi response: witnesses testify to the act of sale, not its validity; simultaneous sales can occur if the owner appointed two independent agents.
This shows the possibility of two valid sales.
4
Apply the analogy to pre-emption
In shuf'a, if one pre-emptor surrenders before judgment, the other takes the whole; if after, only half. The same logic applies here: the division is necessitated by judgment, and abandonment after cannot revive the whole right.
Strengthens the ruling by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبترك أحدهما بعدما قَضَى لهما لم يأخذ الآخر كله. وهو للسابق إن أرخا
ولنا : أن كل واحدٍ ما رضي بالعقد إلا ليُسَلَّمَ له كل المبيع، فإذا لم يُسَلِّم له اختل رضاه بتفريق الصفقة عليه، فيتخير كما قلنا.
فإن قيل : ينبغي أن تسقط البينتان عندنا أيضاً ؛ لتيقن القاضي بكذب أحدِ الفريقين ؛ لعدم تصور البيعين في شيء واحدٍ بكماله من كل واحد من رجلين في وقت واحد.
أجيب : بأنّ كل فريق شهد بنفس البيع لا بصحته، ويتصوّر وقوع البيعين في وقت واحد بأن وكل المالك رجلين كل واحد على الإنفراد، فباعا معاً، ذكـره الشمني، وغيره.
[١] قوله : بعد ما قضى ؛ قيَّد بقوله : ما بعد القضاء ؛ لأنه قبل القضاء لا يأخذ كله ؛ لإثبات أنه اشتراء الكل بلا مزاحم للقضاء.
[٢] قوله : لم يأخذ الآخر كلّه ؛ لأنه صار في النصف مقضيّاً عليه، فانفسخ البيع فيه، وإنما قلنا ذلك ؛ لأنه ببينته استحق جميعه، وكان يُسَلّم له لولا بينةُ صاحبه، ولما قضى القاضي بينهما صار مستحقاً عليه، وانفسخ البيع في النصف، فلا يكون له أن يأخذه بعد الانفساخ. بخلاف ما لو ترك أحدهما قبل القضاء به بينهما حيث يكون للآخر أن يأخذ جميعه ؛ لأنه أثبت ببينة أنه اشترى الكلّ، وإنّما يرجع إلى النصف بالمزاحمة ضرورةً القضاء به بينهما ولم يوجد.
فصار نظير تسليم أحد الشفيعين فإنّه إن كان قبل القضاء فللآخر أن يأخذ كل المبيع، وإن كان بعده فليس له أن يأخذ إلا النصف ؛ لأنه يستحق بالسبب كله ، والانقسام للمزاحمة ضرورة القضاء به بينهما.
And if one of them abandons after the judge has ruled for both, the other does not take the whole. And it is for the one who is earlier if they gave dates. Our proof: that each one only consented to the contract on condition that the entire sold item be delivered to him; if it is not delivered to him, his consent is vitiated by splitting the transaction, so he has an option as we said. If it is said: both evidences should be invalidated with us too because the judge is certain that one of the two parties is lying due to the impossibility of two sales of the entire same item from one man to two different men at the same time. The answer: every party testifies to the same sale, not to its validity, and it is possible for two sales to occur at the same time if the owner appointed two agents independently and they sold simultaneously. [Shumunni and others mentioned this]. [1] His statement: 'after he has judged' – he restricted it to after the judgment because before the judgment he does not take the whole because he proved purchase of the whole without competition due to judgment. [2] His statement: 'the other does not take the whole' – because he has been adjudged against in half, so the sale is rescinded in that half. We only say this because by his evidence he proved entitlement to all of it, and it would have been delivered to him but for the other's evidence; when the judge ruled between them, he became subject to entitlement against him, and the sale was rescinded in half, so he cannot take it after rescission. Unlike if one abandons before judgment between them, where the other takes the whole, because he proved by evidence that he bought the whole, and he only returns to half due to competition necessitated by the judgment between them, which did not occur. This becomes analogous to the surrender of one of the two pre-emptors: if it is before judgment, the other takes the entire sold property; if after, he only takes half, because he deserves the whole by cause, and the division is for competition necessitated by the judgment between them.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) in the Hanafi school. It states that when two claimants each present evidence that they bought the same item from the same owner, the judge divides the item equally between them. Then, if one of them abandons his claim after the judge has issued his ruling, the other claimant does not take the entire item; he only takes half. The reason is that after the judge's ruling, the sale is rescinded in the other half due to the competing evidence. Before the ruling, if one abandons, the other takes the whole because he proved ownership of the entire item without competition. The author provides rational proof: each buyer only consented to the contract on the condition that he receive the entire item; splitting the transaction violates his consent, giving him an option. The objection that both evidences should be invalid because two complete sales of the same item at the same time are impossible is answered: the witnesses testify to the sale itself, not its validity, and two sales can occur simultaneously if the owner appointed two agents who sold together. The commentary concludes by analogizing to pre-emption (shuf'a): surrender before judgment gives the other the whole; after judgment, only half. Note: The matn originally included both rulings, but this lesson focuses on the first part. The proof is rational (istihsan/qiyas) based on the buyer's consent.

Key Terms
بعد القضاء (ba‘d al-qadā’): After the judge has issued his final ruling dividing the property between the claimants.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses, proving the claim.
شفيع (shafī‘): Pre-emptor in property, who has the right to take the sold property from the buyer under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two claimants both prove purchase of the same item, and the judge divides it between them, then one claimant abandons his share after the judgment, what does the other claimant take?
A) The entire item
B) Only the half that was not abandoned
C) Nothing, because the judgment is invalidated
D) He must start a new case

Key Takeaway
Abandonment after judgment in a two-buyer claim does not transfer the whole to the other claimant; the judge's division is final and the abandoned half is not reclaimed.

Priority by Earlier Date and Definition of Tārīkh
Ruling on earlier date giving priority when both claimants specify dates, plus definition of tārīkh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on when an earlier date gives priority in competing purchase claims and explain the definition and origin of tārīkh (dating) in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
An earlier date gives priority only when both claimants buy from the same seller; not when sellers differ.

This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitāb al-Da‘wā) presents two key elements: first, the legal ruling on how dating affects priority between two buyers who claim purchase from the same seller; second, the lexical and historical background of the concept of tārīkh (dating). The ruling clarifies that providing an earlier date matters only when the seller is the same person. The lexical section defines tārīkh and recounts how the Islamic calendar was established under Caliph ‘Umar. Understanding both parts helps you apply the rule correctly and appreciate the significance of dating in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states 'وهو للسابق إن أرخا' meaning 'And it is for the one who is earlier if they gave dates.' The commentary explains that when two claimants both claim purchase from the same possessor, the one who proves an earlier purchase wins the property.
Both must have specified dates (أرخا) for the ruling to apply.
2
Understand the exception
The text immediately distinguishes the contrary case: if each claims from a different seller, the earlier date gives no priority. This is because each claimant is advocating for his seller's ownership, which itself has no date.
Look for the key word 'بخلاف' (contrary) introducing the exception.
3
Learn the definition and history of tārīkh
The passage provides a detailed definition of tārīkh from lexicography and juristic usage, including variants and the story of ‘Umar ibn al-Khattāb establishing the Hijri calendar after confusion over a document dated only to 'Sha‘bān'.
The calendar begins with Muharram and uses lunar months based on night sighting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو للسابق إن أرخا
[٣]قوله : وهو للسابق إن أرخا ؛ لأنهما لَمَّا ادَّعيا الشراء من ذي اليد اتفقا على أنه مالك للمبيع، ثم أثبت أحدهما الشراء منه في وقت لا ينازعه فيه أحد، فاندفع به الآخر.
بخلاف ما إذا ادعى كلُّ واحدٍ منهما الشراء من رجل آخر غير الذي يدعي الشراء منه الآخر، حيث لا يرجّح فيه صاحب التاريخ الأسبق ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما فيه خصم عن بائعه في إثبات الملك له، وملك بائعهما لا تاريخ فيه، فصار كأن البائعين حضرا وأثبتا الملك لأنفسهما مطلقاً من غير تاريخ.
[١] قوله: تاريخاً ؛ قيل : التاريخ قلب التأخير، وقيل : معــرب مـاه وروز، واصطلاحاً : تعريف وقت الشيء بأن يسند إلى وقتِ حدوث أمر شائع ؛ لظهور ملةٍ أو دولة أو غيره : كطوفان وزلزلة ، لينسب إلى ذلك الوقت الزمانُ الآتي. وقيل : هو يوم معلوم نسب إليه ذلك الزمان ، وقيل : هو مدة معلومة بين حدوث أمر ظاهر، وبين أوقاتِ حوادث أخر. ذكره القُهستاني (۱) نقلاً عن نهاية الادراك». وقال في «المصباح» (۲) : أرّختُ الكتاب بالتثقيل في الأشهر، والتخفيف لغةً حكاها ابن القطاع : إذا جعلت له تاريخاً، وهو بيان انتهاء وقته، وهو معرب ، وقيل : عربي ويقال : وَرَّخْتُ على البدل ، والتَّوريخ قليل الاستعمال ، وأرخْتُ البينة : إذ جعلتُ لها وذكرت تاريخاً، وأطلقت ؛ أي لم تذكره
وسبب وضع التاريخ أوّل الإسلام أنَّ عمر بن الخطاب له أتى بصك مكتوب إلى شعبان، فقال : أهو شعبان الماضي أو شعبان القابل، ثم أمر بوضع التاريخ، واتفقت الصحابة على ابتداء التاريخ من هجرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى المدينة، وجعلوا أول السنة المحرم ، ويعتبر التاريخ بالليالي ؛ لأنَّ الليل عند العرب سابق على النهار ؛ لأنهم كانوا أميين لا يحسنون الكتابة، ولم يعرفوا حساب غيرهم من الأمم ، فتمسكوا بظهور الهلال، وإنّما يظهر بالليل فجعلوه ابتداء التاريخ. انتهى.
And it is for the one who is earlier if they gave dates. [3] His statement: 'And it is for the one who is earlier if they gave dates' – because when both claimed purchase from the same person in possession, they agreed that he is the owner of the sold item; then one of them proved purchase from him at a time when no one disputed with him, so the other is repelled by him. Contrary to the case where each claims purchase from a different man other than the one the other claims from: in that case, the one with the earlier date is not given priority because each is an advocate for his seller in proving ownership for him, and the ownership of both sellers has no date, so it is as if the two sellers appeared and proved ownership for themselves absolutely without a date. [1] His statement: 'a date' – it is said that al-tārīkh is from ta’khīr (postponing), and it is said it is an arabicised form of māh rūz (month and day); terminologically, it is the definition of the time of something by attributing it to the time of a well-known event, such as the emergence of a religion or state or other like a flood or earthquake, so that the future time is attributed to that time. It is also said: it is a known day to which that time is attributed; and it is said: it is a known period between the occurrence of an apparent event and other times. Al-Quhustani mentioned this from 'Nihāyat al-Idrāk'. And in 'al-Misbāh' it says: I dated the book (arrakhtu) with tathqīl (doubling) in common usage, and takhfīf (lightening) is a language variant reported by Ibn al-Qatta‘: if one gives it a date, it is an explanation of its time expiration; it is arabicised, and some say it is Arabic; and they say warrakhtu as a replacement; al-tawrīkh is rarely used; and arkhatu al-bayyinah means I gave it a date and mentioned a date, or mutlaq (unrestricted) meaning I did not mention it. And the reason for establishing dating in early Islam: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may God be pleased with him) was presented with a document written to Sha‘ban, so he asked: is it the Sha‘ban of the past or the coming Sha‘ban? Then he ordered the establishment of dating. The Companions agreed to begin dating from the Hijra of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Medina, and they made Muharram the first of the year. They considered dating by nights because with the Arabs night precedes day, as they were illiterate and did not know how to write, and did not know the reckoning of other nations, so they adhered to the sighting of the crescent moon, and it appears only at night, so they made it the beginning of dating. End.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and lexical explanation from the Book of Claims. The matn states: 'And it is for the one who is earlier if they gave dates.' The commentary explains that when two claimants both claim purchase from the same seller (dhū al-yad), they agree he is the owner. One of them proves he bought from him at an earlier time when no one else disputed, so he takes the whole property, repelling the other claimant. However, if each claims purchase from different sellers, the earlier date does not give priority because each is acting on behalf of his own seller, and the sellers' ownership is not dated. The passage then provides a detailed definition of 'tārīkh' (dating) lexically and terminologically, including its etymology (Arabicized from Persian 'māh rūz'), its technical meaning as assigning a time to a known event, and variants. It ends with the historical origin of the Islamic calendar: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab encountered confusion from a document dated only to Sha'ban, so he consulted the Companions, who agreed to begin the calendar from the Hijra and start the year with Muharram, using lunar months based on night sightings. This is a complete legal unit covering the ruling on priority by date and the concept of dating itself.

Key Terms
أرخا (arkhā): They gave dates (specified the time of purchase) in their claims.
تاريخ (tārīkh): The dating of an event or document; the etymological and technical definition given in the text.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person in possession of the property (the seller or the current owner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When both claim from the same seller
B) When both claim from different sellers
C) Always, regardless of seller
D) Only if there is no other evidence

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an earlier purchase date gives priority only when both claimants bought from the same seller, not when sellers differ.

Preference Among Claimants: Date, Possession, and Time
Ruling on preference between two claimants of purchase from the same seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy of evidence (earlier date > possession > specified time > equal division) to determine which claimant of a purchase prevails in a judicial dispute.

Core Idea
When two claimants both prove purchase from the same seller, priority goes to earlier date, then possessor, then specific time.

Judging competing claims of purchase requires a clear order of preference. The Hanafi school has established a hierarchy based on three factors: a precise date (tārīkh), current possession (yad), and a specified time (waqt). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the ruling and the rationale, including an important intra-madhhab discussion about the nature of possession in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the nature of the dispute
Two claimants both present evidence (bayyinah) that they purchased a specific item from the same seller who currently holds the item. Determine who is the original seller and that the claimants are outsiders to possession.
The case assumes both claimants are outsiders to possession; the seller is the current possessor.
2
Classify the factors presented by each claimant
For each claimant, check whether they have: (a) a full date (year, month, day), (b) current physical possession of the item, or (c) a specified time of purchase (e.g., 'at dawn' without a date).
A date is stronger than a specified time because it pins the transaction to a precise timeline.
3
Apply the hierarchy of preference
If both have dates, the earlier date prevails. If only one has a date or neither has, but one has possession, the possessor wins. If neither has possession and only one specifies a time, that claimant wins. If none of these, they share equally.
The hierarchy: earlier date > possession > specified time > equal division.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on possession
Note that the base text (al-Kitab) suggests possession always beats a later-dated claim, but the Hidayah clarifies that possession alone gives priority only when the other's date is not earlier. Additionally, a nuance arises: the possession might be proven past possession, not current hand-to-hand control.
The resolution from al-Mi‘raj: possession here means proven prior seizure, not current possession, so the case remains two outsiders.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a court strictly follows al-Kitab, the possessor always wins except when the other's date is explicitly earlier. The mufta bihi view (Hidayah) gives possessor priority only when no earlier date exists, and it treats possession as a strong but not absolute indicator.
The practical outcome in a specific case depends on which riwayah the judge adopts.

Source Text

(۱) في «جامع الرموز (۲ : ۲۷۲). (۲) «المصباح المنير» (ص ۱۱ - ۱۲). ولذي يد إن لم يؤرخا ، أو أرّخ ،أحدهما ، ولذي وقت إن وقت أحدهما فقط ولا يد لهما (ولذي يد إن لم يؤرخا ، أو أرّخ أحدهما ، ولذي وقت إن وقت أحدهما فقط ولا يد لهما) : أي إن أرخا فالسابق أحقُّ ، وإن لم يؤرخا ، أو أرخ أحدهما ، فإن كان في يد أحدهما فذو اليد أولى، وإن لم يكن في يد أحدهما، فإن وقت أحدهما ، فهو أحق، وإن لم يوقت أحدهما فقد مر أن لكل نصفه بنصف الثّمن ، أو تركه [1]قوله : ولذي يد إن لم يؤرخا أو أرخ أحدهما ؛ لأن تمكنه من قبضه دليل على سبق شرائه ؛ لأنهما استويا في الإثبات، فلا تنتقض اليد الثابتة بالشك، قال في «البحر»(۱) : وظاهر «الكتاب» كما صرح به في «المحيط» تقديم صاحب القبض سواء أرخا واستوى تاريخهما ، أو لم يؤرخا ، أو أرخت إحداهما فقط، وإنما يتأخر صاحبه إذا سبق تاريخ غيره ؛ لأن الصريح يفوق الدلالة. ثم قال : ولي إشكال في عبارة «الكتاب» وهو أن أصل المسألة مفروضة في خارجين تنازعا في يد ثالث ، فإذا كان مع أحدهما قبض كان ذا يد تنازع مع خارج ، فلم تكن المسألة. ثم رأيتُ في «المعراج ما يزيلُهُ من جواز أن يراد أنه أثبت بالبينة قبضه فيما مضى من الزمان، وهو الآن في يد البائع. انتهى. إلا أنه يشكل بما ذكره بعده عن أن ثبوت اليد لأحدهما بالمعاينة. انتهى ما في «البحر». Book of Claims/Chapter on the Claim of Two Men: i.e., they mention purchase from the possessor with a date. That he is the owner of the sold item, then one of them proved purchase from him at a time when no one contested him, so the other is repelled. Unlike the case where each of them claims purchase from another man other than the one from whom the other claims purchase, where the one with the earlier date is not given preference; because each of them in that case is an opponent for his seller in establishing ownership for him, and the ownership of their sellers has no date, so it is as if the two sellers had appeared and established ownership for themselves absolutely without a date. [1] His statement: 'with a date' – it was said: al-tārīkh is the inversion of al-taʼkhīr (postponement), and it was said: it is an arabicized word from Māh wa Rūz (Month and Day in Persian), and terminologically: it is the definition of the time of a thing by attributing it to the time of occurrence of a well-known event, such as the appearance of a religious community, a state, or other things like a flood or earthquake, so that the subsequent time is attributed to that time. It was also said: it is a known day to which that time is attributed, and it was said: it is a known duration between the occurrence of a manifest event and the times of other events. Al-Quhistani mentioned it quoting from Nihāyat al-Idrāk. It is said in al-Miṣbāḥ: 'I dated the book' with tathqīl in well-known usage, and takhfīf is a language form narrated by Ibn al-Qaṭṭāʻ: when you set a date for it, and it is the statement of the end of its time, and it is arabicized, and it is said: it is Arabic, and it is said: warrakhtu as a substitute, and al-tawrīkh is seldom used, and 'arkhatu al-bayyinah' means I set for it and mentioned a date, and 'aṭlaqtu' means I did not mention it. The reason for establishing the dating system at the beginning of Islam is that ʻUmar ibn al-Khaṭṭāb – when a document was brought to him written up to Sha‘bān, he said: Is it the past Sha‘bān or the coming Sha‘bān? Then he ordered the establishment of dating, and the Companions agreed to begin dating from the migration of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Medina, and they made the first of the year the month of Muḥarram. Dating is considered by nights because night precedes day among the Arabs, as they were illiterate and did not know how to write, and they did not know the calculation of other nations, so they adhered to the appearance of the crescent, and it appears at night, so they made it the beginning of dating. End. And whoever is in possession [prevails] if they did not date, or only one dated, and whoever has a specific time [prevails] if only one specified a time and neither has possession. (And whoever is in possession [prevails] if they did not date, or only one dated, and whoever has a specific time [prevails] if only one specified a time and neither has possession): i.e., if they both dated, the earlier is more entitled; if they did not date, or only one dated, then if the item is in someone's possession, the possessor has priority; if it is not in anyone's possession, then if one specified a time, he is more entitled; if neither specified a time, then it has already passed that each gets half for half the price, or he leaves it. [1] His statement: 'whoever is in possession [prevails] if they did not date or only one dated' – because his ability to seize it is evidence of the precedence of his purchase; because they are equal in proof, so the established possession is not overridden by doubt. He said in al-Baḥr: The apparent meaning of al-Kitāb, as stated in al-Muḥīṭ, is to give precedence to the one with possession whether they both dated and their dates are equal, or they did not date, or only one of them dated; his case is only delayed if another's date precedes his, because the explicit overrides the implied. Then he said: I have an issue with the wording of al-Kitāb, which is that the origin of the case is assumed to be two outsiders disputing over a third party's possession; so if one of them had possession, he would be a possessor disputing with an outsider, so the case would not be [as assumed]. Then I saw in al-Miʻrāj what removes it, of the possibility that it is meant that he proved by evidence that he seized it in the past, and now it is in the seller's hand. End. But it is problematic by what he mentioned afterwards regarding that the establishment of possession for one of them is by direct observation. End of what is in al-Baḥr. And he said in al-Minaḥ after quoting this statement: The truth is that it is another case, and it should have been treated separately; its summary is: an outsider and a possessor each claimed purchase from a third party, and they both presented evidence; the possessor is given precedence in three scenarios, and the outsider in one scenario. End. [2] His statement: 'whoever has a specific time' etc. – because ownership is established at that time, and it is possible that the other was before or after, so judgment is not given based on doubt. Thus in al-Hidāyah.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the Hidayah, addressing the rules of evidence when two claimants both claim to have purchased an item from the same owner, but differ in dates, possession, or a stated time. The passage first presents the core ruling: if both claimants produce evidence (bayyinah) that they purchased the item from the current possessor (dhu al-yad), and one of them proves the purchase occurred at a time when no one contested his ownership, that claimant is given preference and the other is dismissed. However, if each claims purchase from a different owner, then the dates become irrelevant because each claimant is essentially establishing ownership for his own seller, and since the sellers' ownership is undated, no priority is given based on date. The text then provides a detailed lexical and technical definition of 'date' (tārīkh), explaining its origins as a Persian loanword, its terminological meaning as the attribution of a time to a well-known event, and its historical establishment under Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab, when the Islamic calendar was set starting from the Hijrah and the month of Muharram. The ruling is then restated in more detail: if both claimants date their transactions, the earlier date prevails; if neither dates or only one dates, the one currently in possession wins; if neither has possession, the one who can specify a time (waqt) prevails; if none specify, the item is shared equally or left. The commentary from al-Bahr and al-Minaḥ clarifies a subtle point: the apparent meaning of the original text (al-Kitab) suggests that possession always gives priority except when the other's date is earlier, but this is contested because the case presupposes two outsiders disputing over an item in a third party's hands (the original seller). A resolution is offered: the 'possession' here might mean past possession proven by evidence, not current hand-to-hand possession. The passage concludes by noting the rationale for giving priority to the one with a specified time: it establishes ownership at that time, whereas the opponent's claim could be before or after, so doubt prevents judgment against the one with a definite time.

Key Terms
تاريخ (tārīkh): A date, defined as attributing a time to a well-known event; in legal terms, it is the specification of the year, month, and day of a transaction or event.
يد (yad): Possession; physical control over an item that serves as evidence of ownership or prior purchase, especially when dates are absent or equal.
وقت (waqt): A specific time; when a claimant specifies the exact time of purchase, it can give priority if the other claimant does not specify and neither has possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
In a dispute between two claimants of purchase from the same seller, the Hanafi hierarchy for preference is: earlier date > possession > specified time > equal division.

Priority of Possession and Dating in Purchase Claims
Ruling on priority between two purchase claimants with possession and dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of priority based on possession (yad) and dating (ta'rikh) to resolve purchase disputes between two claimants.

Core Idea
Possession signals prior purchase, but an explicit earlier date overrides that signal.

When two people both claim to have bought the same item from the same seller, how should a judge decide? The Hanafi school provides clear rules based on two factors: who currently holds the item (yad) and who has a dated document (ta'rikh). This lesson unpacks these rules and the reasoning behind them, including a key textual nuance discussed by later commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factors
The passage centers on two pieces of evidence: physical possession (yad) and a documented date (ta'rikh). Understand that each carries presumptive weight in court.
Possession is a strong presumption (zann ghalib); dating provides explicit proof (sarih).
2
Analyze scenario with possession
If one claimant has possession, the rule says: give priority to the possessor when neither has a date, or when only one of them has a date (regardless of who). The possessor only loses if the other’s date is specifically earlier.
The possessor wins by default because possession indicates prior purchase.
3
Analyze scenario without possession
If neither has possession but only one has a specific date, that date wins. The other's claim is uncertain and cannot override the explicit time.
This is the second rule: 'and for the one with a date if only one dated and neither has possession.'
4
Understand the rationale
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): possession is a strong indicator of ownership because one typically does not let go of one’s property without reason. A date is explicit and resolves uncertainty.
The text uses the phrase 'because his ability to take possession indicates prior purchase.'
5
Resolve textual nuance
The commentary addresses an apparent inconsistency: the original case assumes both claimants are outsiders, but if one proves prior possession by evidence, he becomes a possessor. This refines the application.
This nuance ensures the rule works even when the item is currently in a third party's hand.

ولذي يد إن لم يؤرخا ، أو أرّخ أحدهما ، ولذي وقت إن وقت أحدهما فقط ولا يد لهما

قوله : ولذي يد إن لم يؤرخا أو أرخ أحدهما ؛ لأن تمكنه من قبضه دليل على سبق شرائه ؛ لأنهما استويا في الإثبات، فلا تنتقض اليد الثابتة بالشك، قال في «البحر» : وظاهر «الكتاب» كما صرح به في «المحيط» تقديم صاحب القبض سواء أرخا واستوى تاريخهما ، أو لم يؤرخا ، أو أرخت إحداهما فقط، وإنما يتأخر صاحبه إذا سبق تاريخ غيره ؛ لأن الصريح يفوق الدلالة.
ثم قال : ولي إشكال في عبارة «الكتاب» وهو أن أصل المسألة مفروضة في خارجين تنازعا في يد ثالث ، فإذا كان مع أحدهما قبض كان ذا يد تنازع مع خارج ، فلم تكن المسألة.
ثم رأيتُ في «المعراج ما يزيلُهُ من جواز أن يراد أنه أثبت بالبينة قبضه فيما مضى من الزمان، وهو الآن في يد البائع. انتهى. إلا أنه يشكل بما ذكره بعده عن أن ثبوت اليد لأحدهما بالمعاينة. انتهى ما في «البحر».
وقال في «المنح» بعد نقل هذه العبارة والحق أنها مسألة أخرى، وكان ينبغى إفرادها، وحاصلها : إن خارجاً وذا يد ادّعى كل الشراء من ثالث، وبَرْهَنَا قُدِّمَ ذو اليد في الوجوه الثلاثة، والخارج في وجه واحد. انتهى
قوله : ولذي وقت...الخ ؛ لثبوت الملك في ذلك الوقت واحتمل الآخر أن يكون قبله أو بعده ، فلا يقضي له بالشك. كذا في «الهداية».
And for the one with possession if they did not date, or one of them dated, and for the one with a date if only one of them dated and there is no possession for either: the commentary explains: His statement: 'And for the one with possession if they did not date, or one of them dated' because his ability to take possession indicates prior purchase, since they are equal in proof, so the established possession is not nullified by doubt. He said in al-Bahr: The apparent meaning of the Book as stated in al-Muhit is to give priority to the one with possession, whether they dated and their dates equal, or they did not date, or only one dated; his claim is only postponed if the other's date is earlier, because explicit evidence outweighs implication. Then he said: I have an issue with the wording of the Book, which is that the original case is assumed to be two outsiders disputing over the hand of a third party; if one has possession, he becomes a possessor disputing with an outsider, so the case is not the same. Then I saw in al-Mi'raj what removes the issue: that it is permissible to mean that he proved possession by evidence at a past time, and now the item is in the hand of the seller. Except that what he mentioned later about the hand being established for one of them by direct observation creates difficulty. End of quote from al-Bahr. And he said in al-Minh after quoting this statement: The correct view is that this is another case; it should have been separate. Its summary: an outsider and a possessor each claim purchase from a third party and present evidence; the possessor is given priority in three scenarios, the outsider in one. End. His statement: 'And for the one with a date...' because ownership is established at that time, and the other might have been before or after, so he is not judged by doubt. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on a crucial rule in litigation: when two claimants both assert they purchased the same item from the same seller, how does the judge decide? The base text gives two principles: (1) If neither claimant has a dated document or only one has a date, the claimant who currently possesses the item is given priority. (2) If neither has possession but one has a specific date, that claimant is given priority. The reasoning is that de facto possession is a strong indicator of prior purchase, as it is unlikely someone would allow their property to be in the hands of another. Similarly, a specific date establishes a clear point in time, making the other's claim uncertain. The commentary also discusses an objection about the scenario where both claimants are outsiders (no possession) and the item is with a third party; the resolution is that the one who proves prior possession (by evidence) effectively becomes a possessor. The ruling is firmly rooted in Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Physical possession or control over an item; a jurisprudential indicator of ownership in disputes.
تاريخ (ta'rikh): A date or timestamp on a document of proof (e.g., a sale deed).
قبض (qabd): Physical taking of possession; here it refers to the claimant having or proving prior possession of the disputed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute where two people claim to have purchased the same item from the same seller, and neither has a date nor possession, but one has a specific time (e.g., 'I bought it on Friday'), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) They share equally
B) The one with the specific time is preferred
C) The one who proves the earlier date wins
D) The judge decides by lot

Key Takeaway
In purchase disputes, the Hanafi school prioritizes the possessor unless the other claimant has a clearly earlier date, reflecting the balance between presumption (yad) and explicit proof (ta'rikh).

Preference of Purchase Claim over Gift and Charity in Disputes
Ruling that purchase claim is preferred over gift/charity claim with possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for conflicting claims of purchase vs. gift/charity and apply the conditions (dating, divisibility) that affect priority.

Core Idea
Purchase claim beats gift/charity claim because purchase is a bilateral contract with immediate ownership transfer.

When two claimants both present evidence of possession from the same transferor, one claiming purchase and the other a gift or charity, the Hanafi school gives priority to the purchase claimant. This preference is rooted in the legal strength of reciprocal contracts (mu'awadah) over gratuitous transfers (tabarru'). The passage also outlines exceptions based on dating and divisibility of property, and notes a scholarly disagreement between al-Bahr and al-Maqdisi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that there are two claimants: one claims purchase from a third party, the other claims a gift or charity with possession from the same party. Both present evidence.
The possession (qabd) is key for the gift/charity claimant to have any standing.
2
Apply the basic ruling
The purchase claimant is preferred because purchase (shira') is a bilateral contract (mu'awadah) and establishes ownership immediately upon contract, whereas gift (hibah) requires possession to transfer ownership.
The ruling applies whether the purchase is valid or void (fasid) – the text generalizes.
3
Incorporate dating
If both claimants have dates and the transferor is the same, the earlier date prevails regardless of the contract type. If the transferor is different, they are equal.
When transferor differs, each represents a separate owner, so no priority due to contract strength.
4
Analyze divisibility of property
For indivisible property (e.g., a house), the ruling applies without controversy. For divisible property, the majority view is that gift of a share (common ownership) is invalid, similar to establishing two proofs of mortgage.
This prevents disputes arising from gifts of undivided shares in property that can be divided.
5
Consider the disagreement
Al-Maqdisi argued that gift should be preferred because it is a prescribed act (mashru'). The author of al-Bahr rejects this, emphasizing the contractual strength of purchase. The mufta bihi position follows al-Bahr.
Charity (sadaqah) is treated like gift in this context; its irrevocability does not provide present priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشراء أحقُّ من هبة وصدقةٍ مع قبض : أي قال أحدهما : اشتريته من زيد، وقال الآخر وهب لي زيد وقبضته، أو تصدق على زيد، وقبضته، فبرهنا ، فمدَّعي الشّراءُ أحق ) .
[١] قوله : والشراء...الخ ؛ أطلق في الشراء فعم الفساد فيكون أولى منها لكونه معاوضة. كذا بحثه صاحب البحر» (۱) ورده المقدسي بأن الأولى تقـديـم الـهـبة لـكـونـهـا مشروعة والمراد بالهبة غير المعوضة إذ لو كانت بعوض كانت بيعاً. كما في ا «المحيط». [٢] قوله : فمدّعي الشراء أحق ؛ أي بأن يقضي له ؛ لأن الشراء أقوى من الهبة ؛ لكونه معاوضة من الجانبين ؛ ولأنَّ الشراء يثبتُ الملك بنفسه، والملك في الهبة يتوقف على القبض، والمسألة فيما إذا لم يؤرّخا ، فلو أرّخا واتحد المملكُ فالاحق هو الأسبق تاريخاً منهما. بخلاف ما إذا اختلف المُمَلّكُ فإنَّهما سواء في صورة التاريخ وعدمه ؛ لأنَّ كلاً منهما خصم عن مُمَلّكه في إثبات ملكه، وهما فيه سواء بخلاف ما إذا اتحد؛ لاحتياجهما إلى إثبات السبب، وفيه تقدم الأقوى.
وكذا الشراء مع الصدقة في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام، ودعوى الهبة و الصدقة مع القبض فيهما مستويان ؛ لاستوائهما في وجه التبرع ولا ترجيح للصدقة باللزوم ؛ لأنَّ اللزوم يظهر في ثاني الحال، وهو عدم التمكن من الرجوع في المستقبل. والترجيح يكون بمعنى قائم في الحال ؛ ولأنّ الرجوع إنما امتنع لحصول المقصود بها، وهو الأجر لا لقوة في السبب، ولو حصل المقصود بالهبة لا يرجع أيضاً، كما إذا كانت لذي الرحم المحرم، أو عوضه الموهوب له عنها ، والصدقة قد لا تكون لازمة بأن كانت لغني كما صرحوا به.
وهذا فيما لا يحتمل القسمة من غير خلاف ؛ لأنَّ الشيوع لا يضره، واختلفوا فيما يحتمل القسمة، والأصح أنه لا يصح؛ لأنه تنفيذ الهبة في الشائع، فصار كإقامته البينتين على الارتهان
Purchase is more deserving than gift and charity with possession: meaning one says 'I bought it from Zayd' and the other says 'Zayd gifted it to me and I took possession' or 'gave it as charity and I took possession'; they present evidence; then the claimant of purchase is more deserving. [Explanation]: His statement 'And purchase...' he spoke generally about purchase, including both valid and void, so it is more deserving because it is a reciprocal contract. Thus the author of al-Bahr argued, but al-Maqdisi objected that gift should be preferred because it is prescribed, and the intended gift is without compensation, otherwise if with compensation it would be a sale as in al-Muhit. His statement 'So the claimant of purchase is more deserving' meaning the judge rules for him; because purchase is stronger than gift, being bilateral consideration, and because purchase establishes ownership by itself, while ownership in gift depends on possession. The case is when they have no dates; if they have dates and the transferor is the same, the one with the earlier date is more deserving. But if the transferor is different, they are equal with or without dates because each is a representative of his transferor in proving ownership, and they are equal in that; unlike when the transferor is the same, because they need to establish the cause, and the stronger cause takes priority. The same applies to purchase vs charity in all the rules mentioned. And claims of gift and charity with possession are equal because they are both gratuitous, and charity is not given priority due to irrevocability because irrevocability appears later (inability to revoke in future), while preference is based on an existing quality in the present; moreover revocation is prohibited because the purpose (reward) is achieved, not because of the strength of the cause; if the purpose is achieved in gift, it also cannot be revoked, as in gift to a close relative or when the donee compensates. Charity may not be irrevocable if given to a rich person as they stated. This is for indivisible property with no disagreement; part-ownership does not harm it. They disagreed for divisible property; the correct view is that it is invalid because it would execute a gift of a share, similar to establishing two proofs of mortgage.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute where one claimant asserts he purchased the item from a third party (Zayd) and the other claims he received it as a gift or charity (with possession) from the same person. The ruling is that the purchase claimant is given priority. The commentary provides two main reasons: (1) purchase is a bilateral contract (mu'awadah) involving consideration from both sides, making it stronger than a gratuitous transfer; (2) purchase transfers ownership immediately upon contract, whereas a gift only transfers ownership upon possession (qabd). The text also discusses the effect of dating: if both have dates and the same grantor, the one with the earlier date wins. If the grantor differs, they are treated equally. For gift vs. charity, they are considered equal in strength because both are charities (tabarru'), and charity's irrevocability is not a present factor. The commentary notes a disagreement between the author of al-Bahr and al-Maqdisi; the author sides with purchase being stronger. Finally, it discusses divisible vs. indivisible property: for indivisible (e.g., a house), part-ownership is allowed, but for divisible, the preferred view is that such a gift in shares is invalid because it is like executing a gift of a share without division.

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): A purchase contract; includes both valid (sahih) and void (fasid) purchases, as the text says 'أطلق في الشراء فعم الفساد'.
هبة (hibah): A gift without compensation; if with compensation ('iwaḍ), it is a sale.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charitable gift; legally similar to gift but with a religious motive; may be revocable if given to a rich person.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): Bilateral transaction with consideration from both sides; purchase is a prime example.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if two claimants present evidence: one claims purchase from Zayd, the other claims a gift from Zayd (with possession), and neither has a date, who is preferred?
A) The gift claimant
B) The purchase claimant
C) They share equally
D) The judge decides by lot

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, purchase claims are stronger than gift claims in disputes because purchase involves bilateral consideration and immediate ownership, but dates can override this priority.

Priority of Buyer over Donee in Claims
Ruling with proof on priority of purchase over gift/charity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a buyer's claim takes priority over a donee's claim when both present evidence, and explain why purchase is stronger than gift/charity.

Core Idea
In conflicting claims, purchase beats gift/charity because it's an exchange that transfers ownership immediately.

When two people each claim ownership of the same property from the same original owner, one by purchase and the other by gift or charity, the Hanafi school gives priority to the buyer. This lesson examines the reasoning: purchase is a mu'awada (exchange) that transfers ownership by contract alone, while gift/charity are tabarru' (gratuitous) and require possession. We will also explore the related dispute on whether a gift of an undivided share is valid when the property is divisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two claimants each present evidence that they obtained the same property from the same original owner. One claims purchase (shira'), the other claims gift (hiba) or charity (sadaqa) with possession (qabd).
2
State the ruling
The buyer is more entitled (ahaqq) to the property than the donee. The judge must decide in favor of the buyer.
3
Analyze the proof
Purchase is stronger because it is an exchange (mu'awada) from both sides and establishes ownership by the contract itself. Gift/charity are gratuitous and ownership depends on possession. Therefore, purchase takes priority.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): the cause (illa) for strength is the immediate transfer of ownership in exchange.
4
Note the qualifications
If the claims are dated and the grantor is the same, the earlier date prevails. If the grantors are different, the claims are equal, regardless of dates.
Charity is treated the same as gift in these rulings. Gift and charity are equal to each other because both are gratuitous.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
When the property is divisible, a gift of an undivided share (shayi') is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, but valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The relied-upon position (al-sahh) is that it is invalid by consensus.
This dispute is mentioned to show the boundaries of the ruling when the property is divisible.
