Book of Claims, Chapter of Two Men's Claims. And purchase is more entitled than gift and charity with possession. 179. And purchase is more entitled than gift and charity with possession: meaning one said 'I bought it from Zayd' and the other said 'Zayd gave it to me as a gift and I took possession' or 'Zayd gave it as charity to me and I took possession', then they presented evidence, so the claimant of purchase is more entitled. [1] His statement 'And purchase... etc.'; he made it absolute in purchase, so it includes what is invalid, thus it is superior to them because it is an exchange. Such is the research of the author of al-Bahr. Al-Maqdisi refuted it: that gift should be preferred because it is prescribed, and by gift is meant non-compensated gift, for if it were compensated it would be sale, as in al-Muhit. [2] His statement 'so the claimant of purchase is more entitled'; meaning that judgment is given for him because purchase is stronger than gift; because it is an exchange from both sides; and because purchase establishes ownership by itself, while ownership in gift depends on possession. The issue is when they have not dated. If they did date and the grantor is the same, then the one with the earlier date is more entitled. Contrary to when the grantor differs, then they are equal whether dated or not; because each is a litigant for his grantor in establishing ownership, and they are equal in that. Unlike when they are the same, because they need to establish the cause, and therein the stronger prevails. Likewise, purchase with charity in all the rulings we mentioned. The claim of gift and charity with possession are equal; because they are equal in being gratuitous, and charity has no preference due to irrevocability; because irrevocability appears later, i.e., inability to revoke in the future. Preference is based on a meaning present immediately; and because revocation is prevented due to attainment of its purpose, which is reward, not due to strength of the cause. If the purpose is attained in gift, it is also not revocable, as when it is for a mahram relative, or the donee gives compensation for it. And charity may not be irrevocable if it is to a wealthy person, as they explicitly stated. This is when it cannot be divided, without dispute; because common ownership does not harm it. They differed when it can be divided, and the most correct is that it is invalid; because it executes the gift in a common share, so it becomes like establishing two evidences on pawn. (1) (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 239). 180. Zubdat al-Nihaya li-Umdat al-Ri'aya. (And purchase and dower are equal) (1). It is said: this is the statement of Abu Hanifa alone. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible, based on the fact that gift of one share to two persons is permissible according to them contrary to him. This is because ownership is obtained by the judge's ruling, and his ruling is like gift of one to two. And it is said: permissible by consensus; because common ownership arises subsequently, since each of them proved possession of the whole, then common ownership occurred after that, and that does not prevent the validity of gift and charity. The most correct is that it is not permissible by consensus. Al-Zayla'i explicitly stated this in Sharh al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Kitab al-Da'wa (Book of Claims) concerning the priority of two conflicting claims of ownership over a specific property. The scenario: two individuals each present evidence (bayyina) that they obtained the same property from the same original owner (Zayd). One claims purchase (shira'), the other claims a gift (hiba) or charity (sadaqa) with possession (qabd). The Hanafi ruling is that the buyer is more entitled (ahaqq) to the property than the donee. The commentary explains the reasoning: purchase is a mu'awada (exchange contract) from both sides, while gift and charity are tabarru' (gratuitous). Purchase establishes ownership immediately upon contract, while ownership in gift depends on possession. Therefore, purchase is stronger. An objection by al-Maqdisi that gift should be preferred because it is 'prescribed' (mashru'a) is dismissed because gift without compensation (ghayr mu'awwada) is intended; otherwise it would be a sale. The text then addresses the situation when the claims are dated: if the grantor is the same, the earlier date prevails; if the grantors differ, the claims are equal because each litigates for their own grantor. Charity is treated identically to purchase in these rulings. Gift and charity with possession are considered equal to each other because both are tabarru', and charity's irrevocability (luzum) does not give it preference in the present litigation because luzum is a future consequence. The final part discusses the effect of division (qisma): when the property is indivisible (la yahtamil al-qisma), the ruling is uncontested; when divisible, there is ikhtilaf. The relied-upon position (al-sahh) is that a gift of an undivided share (shayi') is invalid because it is like establishing two evidences on a pawn. However, Abu Hanifa's opinion is alone; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it based on the validity of giving one item to two persons, and because the judge's ruling can create ownership. The most correct (al-sahh) according to later authorities is that it is invalid by consensus (ijma').

Key Terms
شراء (shira'): Purchase; a contract of exchange (mu'awada) where ownership transfers immediately upon contract, without requiring possession.
هبة (hiba): Gift; a gratuitous contract (tabarru') where ownership depends on possession (qabd) for completion.
صدقة (sadaqa): Charity; like a gift but for reward (thawab); often considered irrevocable, but the text notes it may not be binding if given to a wealthy person.
قبض (qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the property. In hiba and sadaqa, it is a condition for completion of ownership.
معاوضة (mu'awada): Exchange contract; a contract where each party gives and receives compensation, such as sale.
تبرع (tabarru'): Gratuitous contract; a contract without compensation, such as gift or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two claimants present evidence for the same property, one claiming purchase (shira') and the other claiming gift (hiba) with possession, who is more entitled?
A) The claimant of gift, because gift is a prescribed act of worship.
B) The claimant of purchase, because purchase is an exchange contract that establishes ownership immediately.
C) They are equal, because both have evidence.
D) It depends on who has the earlier date.

Key Takeaway
In conflicting claims of ownership, purchase prevails over gift/charity because it is an exchange contract that transfers ownership immediately by contract alone.

Pawn vs Gift: Preference Based on Istihsan
Ruling with proof: pawn preferred over gift by istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers a pawn over a gift when both are accompanied by possession, apply the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish the exception of a gift with compensation.

Core Idea
Pawn with possession is preferred over gift with possession because its contract is stronger.

When two litigants present evidence—one claiming an item is a pawn, the other claiming it is a gift—the Hanafi judge must decide which claim is stronger. The matn states the pawn is more deserving. The commentary reveals that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not qiyas. Understanding why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case provides insight into the Hanafi method of legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states: 'The pawn with possession is more deserving than the gift with possession.' This is the operative ruling for a judge faced with conflicting claims.
The ruling applies when both parties have possession and bring evidence.
2
Examine the Qiyas Counter-Argument
Qiyas would favor the gift because a gift establishes full ownership of the property, making its evidence 'more probative' (akthar ithbātan). The ownership claim is seemingly stronger.
This is a valid analogy, but it is overridden.
3
Understand the Istihsan Justification
Istihsan considers the overall legal effect: a pawn contract involves two obligations (the pawned item and the underlying debt), so it is 'stronger' (aqwā) than a gift, which involves only one (transfer of ownership). The pawned item is also guaranteed (maḍmūn) under pawn, unlike a gift.
Thus the pawn's evidence is considered more comprehensive and trustworthy.
4
Apply the Exception
The passage contrasts a gift with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-ʿiwaḍ), which is essentially a sale. In that case, the gift (sale) is stronger than pawn because a sale establishes ownership both in form and meaning immediately.
This exception confirms the principle: the stronger contract is given preference.
5
Summarize the Principle
When conflicting evidence is presented, the judge must look beyond superficial ownership and assess the underlying contractual strength. Istihsan allows departure from qiyas when the contract's effect is more comprehensive.
This reflects the Hanafi commitment to substantive justice over formal analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرهن مع قبض أحقُّ من هبة معه. (1) قوله : والرهن ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ادعى أحدهما رهناً مع قبض الآخر هبة مع القبض، وأقاما بينة في الرهن أولى، وهذا استحسان والقياس أن تكون الهبة أولى، فإنّ الهبة تثبت ملك العين، والرهن لا يثبت فكانت البينةُ المثبتة لملك العين أكثر إثباتاً ، فكان أولى. ووجه الاستحسان : إنَّ المقبوض بحكم الرهن مضمون بأقل من القيمة ودين المرتهن، وبحكم الهبة غير مضمون ، وعقد الرهان أقوى ؛ لأنه يثبت البدلين المرهون والدين، والهبة لا تثبت إلا بدلاً واحداً، فكان أكثر إثباتاً. بخلاف الهبة بشرط العوض، فإنّه أولى من الرهن ؛ لأنه بيع انتهاء، والبيع أولى من الرهن ، فإنَّ البيع عقد ضمان يثبتُ الملك من حيث الصورة في الحال، ومن حيث المعنى في المال، والرهن لا يثبتُ الملك إلا من حيث المعنى في المال، فكذا الهبةُ بشرطِ العوض أقوى من الرهن.
And the pawn with possession is more deserving than a gift with possession. (1) His statement: And the pawn…etc.; meaning if one claims a pawn with possession and the other a gift with possession, and they bring evidence, the pawn is preferable. This is istihsan, and the qiyas is that the gift should be preferable, because the gift establishes ownership of the thing itself, while the pawn does not, so the evidence establishing ownership of the thing itself is more probative, thus it is more deserving. The basis of istihsan: What is held under pawn is guaranteed with less than the value and the debt of the mortgagee, while under gift it is not guaranteed, and the pawn contract is stronger because it establishes two things: the pawned item and the debt, while the gift establishes only one thing, so it is more probative. Contrary to a gift with a condition of compensation, it is more deserving than pawn because it is a sale in effect, and sale is more deserving than pawn because sale is a guarantee contract that establishes ownership in form immediately and in meaning in property, while pawn does not establish ownership except in meaning in property. Thus a gift with condition of compensation is stronger than pawn.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and its proof regarding conflicting claims of a pawn (rahn) with possession and a gift (hiba) with possession. The matn states that the pawn is more deserving. The commentary explains that this is based on istihsan (juristic preference), whereas the qiyas (analogy) would favor the gift because a gift establishes full ownership of the property, making the evidence for it more direct. The istihsan argument: The property held as pawn is subject to guarantee (the mortgagee holds it as security, and if lost, the mortgagee is liable only up to the debt), while a gift is not guaranteed; thus the pawn contract is stronger because it involves two obligations (the pawned item and the debt), whereas the gift involves only one (the transfer of ownership). Therefore, the pawn is more probative. However, an exception is made: a gift with a condition of compensation (hiba bi-shart al-'iwad) is actually stronger than a pawn because it is essentially a sale, and a sale is stronger than a pawn since it establishes ownership both in form and meaning. So, in summary: ordinary gift (no condition) is weaker than pawn; conditional gift (with compensation) is stronger.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A contract where property is given as security for a debt; the mortgagee holds it until repayment.
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift; a gratuitous transfer of ownership.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from a clear analogy for a stronger legal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which is given preference when there is conflicting evidence of a pawn and a gift, both with possession?
A) The gift, because it establishes ownership.
B) The pawn, because istihsan favors it.
C) Both are equal.
D) It depends on the value of the property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a pawn with possession is preferred over a gift with possession due to istihsan, because the pawn contract involves two obligations, making its evidence stronger.

Preferring the Earlier Dated Ownership Evidence
Ruling on two outsiders claiming ownership with dated evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for deciding between two outsiders who present dated ownership evidence and distinguish between evidence of possession and evidence of ownership.

Core Idea
Earlier date wins when two outsiders both claim ownership with dated evidence.

In Islamic property law, disputes often arise between claimants who are not in possession. The Hanafi school sets a clear rule: if both outsiders present evidence of ownership with a specific date, the one with the earlier date is more deserving. This lesson clarifies the conditions for such evidence and the priority rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants
Note that both claimants are outsiders (kharijan), meaning neither is in possession of the property.
2
Examine the evidence
The evidence must explicitly testify to ownership (milk), not merely possession (yad). If it only mentions possession, it is insufficient.
Refer to the commentary: 'because they testified to possession, not ownership.'
3
Apply the date rule
If both have dated evidence of ownership, the judge prefers the earlier date as it indicates prior ownership.
The principle: 'as-sabiq awla' (the earlier is more deserving).
4
Pronounce judgment
The judge rules in favor of the claimant with the earlier date.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بَرْهَنَ خارجان على ملك مؤرّخ (1) قوله : على ملك مؤرّخ ؛ قيّد بالملك ؛ لأنه لو أقام البينة على أنها في يده منذ سنتين، ولم يشهدوا أنها له قضى به للمدعي ؛ لأنها شهدت باليد لا بالملك. كذا في «البحر». (1) يعني إذا ادعيا ملكاً في آخر وأقاما البينة وأرّخا فصاحب الأسبق أولى، ينظر: «شرح ابن ملك»(ق۱۱۸/ب).
If two outsiders present evidence of ownership with a date. (1) His statement: on ownership dated; it is restricted by ownership, because if he brings evidence that it has been in his hand for two years, and they do not testify that it is his, it is decided for the claimant, because they testified to possession, not ownership. As in al-Bahr. (1) Meaning if both claim ownership of something and bring evidence and date it, then the one with the earlier date is more deserving, see Sharh Ibn Malik.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling about two claimants who are outsiders (not in possession) each presenting evidence of ownership with a specific date. The matn states that if both present dated evidence of ownership, the earlier date is preferred. The commentary clarifies that the word 'ownership' is key: if the evidence only states that the property has been in the claimant's possession for a certain period without explicitly stating ownership, the evidence is insufficient and the claim is decided in favor of the other party or the possessor. The example given: if a person brings evidence that the property has been in his hand for two years (i.e., possession) but the witnesses do not testify that it is his property, the judge rules for the claimant (the one who brought the suit) because the testimony is only about possession, not ownership. This underscores that the evidence must explicitly address ownership, not mere possession.

Key Terms
الملك المؤرّخ (al-milk al-mu'arrakh): Ownership with a specified date; evidence that includes a date for the claim of ownership.
الخارجان (al-kharijan): Two outsiders; claimants who are not in possession of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He prefers the one with the earlier date.
C) He gives the property to the one in possession.
D) He rejects both claims.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute between two outsiders, the judge must prefer the one with earlier dated evidence of ownership, and evidence of possession alone is insufficient.

Precedence in Dated Purchases: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on priority when two claimants have dated purchases from same seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the priority of dated vs. undated deeds in absolute ownership claims, and apply the correct ruling in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A dated deed does not automatically win in Hanafi law; the three imams differ on its value.

When two people claim to have bought the same property from the same seller, the date of purchase often seems decisive. But in Hanafi jurisprudence, the issue is more subtle, especially when one deed is dated and the other is not. This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether a dated deed gives priority over an undated one in a claim of absolute ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Understand the situation: Two claimants each produce a deed of purchase from the same seller. One deed is dated (mu'arrakh), the other is undated (mubham). The seller is not the current possessor. The question: who is entitled to the property?
2
Learn Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that the undated and dated deeds are equal. His reasoning: the undated deed could be earlier, so the date does not remove the possibility that the undated claimant purchased first. Thus, neither is given priority, and the property is divided equally.
This is based on the principle that a date does not add certainty in absolute ownership claims.
3
Learn Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the dated deed is prior. His reasoning: the date makes ownership certain (mutayaqqin), so the dated claimant has proven a specific time of acquisition, which must be respected.
He treats the date as decisive evidence.
4
Learn Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the undated deed is prior. His reasoning: an undated deed proves ownership from the origin (asl), which is stronger than a specific date. He points out that the owner of an undated deed is entitled to offspring and earnings from the property, indicating ownership from the very beginning.
Muhammad considers the undated deed as evidence of ownership from the inception of the property.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice: if both deeds are undated, they are equal and the property is divided. If only one is dated, the ruling depends on the imam: Abu Hanifa says equal, Abu Yusuf says dated takes all, Muhammad says undated takes all.
Later authorities often prefer Abu Hanifa's view, meaning the property is divided equally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو شراء مؤرّخ من واحد أو خارج على ملك مؤرّخ ، وذو يد على ملك أقدم، فالسابق أحق
[1] قوله : أو شراء مؤرّخ ؛ أشار بذكره بعد الملك أنه لا فرق بين دعوى المطلق والذي لسبب. الملك
[2]قوله : مــن واحـد ؛ ولـو قيّده بقوله : غير ذي يد، كما أفاده صاحب «الهداية) بقوله : معناه من غير صاحب اليد ؛ لكان أولى ؛ لأنَّ دعوى الخارجين الشراء من ذي يد قد تقدّم في قوله : فإن برهنا على شراء شيء من ذي يد ... الخ، فلا فائدة في التعميم.
[٣] قوله : فالسابق أحق ؛ أي الأسبق منهما تاريخاً أحق وأولى في الصورتين ، وذكر الصورة الأولى فيما تقدم، وأعاد هاهنا لأجل التاريخ، وجعل الكرخي له هذا متفقاً عليه، وجعل صاحب الأمالي» هذا قول أبي حنيفة الله وقول محمد له أولاً ، وهو قول أبي يوسف ه آخراً. وفي قول محمد الله آخراً، وهو قول أبي يوسف له أولاً هو بينهما ؛ لأنه لا عبرة للتاريخ في دعوى الملك المطلق، ولو أُرّخت أحداهما دون الأخرى فهما سواء عند أبي حنيفة

ولأبي حنيفة : إن المبهم يحتمل أن يكون أقدم، فلا يترجح المؤرخ مع الاحتمال.
Or a dated purchase from one [seller] or a claim against a dated ownership, and the possessor has an earlier ownership, so the earlier is more entitled. [1] His statement: 'Or a dated purchase' – he indicated by mentioning it after ownership that there is no difference between an absolute claim and one with a cause. [2] His statement: 'from one' – had he qualified it by saying 'other than the possessor', as the author of Al-Hidayah indicated by his statement 'meaning from other than the possessor', it would have been better, because the claim of outsiders of purchase from the possessor has already preceded in his statement 'if they prove purchase of something from the possessor...' so there is no benefit in generalization. [3] His statement: 'so the earlier is more entitled' – meaning the one with the earlier date is more entitled and prior in both scenarios. He mentioned the first scenario previously, and repeated it here for the sake of the date. Al-Karkhi considered this agreed upon. The author of Al-Amali considered this the statement of Abu Hanifa, and the statement of Muhammad initially, and it is the statement of Abu Yusuf finally. And in another transmission, it is the statement of Muhammad finally and the statement of Abu Yusuf initially – the difference between them is that there is no consideration of the date in a claim of absolute ownership. If one of them is dated and the other is not, they are equal according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: the dated one is prior. Muhammad said: the undated one is prior, because evidence for absolute ownership indicates ownership from the origin; do you not see that he is entitled to children and earnings thereby? And ownership from the origin is prior to the date. Abu Yusuf's argument: the dated ownership is certain, so it is prior. Abu Hanifa's argument: the undated could be earlier, so the dated does not prevail over the probability.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effective cause of priority in claims of ownership when two claimants present evidence of purchase with different dates. The matn states a general rule: the earlier date gives priority. The commentator clarifies the scope: the rule applies whether the claim is absolute or based on a cause, and the seller is not the current possessor (otherwise the earlier rule about purchase from the possessor applies). Then the dispute is detailed: Al-Karkhi said the rule is agreed upon, but the author of Al-Amali recounted differences. The key issue is: does a date on a deed create priority in an absolute ownership claim? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that an undated deed (mubham) is equal to a dated one because the undated could be earlier, so the dated is not given preference. Imam Abu Yusuf says the dated deed is certain and thus prior. Imam Muhammad says the undated deed is prior because it indicates ownership from the origin, which is stronger than a date. The practical consequence (thamara) is that if both deeds are undated, they are equal; if only one is dated, Abu Hanifa says they are equal (no preference), Abu Yusuf prefers the dated, and Muhammad prefers the undated. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly given here; later authorities may prefer Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
الملك المطلق (al-milk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership claimed without reference to a specific cause or time.
مؤرّخ (mu'arrakh): A deed or claim that is dated.
مبهم (mubham): An undated deed or claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the undated deed is more recent.
B) Because the undated deed proves ownership from the origin.
C) Because the date makes the dated deed suspicious.
D) Because both deeds are equal.

Key Takeaway
In a claim of absolute ownership, the Hanafi imams disagree on whether a dated deed automatically outranks an undated one; the later reliance often follows Abu Hanifa's view that they are equal.

Equal Claims from Different Sellers and Witness Requirements
Ruling on equal claims from different sellers and conditions for purchase testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants prove purchase from different sellers with matching dates, and apply the condition that witnesses must confirm seller ownership at the time of sale.

Core Idea
Equal dates from different sellers mean equal shares; witness testimony must include seller's ownership.

This passage addresses a classic ownership dispute: two outsiders each claim they bought the same item from different sellers on the same day. The Hanafi rule divides the property equally between them because each claimant has proven his seller's absolute ownership. The text then turns to a crucial evidentiary requirement: witnesses in a purchase claim must explicitly state that the seller owned the item at the time of sale. We will unpack the reasoning behind these rulings and their practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between claimants purchasing from the same seller versus different sellers. Note the different outcomes: same seller → date gives priority; different sellers → even date yields equality.
Also covers when only one claimant provides a date.
2
Understand the reasoning for equal shares
Each claimant proves his seller's ownership, which is absolute (no date). Thus each seller would have an equal claim to the item if they themselves disputed it. The claimants step into their sellers' shoes; hence they share equally.
This is a talil (reasoning) used to justify the ruling.
3
Contrast with the same-seller scenario
When both claim from the same seller, the seller's ownership is a single proven fact. The one who dates his purchase shows he was the first buyer; the later buyer cannot prevail against the earlier documented transaction.
4
Analyze the witness condition for purchase testimony
Testimony that 'he bought from X' is insufficient; witnesses must state that the seller owned the item at the time of sale. Reason: a person may sell what he does not own (as agent or trespasser).
Exception: if the item is in the seller's possession, mere testimony of purchase suffices because possession implies ownership.
5
Apply the exception for possession
If the item is in the seller's hand, the testimony is accepted without explicit mention of ownership. If in another's hand, the witnesses must confirm the seller's ownership.
This distinction is from al-Bazzaziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء متفق تاريخهما من آخر، أو وقت أحدهما فقط استويا وإن بَرْهَنا على شراء شيء متفق تاريخهما من آخر) : أي قال أحدهما : اشتريته من زيد، وقال الآخر : اشتريته من عمرو ، وذكرا تاريخاً، أو وقت أحدهما فقط استويا)، فالحاصلُ : أنه إذا وقت أحدهما فقط، وتلقيا من واحد، فصاحب الوقت أحق ، وإن تلقيا من اثنتين فهما سواء. وأما الصورة الثانية ؛ فلأنهما لما ادَّعيا من شخص واحدٍ فقد اتفقا على أنَّ الملك له، فمن أثبت منهما التلقي من جهته في زمان لا يزاحمه فيه أحد كان أولى، ولو لم يؤرخا فهما سواء، وإن أرخت إحداهما دون الأخرى، فالمؤرخ أولى، وإن كان مع أحدهما قبض كان هو أولى، ولو أرّخ الآخرُ ما لم يُثبت أنه أقدم تاريخاً. كذا في «رمز الحقائق للعلامة العيني. [١] قوله : وإن برهنا...الخ ؛ يعني إن برهن أحدهما على الشراء من زيد، والآخر عليه من بكر، واتفق تاريخهما ، فهما على سواء ، حتى يكون المبيع بينهما نصفين ؛ لأنَّ كل واحدٍ منهما أثبت الملك لبائعه، وملك بائعه مطلق بلا تاريخ ، فصار كما إذا حضر البائع فادعى الملك المطلق، فيكون بين الخارجين ؛ لاستواء تاريخهما. وكذا لو وقت أحدهما فقط ؛ لأنَّ توقيت أحدهما لا يدلُّ على تقدم الملك ؛ لجواز أن يكون الآخر أقدم ، بخلاف ما إذا كان البائع واحد ؛ لأنهما لو اتفقا على الملك لا يتلقى إلا من جهته، فإذا أثبت أحدهما تاريخاً يحكم به، حتى يتبين أنه تقدمه لشراء غيره، ثم اعلم أن البينة على الشراء لا تقبل حتى يشهدوا أنه اشتراها من فلان، وهو يملكها. كذا في «البحر») معزيا إلى «خزانة الأكمل». وفي «السراج الوهاج»: لا تقبل الشهادة على الشراء من فلان حتى يشهدوا أنه باعها منه ، وهو يومئذ يملكها ، أو شهدوا أنها لهذا المدعي اشتراها من فلان بكذا، ونقد الثمن وسلّمها إليه ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان قد يبيع ما لا يملك، لجواز أن يكون وكيلاً أو متعدياً ، فلا يستحق المشتري الملك بذلك ، فلا بد من ذكر ملك البائع أو ما يدلُّ عليه. انتهى. وفي «البزازيّة»: إن كان المبيع في يد البائع تقبل من غير ذكر ملك البائع، وإن كان في يد غيره والمدعي يدعيه لنفسه إن ذكر المدعي وشهوده أنَّ البائع يملكها، أو قالوا :
And if they prove purchase of something with matching dates from another, or only one of them specified a time, they are equal. The summary: if only one specified a time and they received from one [seller], the one with the time is more entitled; if they received from two, they are equal. As for the second scenario: since they both claimed from one person, they agree that ownership belongs to him. So whoever among them proves receipt from his side at a time when no one else disputes him is prior. If they did not date, they are equal; if one is dated, the dated one is prior; if one has possession, he is prior; even if the other dates later, unless he proves an earlier date. So in 'Ramz al-Haqa'iq' by al-Ayni. [1] His statement: 'And if they prove...' meaning if one proves purchase from Zaid and the other from Bakr, and their dates match, they are equal, so the item is divided between them in halves. Because each has proved ownership for his seller, and the seller's ownership is absolute without date, so it becomes as if the seller appeared and claimed absolute ownership, so the item is between the two claimants since their dates are equal. Similarly if only one specified a time, because the timing of one does not indicate earlier ownership, as the other could be earlier. Contrary to the case where the seller is one, because they agree on ownership only from his side. So if one proves a date, it is judged by it until it becomes clear that the other preceded him in purchase. Then know that evidence of purchase is not accepted unless the witnesses testify that he bought it from so-and-so while he owned it. So in 'al-Bahr' citing 'Khizanat al-Akmal'. And in 'al-Siraj al-Wahhaj': testimony of purchase from so-and-so is not accepted unless they testify that he sold it to him and at that time he owned it, or they testify that this claimant bought it from so-and-so for such price, paid the price, and it was delivered to him, because a person may sell what he does not own, as he could be an agent or a trespasser, so the buyer does not acquire ownership thereby. So it is necessary to mention the seller's ownership or what indicates it. End. And in 'al-Bazzaziyya': If the item is in the seller's hand, it is accepted without mention of his ownership; if it is in someone else's hand and the claimant claims it for himself, [it is accepted] if the claimant and his witnesses mention that the seller owned it, or they said...
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and elaboration. It addresses the scenario where two outsiders (kharijan) each claim ownership of the same item, each proving purchase from a different seller, with matching dates. The ruling: if both have the same date, they share equally. The reason given is that each claimant has proven the ownership of his respective seller, who holds absolute ownership (without date). Thus the situation is like the two sellers themselves claiming the item; they are equal, so the item is divided. Even if only one claimant specifies a time, they are still equal because the timing does not prove priority over the other claimant whose seller might have owned earlier. This contrasts with the case where both claim from the same seller: there, if one has a date, he is given priority. The passage then shifts to the conditions for accepting testimony in purchase cases. The requirement is that witnesses must explicitly state that the seller owned the item at the time of sale, because a person might sell what he does not own. An exception is if the item is in the seller's possession, then acceptance without such statement is allowed. The passage draws from multiple texts: al-Bahr, Khizanat al-Akmal, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Bazzaziyya. The authority weight is from later commentaries (mutakhkhirin).

Key Terms
البينة على الشراء (al-bayyinah 'ala al-shira'): Testimony or evidence that a person purchased an item from a specific seller.
ملک البائع (milk al-ba'i'): Ownership of the seller; the condition that the seller is the owner of the item at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two claimants each prove purchase from different sellers with the same date, how is the disputed property distributed?
A) The property goes to the seller who is in possession.
B) It is divided equally between the two claimants.
C) The claimant with the earlier time (if any) gets it all.
D) The property is given to the current possessor until further evidence.

Key Takeaway
Equal dates from different sellers mean equal shares; witnesses must confirm seller's ownership unless the seller possesses the item.

Preference of Possessor's Evidence in Non-Recurring Ownership Causes
Ruling on conflicting evidence of non-recurring ownership causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for conflicting evidence on non-recurring ownership causes and apply it to cases such as birth, weaving, and milking, distinguishing from recurring causes.

Core Idea
When evidence conflicts on non-recurring ownership, the possessor's hand tips the scale.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key evidentiary rule in Hanafi fiqh: when both parties present proof of ownership via a non-recurring cause such as birth, the possessor's evidence prevails. This is based on a prophetic hadith and distinguishes between recurring and non-recurring causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core evidentiary conflict
Recognize that the dispute involves a non-recurring cause (e.g., birth of an animal) where both the possessor and the outsider present evidence.
Non-recurring means the cause of ownership can happen only once.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The judge rules in favor of the possessor because his evidence, combined with his physical possession, outweighs the outsider's evidence.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the proof from hadith
The hadith of Jabir ibn 'Abdullah reports that the Prophet gave a camel to the possessor when both claimed birth. This is the primary dalil.
The proof is ahad, but accepted due to practice.
4
Analyze the opposing view of 'Isa ibn Aban
'Isa ibn Aban argued that the evidence cancels out because the same animal cannot be born from two mothers, leaving the property in the possessor's hand without a judgment.
The Hanafi response: testimony is based on apparent causes, not certainty.
5
Apply the qiyas extension to other non-recurring causes
The ruling extends by qiyas to all non-recurring causes (e.g., weaving cloth, milking, cheese-making) but not to recurring causes (e.g., shearing, planting).
Recurring causes follow the default rule: the outsider prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بَرْهَنَ خارج على الملك، وذو اليد على الشراء منه أسلمها إليه، وقال: سلّمها إلي، أو قال : قُبِضَت، وقالوا : قبض، أو قال : ملكي اشتريتها منه وهي لي تقبل، فإن شهدوا على الشراء والنقد ولم يذكروا القبض ولا التسليم ولا ملك البائع ولا ملك المشتري لا تقبل الدعوى ولا الشهادة، ولو شهدوا باليد للبائع دون الملك اختلفوا. انتهى.
[١) قوله : فإن برهن... الخ ؛ يعني لو أقام الخارج البينة على أنه ملك هذا الشيء وأقام ذو اليد على أنه اشتراه من الخارج، فصاحب اليد أولى بأن يقضى له ؛ لأنَّ الخارج وإن كان يثبت أوّليّة الملك فذو اليد يتلقى الملك منه ، ولا تنافي فيه، فصار كما إذا أقر بالملك ثم ادعى الشراء منه.
وكذا لو أقام الخارج ذو اليد بينة على سبب ملك لا يتكرر، كما إذا برهنا كلّ واحد منهما على أنّ هذه الدّابة نتجت وولدت عنده ، أو على أن اللبن له وملكه ، حلب في يده من شاته، أو على أنَّ هذا الجبن له صنعه في ملكه، أو على أنّ هذا اللبدله صنعه في ملكه، أو على أنه قطع هذا الجز من غنمه ، فصاحب اليد أولى، سواء أقام صاحب اليد بينةً على دعواه قبل القضاء بها للخارج أو بعده، أما قبله فظاهر. وأما بعده ؛ فلأنّ ذا اليد لم يعتبر مقضيّاً عليه ؛ لأنَّ بينته في نفس الأمر دافعةً بينةَ الخارج ؛ لأن الكلام في سبب لا يتكرّر ، فإذا ظهرت بينة دافعة تبين أن الحكم لم يكن مستنداً إلى حجّة فلا يكون معتبراً، وإنما كان صاحب اليد أولى ؛ لأنَّ البينة قامت على ما لا تدل عليه اليد، وهو أوليّة الملك بالنتاج ، فاستوت البينتان، وترجحت بينةُ ذي اليد باليد، فيقضى له، وهذا هو الصحيح.
وقال عيسى بن أبان : إنه يتهاتر البينتان، ويترك المتنازع فيه في يد صاحب اليد، لا على طريق القضاء ؛ لأنَّ القاضي تَيَقّنَ بكذب أحد الفريقين ؛ لأنَّ نتاج دابة من دابتين غير متصور، فصار كأنهما لم يقيما بينة ، ولو لم يقيما بينة يقضى لصاحب اليد قضاء ترك.
والجواب : أن الشهادة على النتاج ليست بمعاينة الانفصال من الأم بـل بـرؤية الفصيل يتبع الناقة، وكلُّ واحدٍ من الفريقين اعتمد سبباً ظاهراً لأداء الشهادة، فيجب العمل بهما، ولا يصار إلى التهاتر بمنزلة شهادة الفريقين على الملكين، حيث لا يتهاتر البينتان مع أنّ الشيء الواحد لا يتصور أن يكون مملوكاً لشخصين في زمان واحد، لكلِّ واحد منهما بكماله.
ولكن لما وجد القاضي بشهادة كلِّ واحدٍ من الفريقين محملاً يطلق له أداء الشهادة بأن عاين أحد الفريقين باشر سبب الملك ، وعاين الفريق الآخرُ الخصم الآخرَا يتصرَّفُ فيه تصرف الملك قبل شهادة الفريقين، كذا هاهنا.
واعلم أنَّ القضاء ببينة الخارج هو الأصل، وإنّما عدلنا عنه بخبر النتاج ، وهو ما روى جابر بن عبد الله له : «إنّ رجلاً ادّعى ناقة في يد رجل، وأقام البينة أنها نتجت عندها، وأقام الذي هي في يده أنها ناقته نَتَجَها فقضى بها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للذي هي في يده»)، وهذا حديث مشهور، فصارت مسألة النتاج مخصوصة، كما في «البحر» نقلا عن «المحيط». وألحقوا بالنتاج ما لا يتكرر سببه ؛ لكونه في معناه ؛ لأنه دعوى أولية الملك كالنسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج إلا مرَّةً كالثياب القطنية، وغزل القطن، وحلب اللبن، واتخاذ الجبن، وجز الصوف.
وإن كان سببا يتكرّر لا يكون في معناه، فيقضي به للخارج بمنزلة المطلق، مثل الجز، والبناء، والغرس، وزراعة الحنطة والحبوب، فإن أشكل يرجع إلى أهل الخبرة ، فإن أشكل عليهم قضى به للخارج ؛ لأنَّ القضاء بينته هو الأصل، وإنما عدلنا بخبر النتاج كذا في «المنح»، وغيره. وذكر الطحطاوي نقلاً: هكذا ذكر عن «جامع الفصولين» برهن كل من الخارج وذي اليد على نتاج في ملك بائعه حكم لذي اليد، إذ كل منهما خصم عن بائعه، فكأن بائعيهما حضرا وادعيا حكماً بنتاج، فإنّه يحكم لذي اليد، ولو برهن أنه ولد في ملكه
If an outsider presents evidence of ownership, and the possessor presents evidence of purchase from him... (the passage continues with detailed ruling, dispute with 'Isa ibn Aban, hadith of Jabir, and distinction between recurring and non-recurring causes).
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding conflicting evidence of ownership. It presents the Hanafi position that the possessor (dhu al-yad) is preferred when both parties present evidence on a non-recurring cause of ownership, such as birth (نتاج). The passage begins with the case where the outsider (kharij) presents evidence of his ownership, and the possessor presents evidence that he purchased from the outsider. The possessor wins because his ownership is derived from the outsider's. Then similar cases with non-recurring causes: birth, milk, cheese, felt, etc. The possessor is preferred even if the evidence is presented after a judgment for the outsider, because the possessor's evidence actually repels the outsider's evidence (since the cause cannot be repeated). Then the dispute with 'Isa ibn Aban who argues for ta'atur (cancellation) since one party must be lying, leaving the possession as is without a judgment. The response: the shahada is based on apparent cause, not actual certainty of birth, so both are admissible and the possessor's hand tips the balance. The support is the hadith of Jabir about the camel. Then the passage distinguishes between non-recurring causes (like weaving of cotton cloth, spinning, milking, cheese-making, etc.) which follow the birth ruling, and recurring causes (like shearing, building, planting, cultivating) where the outward claimant prevails unless experts determine otherwise. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the preference for the possessor's evidence in non-recurring cases.

Key Terms
نتاج (nitāj): The act of giving birth or the offspring itself; used as a prototype of a non-recurring cause of ownership.
تهاتر (tahātur): The cancellation or mutual off-setting of two conflicting pieces of evidence, leaving no admissible evidence.
ذو اليد (dhū al-yad): The person who currently has physical possession of the disputed property.
خارج (khārij): The claimant who does not have possession; the outsider who brings a claim of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when both the outsider (kharij) and the possessor (dhu al-yad) present evidence that a camel was born in their respective ownership?
A) The evidence cancels out (tahatur) and the camel is left in the possessor's hand without a judgment.
B) The possessor is preferred and the camel is given to him by judgment.
C) The outsider is preferred because his evidence is about first ownership.
D) The judge refers the matter to experts.

Key Takeaway
In conflicting evidence on non-recurring ownership causes, the possessor's evidence is preferred, based on the hadith of Jabir and extended by qiyas.

Possessor's Priority and the Exception of Original Ownership in Animal Product Claims
Defining animal product terms and establishing the possessor's priority with an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the possessor's evidence is preferred over an external claimant in disputes over animal products and identify the exception based on birth and emancipation.

Core Idea
The possessor's evidence prevails unless the external claimant proves both birth and emancipation, showing original ownership.

This passage from the chapter on claims defines animal products and establishes a fundamental rule: the person in physical possession (dhul-yad) is given priority in evidence. However, an important exception is provided: if the external claimant can prove that the animal was born from his property and later emancipated, his evidence overrides the possessor's. Understanding this balance between possession and origin is crucial for adjudicating ownership disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal products listed
The text lists milk, cheese, felt, and sheared wool, defining each from authoritative lexicons like al-Misbah.
These products are considered akin to the offspring of the animal.
2
Understand the analogy to 'nataj'
Shearing wool is treated analogically as a product of the animal, even though it does not create original ownership.
3
State the general rule
The matn concludes: 'So the possessor is more entitled (ahaqq).' The judge should prefer the evidence of the person in physical possession.
4
Recognize the condition for the rule's application
The commentary explains that the possessor's priority applies only when the external claimant does not also claim both birth (nataj) and emancipation ('itq).
If the external claims both, his evidence proves original ownership on a basis that cannot be contested.
5
Apply the exception
If the external claimant provides evidence of both birth and emancipation, his evidence overrides the possessor's. The judge then rules in favor of the external.
This is because 'bina' al-nataj ma'a al-'itq athbata al-milka 'ala wajh la yastaḥiqqu 'alayhi aṣla.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى/باب دعوى الرجلين وحلب لبن ، واتخاذ جبن ، أو لبد أو جز صوف
[١] قوله : وحلب لبن ؛ حلبت الناقة وغيرها من باب قتل، والحلب بفتحتين يطلق على المصدر أيضاً وعلى اللبن المحلوب، فيقال : لبنٌ حَلَبٌ وحَلِيبُ ومَحلوبٌ، وناقة حلوب وزان رسول : أي ذات لبن ، واللَّبَنُ بفتحتين من الآدمي والحيوان، جمعه ألبان، مثل سبب وأسباب. كذا في «المصباح».
[٢] قوله : جبن ؛ بالضم وبضمتين : ينير. كذا في منتهى الأرب! وقال في «المصباح» (۳) : الجبن المأكول، فيه ثلاث لغات، رواها أبو عبيدة له عن يونس بن حبي سماعاً عن العرب، أجودها : سكون الباء، والثانية: ضمها للإتباع، والثالثة : وهي أقلها التثقيل، ومنهم من يجعل التثقيل من ضرورة الشعر. انتهى.
[٣] قوله : أو لبد ؛ لبد بالكسر : نمد وخوي كيرزين وهير لـشـم و مـوي نشسته برجفيده الباد لـبـود جمع. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، وقال في «المصباح» : اللبد وزان جمل ما يَتَلَبَّدُ من شعر أو صوف، واللبدة أخص منه. انتهى.
[٤] قوله : أو جز صوف ؛ يقال : جززت الصوفُ جزاً من باب قتل : قطعته ، والصوف للضأن، والصوفة أخص منه، وكبش أصوف وصائف : كثير الصوف. كذا في «المصباح».
فإن قيل : كيف يكون الجز في معنى النتاج ، وهو ليس بسبب لأولية الملك ، فإنّ الصوف على ظهر الشاة كان مملوكاً له قبل الجز.
فذو اليد أحق
[١] قوله : فذو اليد أحق ؛ ومحل تقديم بينة ذي اليد في النتاج إذا لم يدع الخارج نتاجاً وعتقاً، وإلا كان الخارج أولى؛ لأنّ بينةً النتاج مع العنق أكثر إثباتاً ؛ لأنها أثبتت أولية الملكِ على وجه لا يستحق عليه أصلا، وبيّنة ذي اليد أثبتت الملك على وجه يتصوّر استحقاق ذلك عليه.
بخلاف ما إذا ادعى الخارج العتق مع مطلق الملك، وذو اليد ادّعى النتاج ، فبينةُ ذي اليد أولى ذكره العلامة الطحطاوي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار).
The Book of Claims/Chapter on the claim of two men. And milking of milk, and making cheese, or felt, or shearing wool. [Footnote 1: His statement: 'and milking of milk'...] [Footnote 2: His statement: 'cheese'...] [Footnote 3: His statement: 'or felt'...] [Footnote 4: His statement: 'or shearing wool'...] If it is said: How can shearing be in the meaning of birth, while it is not a cause for original ownership, for the wool on the sheep's back was owned by him before shearing? So the possessor is more entitled. [Footnote 1 to the matn: His statement: 'So the possessor is more entitled'; and the place of preferring the evidence of the possessor in birth is when the external claimant does not claim both birth and emancipation; otherwise, the external is more entitled...]
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with lexical definitions. It opens the chapter on claims between two men (kitab al-da'wa). The matn lists animal products: milk, cheese, felt (from wool/hair), and sheared wool. The sharh defines each term from authoritative lexicons like al-Misbah. Then a rhetorical question arises: why is shearing treated like birth (nataj) when it does not create original ownership? The response implies that these products are considered as offspring in terms of ownership origin. The main ruling is: 'The possessor is more entitled (ahaqq).' The commentary clarifies that this preference applies only when the external claimant does not also claim both birth and emancipation ('itq). If the external claims both, his evidence proves original ownership on a basis where no claim is possible (i.e., the animal was his slave's offspring), so he prevails. If the external claims only emancipation without birth, the possessor still prevails because his evidence of birth is stronger in establishing direct ownership. The commentator cites al-Tahtawi on this exception.

Key Terms
النتاج (al-nataj): The event of birth or the offspring resulting from it; used analogically for animal products like milk, cheese, wool.
ذو اليد (dhul-yad): The person in physical possession of the disputed item.
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation from slavery; here it refers to claiming that the animal was born to a slave woman who was later emancipated.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses or other legal proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the possessor's evidence prevail over an external claimant's evidence according to the text?
A) Always
B) When the external claimant does not also claim both birth and emancipation
C) When the external claimant claims the item is a product of his own property
D) Only when the external claimant has no evidence

Key Takeaway
The possessor's evidence is preferred in claims over animal products unless the external claimant proves both birth and emancipation, establishing original ownership.
